THE RED PAGE
My Mirandelay
An occasional luxury of mine in rare
moments of leisure is to weave fancies of all
kinds around the theme “Ferdinand and
Miranda.” While at this lately I was think-
ing of the platonic and mystic idea of the
divided parts of the one soul seeking each
other through the world and the ages until
success and consequent merging, away from
earth, into one angel. I was also thinking
of the Gnostic idea of the descent of the soul
(or of Sophia, according to one version) into
matter and its subsequent re-ascension, and
the thought struck me that it should be pos-
sible to frame a poetic form that would sym-
bolise the said ideas. My first notion was
to use the “ dose,” a beautiful form almost
unknown in English, but 'which with its
verses each ending in consecutive lines of
the first verse seemed to promise both tho
music and the recurrence suitable to a
reverie-poem. Then I suddenly thought
that McKay’s Reversolet was the very thing
to express in form the descent and ascent of
the soul. Hey, presto 1 combine the Rever-
solet and the principle of the Glose, and the
thing was :
[ln all save the fifth stanza this mirandelay
justifies itself as poetry, and the “kiss that weds
the welkin and the clay” must be added to the
great kisses in literature. There are “ potenti-
alities ” in this form, and while the souls of Ferd.
and Randa are waltzing and reversing in space,
some other contributors may be able to make a few
bodies mirandelet on earth. £1 Is. offered for the
best mirandelay sent in before November 30.]
A MIRANDELAY.
Soul I would with mine one angel form !
Mind I roam with on the stellar way !
Heart I throb to when the weary moan !
Eyes’ unfathomed light that light mine own !
Kiss that weds the welkin and the clay !
Sylph of Paradise, yet woman warm !
Mind I roam with on the stellar way.
Bringing men the symbols that unbar
Their old doors of glamor to the light,
Dropping unguents that unfilm their sight,
Or from dramas of some flaming star.
Tinging petty waking life’s pale play !
Heart I throb to when the weary moan.
Thirsty, with the idle sipping wine—
Darkened, while the learned split theirstraws —
Harried, and the wolves enacting laws—
At them, idlers, sages, wolves, with mine,
Throbbing human heart, one thunderstorm !
Eyes’ unfathomed light that light mine own
With the clean desire of shriven stars,
For the merging kiss of God—anon,
With the one light left since Eden’s shone,
Morn-warm messenger, whose rosy cars
Bring our children from Ilis very Throne !
Kiss that weds the welkin and the clay !
Bride delectable, yet nun demure !
Fountain of the soul with sweet flesh rimmed !
Chalice to the full with rich sex brimmed !
Dream, prayer, petalled song abide in your
Smile serene -that smooths the creased day;
Sylph of Paradise, yet woman warm !
Kiss that weds the welkin and the clay !
Eyes’ unfathorned light that light mine own !
Heart I throb to when the weary moan !
Mind I roam with on the stellar way !
Soul I would with mine one angel form !
BERNARD O'DOWD.
Pett Ridge
Not long ago W. D. Howells waved a
greeting to W. Pett Ridge from the pages of
an American review, and said, gracefully
and at length, “ thanks for much good read-
ing.” It was right that the chieftain of a
clan should commend a sturdy lieutenant
fighting on the same side. For many years
Howells has championed the cause of realism
in fiction and placed principle before passion.
In his own work, the greatest defect is want
of humor. Pett Ridge has plenty of it. He
has kept to a narrow strip of life—the poor
and lower middle classes of London ; he does
not go deep, but he never flounders in the
shallows of sentimentality. Things happen,
in his novels much as they happen in life,
save that the events are selected for your
entertainment. They are not boisterous or
thrilling enough to appeal to the multitude,
so his books are never “ best sellers.”
Pett Ridge began as a journalist in 1891,
and he published a collection of short stories
in 1894. His first novel, A Clever Wife,
appeared in the following year. Minor Dia-
logues, printed about the same time, met
with general praise, and next to Anstey’s Voces
Populi they are the best of their kind in
English. In his later work— Mord Ernly,
'Erb, Name of Garland, etc. —the dialogue is
neat and pithy, and in the latest novel, Nine
to Six- Thirty (Sydney Book Club), it is one
of the features that appeal to a reader
vcith an eye for workmanship.
Nine to Six-Thirty, as the title suggests,
is a story of office life. A girl who had been
treated as Cinderella by her elder sisters
rakes a job suddenly vacated by one of them
in an up-to-date tourist agency. She de-
velops rapidly, discovers a capacity for
management and sharp practice, and, in
the determination to get on in the world
she pushes anyone down who stands in her
way. Eventually she finds that it is not
gqod for woman to be alone. While the
hardening process was going on she got some
inexpensive amusement by watching the game
of life—the frequent dramatic episodes, short
stories in the street, low-comedy everywhere.
As the lines at a chapter-head put it:
You look on from the special members’ stand,
Or maybe only peepin’ through the fence.
If lucky, vou’ll be playin’ in the team ;
If not, you sell the cards to make some pence;
But you must be somewhere in it, and in it every
minute —
Life is cricket, and the game it is immense.
That is Pett Ridge’s attitude, and he makes
the reader feel its advantages.
Photo, by Appleby.
Florence Rodway
The photograph does not do justice to
Miss Rodway ; no photograph could. You
must imagine a tall fair-haired figure
upon whose face the Tasmanian bloom
defies the menace of Sydney weather.
The early imaginings of this quiet soft-
voiced young lady took shape in large
pictures of massive allegorical figures draped
in the manner of Leighton and shrouded in
profound gloom. The vision was somewhat
ahead of the faculty ; but there is a sense of
power about these pictures which is still
more evident in the few portraits we have seen
in oil and black-and-white, The latter have
the quality Which ■is usually called virile ;
the work seems to have been done with a
sure, strong hand, and there is no trace of
that prettiness which is the distinctly fem-
inine note.
“Sleep,” exhibited at last year’s Society
of Artists’show, and the two pastels pur-
chased by the the National Gallery -are
admirable; the subjects lending some"
thing to the charm of the pictures.
The fluffy softness of the medium makes it
suitable for portraits of children, and Miss
Rodway has recently drawn little Bertha
Lawson, Alfred Wunderlich’s daughter, and
some others. There are two “heads” in
black and white at the Gallery, and a few
oil paintings in private houses. So far Miss
Rodway’s best work is in pastel; but if a de-
mand ever arises for large decorative panels
and frescoes in public buildings, she would be
able to supply work which probably no one
else in Australia could do so well.
This is her story of a career which began
only a little while ago :
“ When I was very small I used to amuse
myself with a pencil. I would take my
father’s best pencils and note paper and draw
steam engines and boats and houses, like
most small children. I hardly ever drew
from a copy, though—preferring my own idea
of the thing. At school I had a drawing
lesson twice a week. I thin ft 1 drew every
ornament in that house, -away from the
drawing class, when I should have been
doing my lessons, I drew little girls. They
always had long hair and long legs, and were
very much admired by the other small girls
at school,
“ The first lessons I had in painting were
from Sid Long.. ~ It was my first trip away
from Tasmania. I came to Sydney, and, being
impatient to paint* began the study of art
with landscape painting.
“I had always the idea that I was to be an
artist some day, and was very much encour-
aged by my father ; but did not work seri-
ously until I went to the Technical School
in Hobart, where I studied for two or three
years under Benjamin Sheppard. He had
been a student at the Royal Academy
Schools, London, and I became possessed by
the idea of studying there, too.
“I gave up painting, and went back to
black-and-white. Sent my work to London,
and was accepted as a probationer at the
schools.
“I worked for a while at Cope and Nicholls’s
class at Kensington, while waiting for the
examination for entrance to the Academy
schools. Later on I passed my exam., and
was admitted as a student for three years.
There were more exams., and I worked up
from the ‘Lower School’ to the ‘Half-
Upper ’ and to the «Upper School.’ We
studied from 9.30 a.m., with some little time
off in the afternoon, until 7 p.m.
“ Most of the time that I was in England I
lived about 12 miles out of London, which
meant many an anxious time in trying to
catch my trains. If I missed my morning
train, I had to dodge the R.A. curator, for
we were not allowed to be late. If I missed
the evening one, I had an hour and a half to
wait for the next.
“Sargent, Gregory, Bacon, Dicksee, Solo-
mon and Clausen were some who took it in
turn to visit us at the schools. At the end
of three years, through passing another
exam., I got my ‘second term’—meaning
another two years. But I only worked for
about five months of pay ‘second term,’ and
came back to Tasmania. I remained there
for a year, and finding no prospect of making
my way, I came to Sydney. There was
something of a struggle for two years, but
now lam doing very well. I have taken up
portrait painting chiefly, and am very inter-
ested in decorative work—for which I think
there will be more demand in the future.”
Good Average Fiction
Reading a Harold McGrath novel is like
watching a game of billiards. The story is
written well enough to provide interest for
half an hour or so ; it is not long enough for
the click, click to become monotonous, and it
is perfectly harmless. In the latest, A
Splendid Hazard (George Robertson and
Co.), one of Napoleon’s immense brood of
fictional descendants prepares to pot the red
and goes in off the white instead. He missed
the glory but scored the love, and every-
one is satisfied. His name is Breitmann, and,
naturally, he reads Heine. (You remember
—“ Haus Breitmann” translated Heine.) A
gentle ironist, who chases butterflies and the
enemies of France, is appropriately named
Anatole. There is also an admiral, whose
one hobby is pirates—he has the finest
library on piracy in the world. Christy’s
illustrations of costumes “as now worn”
will be much admired by ladies.
* * * *
In The Wild Olive, “the author of The
Inner Shrine” (Angus and Robertson)
attempts to do again what she did before.
The sacrificing heroine on this occasion is
not so stagey as her predecessor, and the
opening chapters, describing Ford’s flight
and life in hiding, are well written. When
events thicken, and the way is made ready
for the great renunciation act by the heroine,
the style deteriorates. There'is no illusion
of reality ; you do not feel that you are
witnessing a drama; you know that the
figures are wooden, and the best you can say
is that the mechanism is skilfully worked.
# * * *
Sophie Cole’s Blue-Grey Magic (Dymock’a)
is something fresh in sentimental fiction,
although the doctor reminds us of old-time
heroes depicted by women. He is large in
size, soft of heart and utterly unsentimental,
As a set-off, there is an ultra-modern girl,
through whose blue-grey letters the mischief
is done which makes a very agreeable story.
* * * #
Mary Gaunt’s latest— The Mummy Moves
(Dymock’s) is a fairly good mystery story with
a tiresome detective whose speech is full of
Latin tags and their English equi valenbs. It
begins in a manner now familiar—two men
outside Marston Mansions, London, discuss
the possibility of adventure in such a prosaip
place.
“The flats are all alike,” said one, “ the people
are all alike, they get up in the morning, they
eat, they brink, and they go to bed at night, and
they are just a commonplace herd going on in the
old humdrum way.”
Immediately afterwards they are called to
look at a murdered man m a room upstairs,
where there is also a mummy with blood on
its hands.
Intellectual Mansions , S.W., by Phillip
Gibbs, starts in somewhat the same way.
The party on the flat upstairs, however, is a
very live young woman who writes love
stories at 10s. a thousand words. A number
of writers and artists hold intellectual meet-
ings in the Mansions; but the principal
interest of the novel is political, and the
Suffragettes are strongly represented. This
is a book worth reading, though it is not
quite so good as the same author’s Street of
Adventure, noticed here last December.
The London Daily Telegraph of 25th
August last announced that its new serial
would be “The Children of the Cloven
Hoof,” an Australian story by Albert
Dorrington.
Baroness Hermione von Preuschen is a
painter of note in Germany and a writer of
verse (“ Poems that were lived ” is the title
of one volume). She went to America
recently and told the N. Y. World :
I think the most truly poetic thing you have in
America is the stockyards in Chicago. I was
enraptured with them, and everyone laughed at
me. But they are great — magnificent— the feed-
ing-place of a nation, of a world. They were like
a description of Dante’s.
Frank Brangwyn and His W ork, by W.
Shaw Sparrow, will be published shortly by
Kegan, Paul and Co. It is to contain 20
color-plates and 10 other full-page illustra-
tions, crown quarto size, and will be sold at
10s. 6d. net. This should be a book worth
having. “The Scoffers,” in the Sydney
Gallery, gives the impression that Brangwyn
works in color in the generous, forthright
fashion of Rodin in marble. He has had a
varied career over most of the world, and
the story of his life ought to make good
reading.
The University of Cambridge has taken over
from The Times the copyright of The Encyclopedia,
Britannica, and will publish a new edition at the
beginning of next year. Much of the_ material
has been re-written, and everything is to be
revised down to the middle of this year, so that
the Eleventh Edition will be virtually a new
work. It will be printed on India paper, and
occupy one-third of the shelf-space taken up by
the present edition. The 28 volumes will be
issued at the same time.
R.A.S.: The lines you want are those of a song
called “Good-bye,” by G. Wbyte-Melville,
printed in his Songs and Verses (Ward, Look and
Co.). Here is the first stanza:
“Falling leaf and fading tree,
Lines of white in a sullen sea,
Shadows rising on you and me
The swallows are making them ready to fly,
Wheeling out on a windy sky.
Good-bye, Summer! Good-bye, good-bye ! ”
THEY ALWAYS FELL.
They went forth to battle but they always fell:
Their eyes were fixed above the sullen shields ;
They fought the battle nobly but not well,
And sank heart-wounded by a subtle spell.
They knew not fear that to the foeman yields,
They were not weak as one who vainly wields
A futile weapon, yet the sad scrolls tell
How on the hard-fought field they always fell.
It was a secret music that they heard,
A sad sweet plea for pity and for peace ;
And that which pierced the heart was but a word.
Though the white breast was red-lipped where the
sword
Did press a cruel kiss, to put surcease
On its fierce longing, but did drink increase.
Ah, they with some strange troubling doubt were
stirred,
And died for hearing what no foeman heard.
They went forth to battle but they always fell:
Their might was not the might of lifted spears;
Over the battle-clamor came a spell
Of troubling music, and they fought not well.
Their wreaths are willows and their tribute,
tears;
Their names are old sad stories in men’s ears:
Yet they will scatter the red hordes of Hell,
Who went forth to battle and always fell.
SEUMAS O'SHEEL.
BLACK SHEEP.
From their folded mates they wander far,
Their ways seem harsh and wild ;
They follow the beck of a baneful star,
Their paths are dream-beguiled.
Yet haply they sought but a wider range,
Some loftier mountain-slope,
And little recked of the country strange
Beyond the gates of hope.
And haply a bell with a luring call
Summoned their feet to tread
Midst the cruel rocks, where the deep pitfall
And the lurking snare are spread.
Maybe, in spite of their tameless days
Of outcast liberty.
They’re sick at heart for the homely ways
Where their gathered brothers be.
And oft at night, when the plains fall dark
And the hills loom large and dim,
For the Shepherd’s voice they mutely hark,
And their souls go out to him.
Meanwhile, “Black sheep! Black sheep!” we
cry,
Safe in the inner fold ;
And maybe they hear, and wonder why,
And marvel, out in the cold.
RICHARD BURTON.
We have sonnets enough, and Songs enough,
And ballads enough, God knows !
But what we need is that .cosmic stuff
Whence primitive feeling glows.
Grown, organised to the needs of rhyme
Through the old instinctive laws,
With a meaning broad as the boughs of time,
And deep as the roots of cause.
It is passion and power that we need to day,
We have grace ;i.nd taste full store ;
We need a man who will say his say --
With a strength unguessed before—
Whose lines shall glow like molten steel
From being forged in his soul,
Till the very anvil shall burn to feel
The breath of the quenchless coal!
Your dainty wordsters may cry “Uncouth ! ”
As they shrink from his bellows’ glow ;
But the fire he fans is immortal youth,
And how should the bloodless know ?
Frederic Lawrence Knowles.
(These three from America.)

beautiful and moderate examples of the,
latest creations.

df

with

ifYOU WISH TO BE CURED, READ THIS LETTER.
The medicine that saves and lengthens human life as
this one does; that brings back the health so long
despaired of ; that proves by restored vigor and mental
energy that professional verdicts have not been fulfilled,
and that out of years of pain and suffering men and
women recover and enjoy life, surely this is the medicine
for the people in every rank and walk of existence.
People who are ill, sickly, weak-nerved, etc., should read
this letter to a great medicine. The writer can be seen
at Rutherglen any day, after being in four hospitals and
having no less than 17 medical examinations.
On 19th May, 1910, Mr. Downes again writes to Clements Tonic Ltd.:
“ I have sent my photo., as you desire. I wish I could do
more to advertise Clements Tonic to sufferers of ill-health, for
scores of men and women came to me the last three months
from far and near to see me, and wanted to know if Clements
Tonic cured me. I am thankful 1 can assure them all, without
fear or doubt, it has. I can say that also to the hundreds of
people who know the truth, when 1 was ill in Clunes, Ballarat,
Wangaratta and Corowa, and to those who had no hope for me.
But, thanks to this medicine, I am here before them all this day a
cured man. 1 wish I could show you in person the look of surprise
many persons have (especially those connected with the hospitals)
who meet and see me alive. / Signed ) PATRICK DOWNES.”
Great Southern Consuls Mine, Rutherglen, Vic.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When the Shells Begin to Fall.
Twenty thousand people witnessed the football match
last Saturday.— Daily paper.
A roar goes up from the football ground—all’s
well with the world to-day ;
Hear the barrackers howl and dance and bound
whilst the mud-splashed persons play !
But it seems to me that the time draws near when
a Voice at Australia’s door
Will announce that Trouble is hastening here with
the fresh-laid eggs of War !
Though I hear the barrackers shout and stamp in
their noisy wrath jejune,
There is never the tramp of an arm&d camp on a
Saturday afternoon !
And it seems to me that it’s overdone, this cult of
the useless ball.
Shall we gasp and run for our (missing) gun When
the Shells Begin to Fall ?
I am tired and sick of the howling mob, and I
reckon that James and Bill
Would be better prepared for Australia’s job if
they savvied the range and drill!
I am tired of the roar of a thousand throats—of
the “ clurk ” with his frantic curse ;
And it seems to me where the goal-flag floats they
are pushing Australia’s hearse !
I have listened in vain for the rhythmic beat of
an army drilled and clean ;
There is never a street where a man shall meet
with Australia’s War-Machine !
So it seems to me that the time arrives for this
game to dwindle small ;
Shall we save our lives (there are kids and wives !)
When the,Shells Begin to Fall !
It is time that our eyes were opened wide to the
trend of the frowning years ;
And I do not think that Australia’s pride is found
where the ’bailer jeers !
I have waited long—are you waiting, too?—whilst
Governments chaffer and change ;
They are pushing amendments slowly through—
but there’s never a rifle range !
There is never a call for the Nation’s best, for the
men grown from their teens ;
When the barrackers rest in their graves unblessed,
shall we count with the Might-Have-Beens ?
So it seems to me that’s it’s time we broke from
this niddering football thrall;
Shall our rifles smoke (they're a mournful joke 1)
When the Shells Begin to Fall ?
We are drilling a crowd of feckless boys, and the
school-kid humps his gun ;
We are filling the air with useless noise, and ’tis
thus that the good years run !
We are cursing the umpire’s sinful whims, “ Free
kick ! You’re a blanker ! Gar-r-r /”
So they shriek and howl till the reason swims,
and I ask where our Fighters are ;
Shall we hold this Land when the war-storm
breaks and the bolts of anger blow ?
So I ask once more—l have asked before, but
nobody seems to know !
And it seems to me that the hour has struck —do
ye hear your country’s call ?
Shall we trust to luck (hear them cheer the ruck !)
When the Shells Begin to Fall ?
VICTORIA.
GRANT HERVEY.
The Soul of Babette.
“With all my heart,” she cried, “ with all
My woman’s soul I love you, dear! ”
Her voice was true and musical,
And, oh, her eyes were fond and clear.
“ Dear lover, husband mine !” : he sighed,
“ You are my world, my joy, my pain !”
I gazed on her with tender pride.
And kissed her ready mouth again.
But straight she turned with laughter cold
And smeared our dearest shrine with dirt;
She bartered her red mouth for gold,
And pawned her honor for a skirt.
Yet, ah, her voice was musical,
The eyes I loved were soft and clear.
“ With all my heart,” she cried, “with all
My woman’s soul I love you, dear ! ”
MAORILAND.
FRANK MORTON.
The Poet Confesses.
I wrote some jolly good lines last night,
Blasted them out of my weary brain ;
Thrilling with passion they were and bright;
Gave them the title “Grey Eyes of Pain ” ;
Founded the poem on Mary Jane ;
Knew her before I began to write ;
Kissed her just once in a gloomy lane;
At least I tried to —I didn’t quite.
I didn’t know she would scratch and bite ;
Else I would never have missed my train;
Just to be pummelled with all her might,
Until I slipped in a filthy drain ;
I got no kisses from Mary Jane !
Oh ! but her eyes had a wild-beast light,
As she informed me her laboring swain
Revelled in punching “a dirty skite.”
Though she had managed my love to blight,
I tried to soothe her—also explain ;
Wasn’t too anxious o’er her to fight;
Thought it quite likely that I’d be slain.
I couldn’t pacify Mary Jane !
“ Bad men,” she said, she could “ tell at sight” ;
Never set eyes on the girl again;
Wild as the Devil she was, and white.
Editor, say are my sins inane ?
Are all my rapturous statements trite ?
I did my best to kiss Mary Jane,
But she repulsed me and took to flight.
N.S.W.

How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to Tiik Bulletin would oblige the paper
(and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some care as to
methods of address.
Business letters (‘with regard to subscriptions, advertise-
ments, etc.) should in no case be addressed to the Editor,
but to the Manager. To send them to the Editor is to
invite inattention.
It is not a pood thing to address editorial letters to
anyone by name. “ The Editor, Bulletin,” is the proper
superscription for "copy." Delay, and even loss, is
sometimes caused by addressing to persons instead of
officials. The person may not be there; the official
always is.

THURSDAY, OCTOBER 2J, 1910.
THE DOWNFALL
Like the oil kingdom of Poland, the Wade
Government of N.S.W. committed suicide. There
is nothing to be gained by the use of large,
haiiy superlatives, which are, at best, only the
loud and disarranged sounds which give away
the unfurnished mind ; therefore it isn t worth
while repeating the statement of certain Labor
men to the effect that the Wade Ministry has
been the worst with which the State was ever
afflicted. It may have been the worst, or it may
not; certainly, if it wasn’t the worst, the other one
must have been something awful. The combina-
tion which met the shook of its life last
Friday was essentially one of small aims,
small ideas and low-down political in-
stincts. It lived in the cellar or basement
of things, and never looked upward. It was so
essentially mean that it hadn’t a glimmer of an
idea of its own meanness; it was so little that it
had to stand on a threepenny bit to see over two-
pence, yet it firmly believed itself a giant. Its
lamentable mental attitude was especially dis-
played during the last few months of its career.
The great downfall of Toryism at the Federal
election of last April evidently impressed it with
the idea that it must hustle for some more sup-
port and buy a lot more popularity. There are
two ways of meeting such an emergency. One is
to purchase the support of the community whole-
sale by doing big conspicuous things, or even one
big conspicuous thing, for the good of tlie country in
general. A leader who plunged on the immediate
establishment of a Greater Sydney, or the im-
mediate opening of the Closed Ports, or came to
the front with a definite proposal for the abolition
of loans and the repayment of the public debt, or
who grabbed with both hands at the skirts of
Chance in any large, imposing fashion, would
either make a great success or a great failure.
Most probably, unless the rest of his policy waa
very bad, he would make a great success. The
other device is to buy, or attempt to buy. the
support of individual sections with individual
bribes, in the hope that the country will over-
look the fact that one fellow lias to pay
the other fellow’s bribe, and the bigger the
bribe one fellow gets the greater the other
fellow’s ruin. This Wade party adopted this
petty sectional method—quite naturally. It
bade for the support of 20,000 railway and tram-
way men by a promise of such absurdly lavish
pensions—absurdly lavish in proportion to the
price to be paid for them—that the whole busi-
ness became a shrieking jest. It tried to mollify
land-sellers and land-buyers with an outrageously
unsound scheme whereby the State was to inter-
fere between them, and advance, in a time of
dangerously high values, 95 per cent, of the
purchase money. It sought to pacify certain
State leaseholders with the offer of the freehold
at a mere fraction of the current value, which was
practically giving away State property. Not
knowing "how to create wealth by sheer good
management, it was full of flurried schemes for
politically robbing Peter (who might not notice
the robbery till after election day) in order to
remunerate Paul. And the result was that Petek
was angry at what he was going to lose, while
Paul wasashamed of what he expected to gain.
* * * *
The fact that the people appreciate large ideas
and straight-going never apparently loomed on
the “Liberal” horizon. The party and the
fatuous papers which blasted it with their support
stuck to the old stock-in-trade—the anti-Victorian
grudge, theanti-Federal curse, the anti-Australian
snarl, the policy of demanding everything for
Sydney and denouncing everything that wasn’t
Sydney. The N.S.W. community never gave
them any reason to believe that it was craving
for a shabby little Federal capital at Wade’s
own door—something acquired by dirty means,
and snatched by dirty hands, and handed to the
Cornstalk people on a dirty dish like a soiled head
of John the Baptist. It gave no evidence of
a frantic demand for possession of the naval
station, or any of the other things on the strength
of which the Wade party posed as the special
saviours of the State. The people took a larger
and more Australian view than their alleged
leaders, and with them there is no reason to be-
lieve that these clamors cut any ice. But the
alleged leaders, like the Bourbons, learned no-
thing and got no further forward. They had seen
George Reid go.down heavily in 1906 with his
Anti-Sosh and “confiscation ” cries, and they bad
seen the still worse failure of the old Reid party
last April; yet they talked the old confiscation
foolishness in theold, old way ; also the Walls of
Derry foolishness,, and the alleged Labor-and-rum
alliance foolishness. They might have survived
the dying rockchopper, and Peter Bowling’s
leg-irons, and the disgraceful McKte case, and
even the murky-looking circumstances connected
with the Bowling trial; but their lack of big
ideas, and their absurd devices for buying stray
scraps of popularity on the cat’s-meat principle
with the public assets, and their anti-Australian-
ism, and their large vituperations, and their insig-
nificant meannesses, and their transparent trading
on what they imagined to be the public ignorance
and credulity were fatal. It was typical of them to
send a commission round the country to consider
the opening of the Closed Ports while intimating as
clearly as they dared in the city that the move-
ment was a mere pretence, and that the old con-
gestion policy was to be rather strengthened than
otherwise. A superbly adroit politician may run
two contradictory policies at one and the same
time, and run them within a few miles of each
other ; but a clumsy politician with the light touch
of an elephant and the noiseless footfall of a
traction engine should leave such things alone.
* * * *
It is difficulty to imagine how a campaign could
have been much worse managed than that of
the “ great Liberal party.” The Ministry’s loud-
mouthed adulation of itself—of its honesty, its
fairness, its mental brilliance, its general incom-
parableness—grew wearisome, and then sicken-
ing. Certain of its maddened supporters, mostly
females, did it no good by keeping before the
public the fact that Wade was the only N.S.W.
Premier who apparently came within an ace of
using his authority to appoint himself to a posi-
tion on the bench to which his legal experience
and standing gave him no claim whatever, and they
poured the butter of adulation all over hitn because,
after much delay, he announced that he wouldn’t
do the deed. People grew tired of hearing of
Wade’s early football exploits, and of his
alleged heroism in a long-ago coal-mine accident.
They knew him as a man who was capable of
wasting the public money in two years of costly
litigation dragged through the highest courts of
the Empire, that he might get his own railway
refreshment-room keeper fined ss. for selling a
cigarette, and in that capacity they regarded him
as something of a joke. They hated his sordid
litigation with old public servants over scraps of
pension money, especially as it almost invariably
proved that the Government was in the wrong and
the aged and harried pensioner in the right;
and when another of these wretched cases hap-
pened along in the middle of the campaign
it was unhealthy. The voters found Wade, on
closer inspection, a very wooden and uninspiring
affair, and a person whose speeches were one-half
wild abuse and one-half tame platitude that
would feed out of the hand. When the Tory
papers tried to lift him to an elevation on which
even a Gladstone or a Diaz might have felt
dizzy and cold about the feet, and described
every two-men-and-a-dog gathering in the
Government interest as a great national up-
heaval, and prophesied almost certain success
for the Tory candidate in places where
everybody knew that under the old system he
would have been hard put to it to save his de-
posit, part of the community shrieked with mirth,
and another part unwisely concluded that it was
all over bar the shouting. In the last days of the
campaign or scramble or dog-figlit the “Liberals”
started to pose as the big-loan party, and to fill the
air with denunciations of the Labor policy of re-
stricted borrowing, and even a crowd which had
been drinking out of the bottle should have
known better than that, for Australia is growing
tired of big loans. But the Premier put the
finishing touches to his work when he issued his
final manifesto. In that disastrous document the
public found not one definite promise to do any-
thing—only the vague old remarks about sound
finance and honest government, and maintenance
of the Constitution, which have been the stock-in-
trade of every second-rate politician since the
original second-rate politician was born. And that
was very long ago, when Mandril Trektop was
King, and the deficit was stated in cocoanuts.
On Friday the Ministerial majority vanished,
and about one-third of the Ministry lost the num-
ber of its mess. The Labor victory should have
been much greater, but the best that can be said
for the Labor leaders is that they didn’t make
quite so grievous a muddle of things as their
opponents. James McGowen is a plain, ordin-
ary, uninspiring man who has grown old in
Opposition. He gives the impression that his
real name is Fbenezkr Stodge. His second in
command is a flashy, voluble, shifty individual of
whom little is expected. They both share Wade’s
delusion that what the great heart of New South
Wales desires is a grumpy attitude towards the
other States ; a sour hostility towards the Com-
monwealth ; a mean, cantankerous anti-Australian-
ism ; a darkly suspicious outlook upon those parts
of their own State which don’t happen to be Syd-
ney ; and a grabbing for that city, by clean or
infected means, of everything that can be grabbed
at Australia’s expense. Though Sydney cares so
little for having the Australian capital right at
the office doors of the Herald and Telegraph
that it didn’t raise one public meeting in protest
when it was proposed, in Australia’s interests, to
place it in another part of the State, yet
McGowen and Holman firmly believe that the
heavens will fall if the capital isn’t placed right
alongside the Herald and Telegraph because
these papers have told them so. Their faith in
Tory papers is pathetic. Still, things might
be a great deal worse than they are.
In the first place, the Labor party,
State and Federal, is to a great extent one
organisation, and as it spreads all over Australia
it is difficult for even the most local kind of Labor
man to avoid being occasionally threatened with
a broad Australian view. Then the Labor party
lias a definite programme supplied by the central
authority; and, though it doesn’t include an abso-
lute no-borrowing policy, it gets nearer thereto
than any other programme which has yet been
published in N.S. W. Above all, the Labor leader
lias the advantage of not being Wade ; and any
time in the last six months the advantage of not
being Wade has been something tremendous.
When the Labor Ministry is formed it will
consist wholly of inexperienced men; and
such a Ministry, if opposed by an able tactician,
often gets into discredit by falling over its own
feet on matters of detail. In this case the Labor
party will have an unusual advantage, for its
chief opponent is a man who always falls over his
own feet. With reasonable honesty, no burden
of a disreputable past, and the chance of doing
things in the intervals when the Opposition
leader is slowly getting up after stumbling over
his personal extremities, a McGowen Cabinet will
have a fair chance of usefulness. And when
Labor takes office, and becomes a constructive
body, the capable men of the party will have an
opportunity to come to the front. Most of the
aggregate intelligence of the State is behind the
Labor movement. It is mere accident—the acci-
dent of long stagnation on the Opposition
benches—which has enabled the brown, dull,
Sunday-school element of the party to assert itself
by a process of seniority, which is only another
name*for old age, which is another term for mould
or fluff.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
More About the Parish Pump Railway.
Were the Commonwealth bound to build the line
directly for the benefit'of the comparatively small popu-
lation around Adelaide, and not for the benefit of the
whole of Australia ? What this Bill does is to tie the
hands of the Federal Parliament down to one route. It
is not pretended that there would be any traffic on the
line running from Adelaide to Port Darwin.. But, if the
line were brought down from Port Darwin into Queens-
land territory, and then connected wtih New South
Wales, and through New South Wales to Victoria, it
would open an immense area of country and secure a vast
extent of traffic. In South Australia it will serve a hand-
ful of people, and will never, on the testimony of every-
body—not in South Australia —pay for the grease on the
engine wheels. By striking into the Itastern States it
would at once come into contact with three millions and
more of people. The effect on the development and ex-
pansion of settlement in the Northern Territory must be
obvious. — Sydney Telegraph, ISIIO/’lO.
If Australia dies young it Will be written on its
tombstone that pafochial selfishness was mainly
responsible for the deep damnation of its pegging
out. The craving to bring everything to “our”
State and plant it at “ our ” back door beside
“our” hen-house and adjacent to “our” ash-
barrel, or else to let Australia go to perdition out
of sheer cusseduess, seems quite incurable among
the Tory classes. The above is a fine sample of
the sort of argument which is considered good
enough in a notable parish-pump case. For some
curious reason it is considered necessary in Tory
quarters to bring the very essential transcon-
tinental railway round by a long eastern circum-
bendibus. It is first to come at great length
through that dry western strip of Queensland in
which lie the perishing towns of Boulia and
Birdsville. This strip, from about the mouth of
the Norman River southward, isn’t really Queens-
land, but a bit of the Northern Territory tem-
porarily attached to Queensland under letteis
patent, just as the rest of the Territory is attached
to South Australia; and if a railway built with
Federal money comes through it the question of
Queensland’s wholly unreliable title will almost
certainly be raised one day or another. And no
one can say that Queensland would not be rightly
served for its gross selfishness if that event hap-
pened, and confiscation followed. Then the devia-
tion party proposes to bring the transcontinental
into N.S.W., and connect it with the fading town
of Bourke. The Bourke railway thinks nothing
of losing £15,000 in a single year, and the adja-
cent branch lines to Brewarrma and Warren are
also highly unprofitable speculations. I here
is to be a connection with the fast-wearing-
out silver town of Broken Hill and the
utterly dead towns of Tnackennga (which
has really vanished off the earth) and
Silverton. Possibly there will also be a connec-
tion with the Riverina lines to Hay and Finley
(N.S.W.), which lose, between them, nearly £50,000
a year. It is in this land of fading population and
mostly unpayable railways that the
transcontinental is to find “a vast ex *
traffic.” Also it is to “ open an immense area or
country” (much of it country which has been
opened by State railways and has closed aga ),
then the direct line would also “open up a
rnense area of country.” In order to crawl
these parts the line is to be made longer and more
costly. It will occupy more time in build ng
under these conditions, and no one k
much or how little time we have lhe distance
between Port Darwin and Melbourne, o
Darwin and Perth, will be lengthened by
the deviation; and that between lor ‘
win and Sydney will be made very
shorter than by the straight hne a
connection with the waters of Port Jackson,
Broken Hill. The railway will “come in contact
with three millions and more of people j •
much one way as the other, and, despite
hard lying of which the TELEGRAPH is ’
there is piobably far more chance '
grease on the straight route than by the d *
When N.S.W. can’t get axle-grease out of its mi n
line to Bourke, the great riches of the _count
thereabouts are problematical. Ih© devia
will more directly take in the little ci y
Brisbane, at the cost of leaving out the larger and
more promising and more progressive Adelaide,
will increase the distance between Central Aus-
tralia and Europe, and it will add greatly to the
number of miles that troops will have to travel in
order to reach Port Darwin overland in time or
war. Also, it will do away with the hope ot
having a Federal line from sea to sea, with a
Federal port at each end. But with a certain
class the satisfaction of dragging the railway into
“our” State outweighs all other considerations.
The deviated line has not one advantage or pros-
pect or hope to recommend it from the Australian
point of view, and that fact is obvious to 99 out ot
every 100 of its advocates. But it is backed by the
parish habit of mind, and the parish habit of mmd
accounts for manv mad doings.

Failure of the Dilapidated Pub Party.
The No License party struck its Whiskyloo in
N.S.W. last Friday. It didn’t carry No License
in a single electorate, and it carried Reduction in
very few. The weird idea that the regeneration
of man can be brought about by compelling him
to buy at least two gallons of liquor when lie only
wants half a pint has been considered by the
people, and their verdict is that they can t see the
point. All through the anti-liquor campaign
which preceded the election the community was
blighted with statistics about the alleged de-
ciease of drinking in Maoriland No License
districts—also about the alleged increase. Ihe
windows of Heaven were opened, and it
rained figures for 40 days and as many
nights. To all this devout mullock there was
only one possible answer. If liquor is a bad
thing, then it is a fool (or else a knave) of a party
which, wilfully and needlessly, allows it to be
brought into No License districts in unlimited
quantities, to be sold freely (provided you buy a
darned lot more of it than you used to do), to be
kept in clubs and private houses (any quantity of
it), and to be drunk to almost any extent. And
if liquor is a good thing, then it is sheer hypocrisy
to stop the retail sale of it under condi-
tions in which the seller dare not encourage
actual drunkenness for fear of losing his
valuable license. All the eloquence, and all
the loose and tight statistics on earth can t alter
the fact that the position is wholly illogical, and
all the figures about the alleged success of 1 ro-
hibition in the United States have no bearing on
the case, for Prohibition is no more No License
than Mosks the law-giver is Moss the money-
lender. To people who have visited the JSo
License districts of Maoriland the policy is not
inspiriting. As a rule, the}' find the once fine and
comfortable hotels degenerated apparently
through the loss of the bar profits—into faded
hash-towers, while a furtive smell of drink sug-
gets that the profits are still there, but that
some one else is enjoying them. The gene-
ral impression is that No License is a
bad tiling for the hotelkeeper and a very
good one for the wholesale dealer, the
man who runs a club, the sly-grog person and the
secret drinker who is glad of an excuse to keep
his liquor in the house and soak surreptitiously.
The system which makes two gallons of beer
legal and one glass unlawful passes comprehen-
sion, and all the Judkinses on earth can’t make it
understandable. In fact, it is strongly suspected
that the No License crowd itself realises the
absurdity of its position, though its stupendous
vanity prevents it acknowledging the fact. So
Judkins drags his diminished tail back to the
place whence he came, and a period of rest is
expected.

Fourteen Old Gentlemen.
At the present time 14 old gentlemen, most of
them not even threatened with brains, are hold-
ing up progressive legislation in Victoria. A
house of nearly 70 active members puts in a
laborious time passing measures; a day or two
later, the 14 old gentlemen apply their collective
boot and kick the measures out. A very
good Electoral Act was treated in that manner
a few days ago; and then an Act providing
for a wages board for coal-miners. Owing to the
action of these ancient dodderers, the Victorian
State coal mine is going about in hobbles, as they
put a clause in the Coal Mines Act prohibiting the
State from selling coal to the general public, how
comes the entertaining spectacle of the moderate
Tories abusing the 14 old people and pointing out
that they are, in a fat-headed fashion, playing into
thehands of the Labor party. Asan example, the
land tax is quoted. If these 14 fossils had per-
mitted a State land tax, they would have lore-
stalled the Federal land tax. But still the 14
relics go on their fatuous way, and, as the
moderates see, their muddle-headed opposi-
tion to all liberal measures will be used
with effect when the two referendnms to alter the
Federal Constitution come to be voted on shortly.
The Victorian House of Fat has only four decent
Liberals in it. Six other members usually vote
with the Government. An average meeting is
24, so that the 14 aged ruins who are against
all’ progress can do just what they please.
The only consolation for Liberals is that the 14
family heirlooms are really pulling the House
down on top of themselves. When it does fall, it
will never be reconstructed. In fact, it is
very likely that no one will ever trouble to
search for the political remains of the 14 old
gentlemen who will have been responsible for the
catastrophe.
Queensland’s Separation Movement and Other
Separation Movements.
A curiously futile-looking business is the propo-
sition of Higgs, member of the House of Repre-
sentatives, for the subdivision of Queensland into
three States. The arguments in favor of subdi-


vision are good enough, yet Queensland’s case is
not so unusually awful that the representatives
of the other States are likely to burst their
boilers in the effort to get it any special
form of , relief. North and Central Queens-
land don’t require separation, as the northern
and southern parts of N.S.W. do, because it is
the only means of escape from the oppressive in-
fluence of an overgrown capital, which keeps their
ports closed against foreign commerce and com-
pels the people to pay railway charges over
hundreds of useless miles in order that they may
export and import through the bloated metropolis,
lownsville, Rockhampton and Cairns have big
railways striking out into the west —the sort of
railways that Coff’s Harbor, Port Stephens and
Twofold Bay, in N.S.W., want so badly. Nor
does Queensland want separation nearly so much
as Western Victoria does; if Portland was half
as well treated by Melbourne, in the way of
railway facilities, as Townsville is by Bris-
bane, it would complain a great deal less
than it does, and be rich enough to drink a great
deal more. And even if Queensland is an un-
wieldy State it doesn’t call for subdivision on
that ground near so urgently as Westralia does.
v ttlese circumstances certain members from
other States are not likely to enthuse much over
a proposal that they should rush to assist Queens-
land in this matter without any assurance that
Queensland will assist them in return. More-
over, the method of Higgs, M. H.R., is so hope-
less that it seems mere waste of time to meddle
with the matter. He suggests that the two
b ederal Houses shall declare their willingness to
create the new States of Northern and Central
Queensland. Should they do so, thenthenew States
may be established, provided the State Legislature
at Brisbane is willing, but not otherwise ; and the
chance that the State Legislature at Brisbane
may be willing is apparently just about what it
was any time the last 30 years or so—-no less and
not much more. If it was proposed to take a
referendum of all Australia on the question of
temporarily handing over to the Australian Par-
liament all the powers, assets and liabilities of the
local Legislatures, so that it might subdivide the
country into small manageable States, each with
a common-sensible little Parliament of one Cham-
ber, there would be no need to ask the consent of
the two local Houses at Brisbane, or the two local
Houses anywhere else. In that case, it might
be possible to achieve something. Queensland’s
Separation movement would then be backed up
by the full strength of everybody else’s separation
movement, instead of playing a lone hand. As it
is, the rank selfishness of the demand that
Queensland’s little grievance should receive
special attention is more likely to evoke cuss
language than outside support. The fact will be
specially noted that, in the event of Separation,
South Queensland, as the original State, would re-
tain all its old representation in the Senate, though
its membership in the House of Reps, might be
diminished, while the two new States of North and
Central Queensland would also have, of necessity,
a certain amount of representation—the exact
amount would be fixed by Parliament. There-
fore subdivision of one State only would mean
a congestion of political power in tiiat corner of
the Commonwealth quite irrespective of popula-
tion. And the new States of Separated Queens-
land, enjoying a rush of Federal authority to the
head, would probably rather oppose than assist a
demand for autonomy from Kalgoorhe or Monaro
or the Western District of Victoria on the ground
that the original act of Separation had done a lot
to shift the balance of Federal power to the north
(which was good), while the establishment of these
new States, each with a separate membership in
the Australian Parliament, would tend to remove
it south again (which would be darned bad). All
these considerations tend to cast such a gloom
over the proposals of Higgs, M.H.R., that it
seems hardly worth while to pursue them further.
NS.W.’s Endorsement of the “Unwritten
Law.”
If, in N.S.W., you suspect an acquaintance of
fiivolling with your wife, and in a burst of annoy-
ance proceed to mutilate him with a knife so
that his life is endangered and his health perma-
nently injured, nothing particular will happen to
you. Certainly you may be tried. But the jury
will recommend you to mercy, and urge his
Honor to treat you as a first offender; and his
Honor, with tears of sympathy in his voice, will
bind you over to come up for sentence if called
on. This was established when John Heath, the
mutilator of Mrs. Heath’s friend Mottekshead
left the dock at Darlinghurst last week
a free man, trailing judicial bouquets as
to his exalted character behind him. It
amounts to an endorsement by the court of the
“ unwritten law ” business which is reprobated
even in America, the land of the lyncher. Adul-
tery is a reprehensible practice, no doubt, but it
is not a crime. Injured husbands are provided,
under Australian law, with a remedy against
those who wrong them. Many cases occur
daily, in the vast territory which that law em-
braces, far harder than the case of John Heath.
It is certain that many of the husbands
who, like Heath, suspect that they have been
betrayed, feel quite as mad as Heath felt when
he discovered his supposed rival on the verandah
of his Woolwich home last June. But they don’t
consider that that entitles them to act the part of
torturers or executioners. They know the law,
and they abide by it. If they feel that the home
is irretrievably burst, they start divorce proceed-
ings. If it is revenge they are after, they apply
for damages against the seducer; and, assuming
the latter is guilty, the jury mostly sees to it
that he pays for his lapse. In a whole
heap of cases the husband feels that it
is not for him to throw the heaviest brick,
and there are explanations and a reconciliation.
In any case, the community has not given the
slightest indication, so far, that it considers the
remedies already provided against adultery in-
adequate. It has been left for a N.S.W. Judge
to lay it down that, where a man unlawfully
wounds another, and does it with such thorough-
ness that the victim’s life is despaired of for
weeks, he (the mutilator) shall be treated as a
species of Galahad, and discharged from custody
—not merely without a stain on his character, but
with a feather in his cap. The amazing treatment
meted out to Heath is likely to do more
than cast mud, dead cats and general obloquy
at the State’s divorce law. There are numbers of
people, of both sexes, possessed of ungovernable
tempers and jealous dispositions, who need scant
encouragement to behave as Heath did, and who
are extremely liable to get to work with a lethal
weapon on suspected supplanters the moment
they consider it is safe to do so. It will not
be the fault of Judge Rogers and the jury
associated with him in the trial of Heath if the
work of Divorce-Court Judges in N.S.W. is
lightened in the future and that of the casualty
wards and morgues vastly increased.
The Bookmaker: A Sermon for Young Men-
The bookmaker, with a good deal of justice, claims
that whatever he gets he earns, and earns it by hard
mental work of the most nerve-racking character.—
Melb. Argus.
This sympathetic statement of the bookmaker’s
claim to toleration is preceded by a reference to
the high expenses which the hard mental worker
has to meet. He has to pay license fees, clerks,
travelling expenses, admission fees (the travelling
and admission expenses are common also to the
backers on whom he lives), and “he has to
buy information, for early racing news is
of vital importance to him.’’ Thus does
the Argus, and every other counsel for
the defence of Ikey Mo, argue uncon-
sciously for the legalisation of the totalisator,
whilst striving to perpetuate the evil of the
man with the leather bag. Granting that
bookmakers put a lot more mental work into their
business than (say) bootmakers, even the Argus
must admit that, from a national point of view,
their industry is a shocking loss of brain-toil to
the community. They produce nothing but a
conflict of betting-market prices which keeps the
punters (or buyers of odds) in a perpetual state of
doubt as to whether they are getting fair treat-
ment. The punter, if he is not a careless simple-
ton, hunts around the ring for the longest
available price about his “fancy,” and when
lie finally accepts five to two from Moses,
he hears Aaron in the distance offering three
to one. This phase of the “ glorious uncer-
tainty of the turf” is eliminated by the tote.
The machine lays one price to all people.
No unnecessary expenditure in hard mental labor
is imposed on it. It has no nerves to be racked.
It spares the public the din of the betting ring,
and in its own peaceful fashion pays two divi-
dends instead of one. When a rank outsider
“ pips” the red-hot favorite on the post the book-
makers pay 20 to 1 to a very few punters and
nothing at all to the others. The tote, under the
same circumstances, pays 30 or 50 to 1 to the lucky
few and returns some of their money to the many
who backed the favorite at evens or short odds.
To the tote it is immaterial whether certain
horses are being backed on the strength of good
trials, or on account of roguish arrangements
whereby other horses are “ stiffened.” The “ fit-
ness ” or “unfitness” of any individual starter
matters nothing to the machine. It never needs
to “buy information,” whereas the bookmaker
must get enough out of the public to keep him in
“information” as well as in champagne, dia-
monds and other luxuries. Nay, if he is to be an
exceedingly influential trader, who keeps horses
of liis own and “mixes” his game, he must be
provided by the public with ample means for
bribery and corruption, in order that he may
take the public down and bring off big coups.
How often is it said that “ bookmakers’ horses,”
running in their owners’ names or in the names of
men they control, or ridden by jockeys who are
under their thumbs, are destructive of good sporton
the turf. The tote is a bookmaker who owns no
horses, nor shapes the policy of any stable. It
devises no dishonest schemes, makes no ungodly
noise, employs no tag-rag army of runners
ruffians, tick-tackers and what not. It pays out
over two horses in every race, and devotes. 5 per
cent, of its profits to worthy charity instead of
disbursing half-crowns and half-sovereigns amon° r
wasters, as the bookmakers do. Where it is
established in place of the bookmaker, the silent
respectable machine adds to the productiveness of
the community by withdrawing their old means of
subsistence from quite a large number of able-
bodied parasites, thereby compelling them to find
work. And if the bookmaker—as the Argus
puts it—is a hard mental worker, the
tote which forces him to employ his brains in
another field of industry is a boon to the Com-
monwealth. Finally, the Argos, mentioning in
praise of bookmakers that they “ require a good
deal of ready money to bet at a big meeting,”
says tha,t “ most of them take between £4OO and
£5OO with them.” The tote is not responsible
for any such idleness of capital. Its methods of
living on the community whilst assisting the
public charities entails no deplorable waste of
time, money or nervous energy. All along the
line of argument it is the lesser of two gambling
evils. Political economy demands it in place of
the bookmaker. Punters plead for it; “ the
sport of kings ” wants it badly. And N.S.W. and
Victoria are bound to get it.

Mock-Martyrdom in Politics.
The “Liberal” press overdid things in its
efforts to present C. G. Wade as a Calum-
niated Saint. There came a time when the
public wearied of Peter Bowling, his hard-
ware and his jury panel; and that was the
psychological moment for Liberalism to have
dropped all such criticisms with a sneer, where-
upon they would have died from old age and
dulness. But “ Liberalism ” didn’t know enough.
Long after newspaper-readers were utterly sick
of the circumstances under which Bowling was
gaoled, the wooden-headed Sydney journals con-
tinued to use the misfortunes of Peter as texts
whereon to hang agonised discourses concerning
the wrongs of Wade. This error in tactics pre-
sented the Labor men with excuses to pour
further broadsides into the sporting champion
from Chatswood. The final rally between
Wade and Meagher, on the subject of the
jury panel scandal, must have lost the
Liberal cause heaps of votes by sheeting home
the fact that, however peerless Wade was as a
footballer, he was a failure as a martyr, arid a
shifty politician to boot. That the Crown Law
authorities passed the Bowling jury-panel on to
the police, in order that inquiries might be insti-
tuted regarding the jurymen’s “impartiality”
could not be contested. Wade was therefore
hounded on by his supporters to show that what
he had done had been done regularly by N.S.W.
Attorney-Generals. The evidence adduced was
thin. It was suggested that Jack Want approved
of the practice. “With him,” said Wade, “I
discussed the question many times.” Jack
Want is dead, and be left no written evi-
dence in support of Wade’s contention. On
the other hand, the only two ex-Attorneys-
General of N.S.W. who were living and available
denied ever having heard of such a practice before
the Bowling trial. Wade quoted the late C..T.
Darley as one who held that it was permissible
for the Crown to sound jurors before a criminal
trial. Darley is as dead as Want; but he was
an unhappy pick, inasmuch as his opinions on this
question are to be found in black and white. The
Laborites mentioned the case of Parker v.
Falkner, quoted by The Bulletin, a couple of
months back, where Darley laid it down that:—
The moment the jurymen are summoned they become
part and parcel of the tribunal which may have to try the
case, and any party who attempts to approach any jury-
man when once summoned is guilty of interfering with
the due administration of justice.
L T pon this weighty brick being propelled at him,
Wade suddenly encased himself in his righteous-
ness as in a blanket, and retired from the fray,
intimating that he would leave the country to
judge between him and his critics. The country
would have been demented if it had con-
sidered Wade’s character magnificent enough
to ' weigh down the plain facts that were
stated against his administration. Undoubt-
edly thousands of votes were lost to “Liberal-
ism ” by the nauseating flood of cant and
mock heroism that surged round it. The moral
seems to be that in future “Liberalism’’should
go slow with the Wronged Idol ticket, unless
there is a certainty of being able to show that the
Idol in question really was wronged. There is no
spectacle at which the “great heart of the popu-
lace ” is more revolted than that of a person arro-
gating to himself a halo when the only headgear
he is properly entitled to is an ordinary plug hat.
The Church Bell That Is Only Holy
After 9 a.m.
A Judge of the Vic. Supreme Court has granted
an injunction against the ringing of a certain Mel-
bourne church bell in the early morning of Sun-
days and holidays. It was a horrible instrument
of ear-torture, and his Honor’s comments during
the progress of the case suggested that he would
have sentenced it to utter silence but for the fear
of exciting godless outcries against all church
bells. So he compromised by deciding, not that
the complainant had a right to protection against
the nuisance of the church bell at all times, even
as all citizens are safeguarded against muffin bells,
but that he was entitled to “sleep in,” if he
wished, on Sundays and other days of rest. Said
his Honor: —
I think it a plain, sober and simple notion to object to
being roused from sleep by your neighbor’s bell
when you are lying in bed at 7’30 a.m. on Sunday morn-
ing. I think that a large portion of the dwellers in Mel-
bourne suburbs would be found in bed and asleep at
7*30 a.m. on Sunday morning. That many people are up
and about at that time does not lessen the right of others
to remain in bed and complain of being pulled out by the
ears. Churches are not exempt from the application of
the maxim, which holds good in other cases of nuisance.
So the complainants, man and wife, got a verdict
for the £5 damages which they had claimed, in
addition, to Costs ; and the vicar and his church
guardians were forbidden to ring the bell before
9 a. m. on Sundays, Good Fridays, Easter Mondays,
etc. But, for once in a way, the judicial argument
was more interesting than the judicial decision. If
the bell were part of the business carried on in
the church, his Honor confessed he could have
done nothing. But—and herein one gets the
most interesting view of Justice a’Beckett’s
judgment—bell-ringing was “not a necessary
incident to its trade.” Had it been an item in
the church service, like choir-singing and organ-
playing, it would have counted as a noise
“actually necessary for trade and commerce,”
and then the plaintiffs would have lost their case.
A citizen must subject himself to the conse-
quences of legitimate trade operations in his
immediate locality. He has no remedy, for
instance, against the noise of railway trains.
At the same -time, it looks as though he might
have an action against Tait if what worried him
was the whistle or the bell of warning that Tait’s
conveyance was about to start. It is, however,
the compromise part of the business that is
likely to cause trouble. In the first place, the
church is judicially classified as a business con-
cern, and therefore not entitled, any more than
any other trade, to make a noise that isn’t actu-
ally necessary to-the trade. But, while that is
laid down as the law, the decision practically
means that the church is a trade only up to 9
o’clock on certain mornings; after that it isn’t a
trade, and can ring the bell l whether it is a
nuisance or not. This leaves the matter vague
and unsatisfactory, and encourages the muffin-
man to argue that, while his is a trade on Mon-
days, or when the wind is blowing from the
west, it is something else on Tuesdays, or when
the wind is blowing from the north, or on the
nine days following full moon.

The Slaughtermen’s Strike of the Future.
If the statement made by Senator
Pearce, Commonwealth Minister for Defence,
concerning the power of the German Socialist
party in maintaining the peace of Europe, is not
entirely correct, there is a good deal of truth in
it. Said Pearce
It was an acknowledged fact to-day that the peace of
Europe was guaranteed not by any entente cordiale
between the nations, or friendship between the crowned
heads, but by the growing power of the Socialist party
in Germany. If that party became the dominant party
it would be the greatest guarantee of peace they could
have.
An important discussion on the best means of
halting red war before it can start to serve up
desolation took place at the recent International
Socialist Conference at Copenhagen. A sub-
committee of the anti-Militarist Commission was
appointed to frame a composite motion, and the
only critical point of difference among members
was as to the advisability of recommending a
general strike in the event of national hostilities.
Kier Hardie (British) and Vail bant (French)
strongly urged the inclusion of a paragraph to
that effect, but Leoebour (German) raised objec-
tions. The difficulty was eventually overcome
by Hardie and Vaillant being allowed to move
the strike proposal as an amendment to the
general recommendation. Later, the amendment
was brought down as follows :
The Congress recommends the affiliated parties and
Labor organisations to consider the advisability and
feasibility of the general strike, especially in industries
that supply war material, as one of the methods of pre-
venting war, and that action be taken on the subject at
the next Congress.
Ledebour, on behalf of the German party,
attacked the amendment with all his brilliancy,
on the ground that while it was right in theory
it was quite impracticable—which doesn’t seem
to bear out Senator Pearce too well. The British,
French, American and South German represen-
tatives supported the amendment, but the Aus-
trians doubted its wisdom. One said that its
adoption would sound the death-knell of the
Social democratic party in Austria. The Bel-
gians refrained from voting. Eventually a com-
promise was arrived at whereby the amend-
ment was carried on the understanding that
it would be considered by the International
Bureau. There can, then, be no doubt that the
war-strike is being seriously considered. Even
in Japan, with all its newly-acquired national
arrogance, the note has been sounded, and quite
recently a well-known Japanese writer published
an article on “Socialism in Japan,” in which he
discussed the future relations between that coun-
try and the United States, and appealed to the
Socialists of the world and the workers generally
to prevent war breaking out between Uncle Sam.
and the Jap. For myself, I have great hopes of
yet shaking hands with the day when, on Fat
clanging the war-bell and yelling “ Toarrns !” the
people of the nations will down tools and tell the
perspiring gentleman to go to arms himself.
A cry from the West:—
Westralia has the Closed Ports complaint in almost as
virulent a form as either Victoria or N.S.W.; and a glar-
ing instance thereof is Esperance. The linking of
Esperance with the Eastern Goldfields would bring
Kalgoorlie, Boulder, Coolgardie and Kanowna 150 miles
nearer a seaport; also it would bring them two days
nearer the Eastern States ; for, besides the saving in rail-
travelling, they would have 524 miles less of the uncertain
ocean that heaves around the Leeu win. The distance from
Adelaide to Kalgoorlie by way of Fremantle is 1737 miles ;
by way of Esperance it is about 1056 miles. But still, as in
the days when the fields were being pioneered, all traffic has
to be dragged round the Leeuwin, because the idea of
opening- up a new port on the south coast is anathema to
the Perth Government.
Esperance is an ideal health-resort. To open it to the
interior would be to confer a blessing on the scorched
Goldfielders hankering for a change. From a defence
viewpoint, also, it is desirable to attract population to
the empty coastline between Albany and Eucla. And
as a business proposition the scheme is sound. There is
a large belt of excellent land between Esperance and
Norseman. Within the last six months 120,000 acres
have been applied for, and there is little doubt that over
a million acres would be taken up were the railway built.
There is a large population on the Goldfields eager to
settle on the land, and in no need to go hunting for
selections further afield when there is material for a new
agricultural settlement between Kalgoorlie and the
nearest seaboard.
But Perth Governments and the Perth press object
bitterly, in the interests of West Coast property-owners,
to every forward move ; and at any mention of a railway
frem the Goldfields to their nearest port a yell arises sug-
gesilve of Fat being violently separated from Capital.
Wherefore, despite petitions, deputations, resolutions of
public bodies, and Parliamentary motions by Goldfields
members—despite the resolution of the Chamber of Mines
in 1901, “ that the railway would be beneficial to the whole
of the State, and should be constructed without delay,”
despite the action of Mahon and Kirwan, M’s.H.R., in
appealing to the Federal Parliament in 1002, Esper-
ance in Westralia stays as hermetically sealed as Port
Stephens in N.S.W.
There is a national as well as a local aspect to this
question. The transcontinental railway from Kalgoorlie
to Port Augusta can’t be delayed many years more. If
the Esperance-Norseman line precedes the larger work,
the saving in cost to the Commonwealth must be very
large. From Fremantle to Kalgoorlie is 387 miles, as
against 240 miles from Esperance, and rails for 500 miles
of line at least will have to be conveyed to the Kalgoorlie
end when the line is put in hand.

A new subject of bitter complaint on the part of
the N.S.W. Premier arose last week, when news
(which was really an unaccountable republication
of a fact that was advertised 12 months ago)
came to hand that the British naval headquarters
in these seas would probably be removed to Auck-
land (M.L.). This shows YVade in an astonishing
aspect; apparently he wants Sydney to be not
only the most crowded commercial harbor in Aus-
tralia, and the headquarters of the Australian
navy, but the headquarters of the local British
squadron also. Yet Australia, when once it gets
its own fleet afloat, will cease to have any
connection or relationship with the British
squadron hereabouts. That composite insti-
tution will consist of several British vessels,
appointed to the Chinese station, and the Maori,
land ships—Maoriland being the kind of place
which prefers to hitch on to a distant Anglo-Chow
squadron than to connect with an Australian one
closer at hand. The idea of making a State
grievance out of the fact that this partly-Maori-
land fleet is likely to have its local headquarters
in its own country instead of at Sydney is the
sort of thing that only an impossible person like
Wade would be guilty of. In any case, Auckland
harbor is larger, deeper and better protected from
attack than Sydney, and it is far less crowded
with mercantile shipping; also, it has almost the
best graving dock in the world. It isn’t so pic-
turesque as Port Jackson, and, ashore, aggravated
piety and No License are fast making life a
burden ; but, all the same, the advantages from a
strictly business point of view are such that the
removal is almost certain to happen one day or
another.
Peace and War.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Baptist Union to-day deplored recent legislation
for the purpose of enforcing conscription, and expressed
the hope that, in all matters of difference between the
nations, Australia would endeavor to the very utmost to
adjust matters by methods of peaceful arbitration.—
News item.
I stood awhile by the ocean-side of life,
Commanding that the ebb and flow should
cease;
I said : “ These are the dying days of strife—
All hail unto the dawn of perfect peace ! ”
Whilst yet I spoke I saw the lightning’s flash
Leap out in flamed derision ’thwart the sky ;
And in the rolling thunder’s heavy crash
This universe flung forth its soul’s reply :
“I that was born before the birth of Time—
The spawn of worlds that met all-palpitate;
I that was shaped in passioned hours sublime,
’Midst shock of spheres and cannonading Fate;
I that have felt the glaciers on my flanks,
And known the sullen roar of angry seas—
This is my word : ‘ Prepare the fighting ranks!
There is no Peace ! ’
“The steaming oceans cooled in days of yore,
And back and forth there swam first shapes of
life;
Strange creatures left the surges for the shore,
To raise again their mimic forms of strife.
In them I saw the anger of the spheres,
And all the primal force and bitter hate ;
I heard the whirling loom of many years,
Slow-shaping Man from warp and wefts of Fate;
He, too, was born with anger in his blood,
And all along his corridors of soul
I saw the passions pour their boiling flood,
Whilst Bitterness and Hate kept grim patrol !
Who prates of peace—of days when fighting men
From all the iron grips of war will cease ?
For these I write with lightning’s vivid pen :
‘ There is no peace 1 ’ ”
I hastened forth—the ocean-tide of life
Still flings its angry surges on the shore ;
I see the sullen nations heave in strife,
And primal force leaps high in days of war ;
Vain voices lift their clamor in the night,
And saddened preachers ask the endless
“Why
In Calvary’s and Nazarene’s despite,
Still lifts the vanquished sob and victor’s cry ?”
Of mythic Peace hear ye the ancient prate.
Whilst lands grown weak put faith in thewless
laws;
Before the storms of wrath and deathless hate
Wrecked empires blow like winter’s scattered
straws !
’Neath protoplasm and the stern phalanx
Force surges still—and strength will have its
fees;
All things cry out: “ Prepare the fighting ranks —
There is no Peace !”

“ Liberalism’s ” Tribute to Labor.
Thebe was one point in regard to the N.S.W.
elections that the “Liberal” dailies were agreed
on. Here is what the S. M. Hehald said, on
the Monday following the unpleasantness of
October 14: —
Liberals owe such defeat as they have met with to their
deep-rooted apathy.
And this is how the Telegraph put it
The heavy 7 percentage of electors who did not. vote were
chiefly Liberals. To the apathetic Liberals must be
assigned the whole responsibility.
Consider some of the people who are so un-
patriotic, or mad, or bad, or all three, as to take
no interest in the urgent question of how their
country shall be governed. A big proportion of
them are criminals, habitual and otherwise. To
the professional burglar, for instance, it is a matter
of indifference which political party comes out on
top, since each is equally hostile to the house-
breaking line of Private Enterprise. Dipso-
maniacs don’t vote. They lack the moral sense
which impels the normal citizen to do his duty at
the polls ; and, anyhow, when polling day comes,
they are liable to be too drunk to vote ; failing
which they are probably suffering a recovery
from the previous day’s burst, and are in
no condition to toil to the booth. The same
applies tomorphinomaniacs, opium-eaters and drug
fiends generally. Ladies of the Flossie type are
notorious non-voters, as are operatives like
“Bill the Bludger” mentioned by Reuter last
week. Persons of weak intellect stay at
home and yammer while their more intelligent
relatives and friends go forth to deliver a biff
at Progress or Reaction, as the case may be. Race-
course touts, spielers, tick-tackers, three-card
experts, Fiji uncles, bridge-maniacs, card and
billiard sharps, pub-loafers, gutter-rakers, domain-
dossers, purse-snatchers, counter-lunchers and the
erotic kleptomaniacs who live propped against city
verandah-posts for the purpose of leering at pass-
ing females, are all of the non-voting class. So are
illiterates and folks who, though sane and able by
virtue of a socialistic educational system to read
and write, are of such low mental calibre that
they lack the capacity to distinguish between
political issues. People with the “ tired feeling ”
in an acute form —those whom psychologists
term degenerates—never vote; they are too
weary, and, anyhow, they can’t appreciate that it
is worth the trouble. In short, it is the decent and
intelligent people in any community who may be
relied on to sacrifice their personal convenience in
what they conceive to be the cause of the
State; and it is the utter, hopeless wasters
who are cheerfully prepared to see the State
go to Sheol before they devote five minutes
of their leisure to helping to avert the catas-
trophe. Labor doesn’t often have adulation
spilled on it by “Liberalism,” wherefore it must be
all the more cheering to it to learn—from the
enemy’s own lips, as it were—that the majority
of thinking and patriotic people are on its side,
the hordes of the short-sighted, ignorant and
criminally lazy, who constitute the army of non-
voters, being almost exclusively “Liberals.”
