The Case of Mr. Sealing-Wax.
“ Red Tape Junior One of the curiosities of
the N.S.W. election campaign was the monstrous
politeness of the S. M. Herald to the Public
Service. Almost every day the paper was breath-
less with anxiety as to the conditions under which
dear Mr. Sealing-Wax was working, and the
health and happiness of Mrs. Sealing-Wax and
the sweet children. Three days running we
had a leader to ourselves, pointing out that
McGowen (who is only a Labor person,
after all, my dear) never bothered his head
about the Sealing-Waxers except when he
wanted their votes. Not content with this
solicitude, the large-hearted old thing thought-
fully published a two-column heart-to-heart talk
with the Service by “£.S.D.” Apparently he
was one of us, although I don’t know anybody
in the Service named £.S.D., or even S.D.
He fractured about 11 Acts and 99 regulations
by meddling in politics, but he triumphantly
proved McGowen’s intention of giving us greater
scope and a wider field by nationalising iron
works, sugar works, boiling-down works, coal-
mines, liquor traffic, hot-pie stalls, McCaughey’s
bit o’ land, David Storey’s hattery, and Uncle’s
place. Altogether, I counted up several dozen
reasons why we should vote for McGowen, and
one why we should vote for Wade —that one being
the goal he kicked at Oxford. Probably the old
lady’s age made her forget to mention that Wade
had refused the Service an Appeal Board, did not
provide a superannuation fund (except the rail-
way one, which will probably run off the rails and
break its back before long), did not arrange a re-
constitution of the P. S. Board, abolished our ex-
aminations, and handed over many of the plums
of the Service to hangers-on. The Public Service
Journal held a better hand recently when it
said :
From the time that the Public Service Act became law
it was the hope throughout the Service that appoint-
ments as far as possible would, as the Act directs, be
made from inside the Service, and not from outsiders
when there were any plums or otherwise going.
Political patronage nominally received its death-
blow by the Act mentioned; but it has been
better kept in the breach than the observance,
and the blow, far from being final, was, like the
account of Mark Twain’s death—greatly exaggerated.
... And now w'ith the Justices Amendment Act... Mr.
Wade has deliberately opened the door which Mr. Reid
endeavored to lock and bar in the interests of the Ser-
vice, and has, by a clever juggle of words, so amended
the Act that the Board may be able to appoint anyone
suggested by those to whom the Board has to look for
bread and butter. The Board is appointed politically,
and, reflecting upon this, we ask what sort of a chance
would a poor wretch in the Service have when applying
for a stipendiary magistracy against some briefless
barrister or indigent solicitor who had a friend in the
right spot of the political arena ?
That wasn’t exactly the same as the S. M.
Herald said. But the 'difference between the
Journal and the Herald is that one has the in-
terests of the Service to look after, while the
other was concerned with the interests of its pal
Wade.
Jack London’s Snark, which has been laid
up in Lavender Bay, Port Jackson, for a couple
of years past, has been “sold to the Islands,” the
last destination of quite a fleet of sailing craft.
The cost of building the Snark is put down at a
modest £5BOO, and London’s efforts to get some
of his money back has delayed the sale. But at
last the lugger has been sacrificed, and the expen-
sive fittings, etc., will be gutted out before the
anchor’s weighed in a couple of months. Another
one-time pleasure yacht trading thereabouts is
Rupert Clarke’s La Carabine, which now carries
niggers and cocoanuts in the place of beauty at
the prow and pleasure at the helm.
There were only three N.S.W. candidates
who didn’t have to worry about what the electors
might do to them—Caun, McDonell and Scobie.
All three represent far-western districts, and the
“ Liberal ” machine didn’t think it worth while
chasing them. In fact, the only far-western
electorate that the “Liberals ” nominated for was
Sturt; and the Labor candidate treated the oppo-
sition with such supreme contempt that he wrote
a message to the electors, dropped it into
the post, and left it at that. Labor
has been the only brand of member in
far-west N.S.W. since the late Speaker Joe
Abbott’s day. Scobie, a Menindie saddler, gave
big Joe a couple of runs ; but the Speaker’s wig
mesmerised the Westerners, and Scobie had to
wait. Since Abbott left nobody has had a hope
against the large, white-headed, big-hearted
Scotchman, who knows his West almost as well
as he knows his Burns.
The Corpse.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Liberal apathy is responsible lor this defeat.— Chorus
of flabbergasted dailies.
They flog the corpse of Apathy with whips of
angry speech,
And many leading articles are filled with saddened
screech.
The Sydney Mourning Phagocyte is leaping on
its hat,
The apple-cart’s a ruin, and G. Wade is trodden
flat.
A choir of vanished Minister is wailing loud and
long—
You’d think the cheerful cherubim had banged
the Judgment gong.
Behold this fact imperative uplifts its earnest
head—
What ails the prostrate Liberal ? The poor old
cove is dead !
Biff!
The Telegraph despondently surveys the ghastly
scene ;
What horrible catastrophe has caused its anguished
mien ?
Was it some fearsome catapult that flung from
distant Mars
A rock of dreadful magnitude that echoed through
the stars ?
Ah no! the Mourning Phagocyte discreetly does
not say
What struck the perished Liberals in Wade’s
Deforming dray.
But if you ask this Commonwealth precisely what
took place,
You’ll learn State Spites have rocketted ; their
corpse explodes in space.
Bang !
The Argus weeps in Collins-street; its tears sub-
merge the trams;
And crusted old Conservatives are breathing
fervent damns.
The House of Ancient Doubts and Dreams, where
cow-like persons sit.
Is feeling cold, BELSHAZZAR-like; ’tis time for
sloth to quit.
The Commonwealth exultantly begins to swing
its axe,
And Fat’s dishevelled universe is seamed with
yawning cracks.
The hearse is heaped with customers, and Murray
stands aghast,
The party anti-national begins to burst at last.
Crash !
The solemn Slowbart Mercury, a sheet of dismal
lead,
A rag whose ink is chloroform, has sunk its sad-
dened head;
In Tassy’s House of Dodery loud wails the shrill
banshee.
For catafalque and monument await each M.L.C.
Beside old steep Mount Wellington prepare the
coffins now ;
The clammy hand of Azrakl is on the Tory’s
brow.
From Slowbart and from Launceston ascends the
tombful whiff,
Loud howls the sad Examiner, the Tory corpse is
stiff.
Thud !
The sickened Brisbane Courier lies groaning on
the mat;
’Tis hardly breathing (Ichabod !), its gods have
fallen flat.
From Barcaldine to Normanton, from Warwick
to tne Towers,
Bananaland, exultantly, chants “By request, no
flowers ! ”
For now each ancient obstacle goes sliding to the
tomb,
Each Stinking Fish emporium is closed with slabs
of gloom.
There is no balm in Gilead as far as Philp can
see,
And Kidston wails in dismal tones “Death
waiteth near tor me.”
Boom !
The holy city’s Register is clawing out its hair,
The ’Tiser bangs its occiput upon the dismal
stair.
While Dismal Dick disconsolate is weeping in
the soup;
The Peakes in old Crow-eater Land like wilted
blossoms droop.
I hear the Torrens murmuring this jubilant re-
frain :
“The useless push are perishing. Thank God,
they’ll soon be slain ! ”
Old views and bogus policies to-day are grave-
ward whizzing—
The fact infests the continent; Australia’s stocks
have risen.
Hoo-blanky-ray !
In Perth the voice of ’Groperdom goes moaning
through the stars;
But joy pervades the toiler folk who gouge with
drills and bars.
The plain Australian citizen grows cheerful at his
trade,
Joe Cook has wrecked his chariot, and now
farewell to Wade.
The Fusion and the Liberal, whose name is really
Sloth,
Will soon be ancient memories like Kish and
Behemoth.
The nation grasps its implements, whilst from
their sinking raft
The Insects sight a Commonwealth. The land
begins to graft.
Three cheers for Australia !

4.
The general thesis of “Paid in Full,” the
new Sydney Theatre Royal play, is convincing
enough. Emma, the wife of Joe Brooks, has
married, as her family would say, “beneath her.”
Her husband is an ill-paid drudge, and likely to
stay so, being a born incompetent and waster.
It is natural that she should be dissatisfied ;
natural that her mother should call on her and
chatter exasperatingly about the shame of existing
without a “ hired girl ” ; natural also, more or
less, that Captain Williams, a friend of the family
and her husband’s employer, should join in the
general wail of commiseration. And from these
things it follows that the poverty-stricken
Joe Brooks should be in a chronically-
maddened state with his wife, her relations,
his employer and the world at large. In
other words, that he should be ripe for em-
bezzlement, or any other way out of his intoler-
able position. In due course he does embezzle,
and it is hereabouts that the play goes to pieces,
so far as the eternal verities are concerned.
* * * *
Many of the happenings subsequent to the
fall of Joseph are explicable only on the assump-
tion that those concerned have been smitten with
insanity. Joe and his wife leave their cheap
rooms and move abruptly into a gorgeous hotel-
apartment. The embezzler explains that he can
afford this vast change since Williams, previous
to leaving for Guatemala, “raised his screw.’
The lie is too preposterous for human consump-
tion, but Mrs. Brooks is represented as crediting
it, and so are her relations and friends. When, after
a few weeks, Williams returns, Brooks has got
through over 16,000 dollars. Now, Williams is a
bad man to swindle. His perception is super-
human. He has marched to affluence through
murder, piracy, theft, rapine and sundries. To
him, the life of a human being is no more than that
of an insect. He is devoid of pity, fear orany other
human sentiment. Epileptic rage contorts his
crime-scarred visage as he surveys the luxurious
Brooks’ apartment. Joe is informed that his
absurdly-conducted felony is known at head-
quarters. In desperation he implores Emma to
visit Williams in his rooms at night, wins her
consent, and himself arranges the assigna-
tion over the telephone. Williams, heed-
less of possible blackmail complications,
fairly leaps at the proposal. The deeds
and motives of the parties grow’ more and more
unlikely and mysterious. The ex-pirate is
shown chuckling hideously in his lair, what time
he writes on a scrap of notepaper. Mrs. Brooks
enters. Did she ever intend to pay the price for
her husband’s salvation that is implied by a
midnight visit to the flat of a notorious libertine ?
If she didn’t, why not have postponed her call till
the morning and kept he’r character ? Did
Williams mean honorably by her all along, and
was his vaunted infamy a coy disguise ? If so,
why put on this innocent and virtuous lady the
agony and humiliation of trailing round to him
like a mere Female? Anyhow, this allegedly
soulless old malefactor, who has never known
what it was to respect Purity in distress or else-
where, on being roundly abused Brooks
for his licentiousness, unexpectedly effects a
lightning change into the rsle of Heavy Father.
With a winsome smile he produces the scrap of
paper aforementioned, and hands it to his visitor.
And lo ! it is a statement that the erring Joseph’s
books have been audited and found correct. All
Williams asks in return for his 16,000 and
odd dollars is the respect of an American (and
hence virtuous) wife and the right to call her by
her first name. Emma leaves Joe tor ever; but
the audience is not wholly depressed, for it recog-
nises that the fascinating and altogether worthy
“ .Timsy ” Smith is somewhere adjacent, and that
Reno isn’t a bad place to put in a few months,
anyhow, i

Miss Katherine Grey acts incomparably
as the central figure—one of those strong, help-
ful, patient, and (above ail) Good women at whose
shrine American men have worshipped in ten
thousand U.S. novels and plays. Cyril Maekay
presents a masterly portrait of the weak and
currish Brooks. The Captain Williams of George
Bryant is an impressive figure so long as he
remains Bad ; and William Desmond’s “ Jimsy
Smith—-a delightful, if unusual, character, com-
pounded in about equal parts of goodness,
generosity, strength and brain-power—must win
the affections of all right-thinkers. Miss Susie
Vaughan, Miss Florence Gleeson and Leslie
Victor do well in minor parts.

* * *
To-morrow (Friday) week is, so the adver-
tisement alleges, the last chance that Sydney will
have to rush headlong into the Criterion Theatre
and view “The Night of the Party.” This is dis-
tressing information, but the city hopes to bear
up with the aid of a porous plaster on the small of
the back and other carefully-seiected medical
assistance. If these things fail there will pro-
bably be nothing for it but amputation, ihe
case doesn’t seem to be one in which a second
ballot would have the slightest effect. When the
show departs it will leave behind it a kindly
recollection of the lady who dropped into the
bachelor’s establishment during the early hours
between midnight and the time when the rooster
gets out of his four-post bed and rends the silence
with the loud discourse of his beak. She does
nothing of great importance, but she does it
pleasantly, and compared with the laborious fuss
of Crosbie, the valet, she is a nice and fairly re-
poseful person.

The answer to that age-old puzzle, “Why
Men Love Women,” is still receiving a large
share of public thanks and interest at the Palace
(Sydney). The play removes the last shade of
doubt as to the accuracy of the late lamented John
Dryden’s remark that “ Love is the noblest frailty
of the mind.” No change of programme had been
announced by the Hamiltpn-Maxwell co. when
this gentle publication went to press.
* * * *
Carreno played a brilliant farewell to
Sydney at the .Town Hall last Saturday night,
after a busy afternoon with the Symphony
Orchestra. The audience was numerous and
enthusiastic, but not numerous enough for one
critic, who gravely asked if it were not a re-
flection on the artistic aspirations of the city
that there should have been a single vacant
seat at any of the Carreno concerts. The vacant-
seat trouble—or hall-baldness—has been a bit
troublesome this season ; but The Bulletin does
not think that persons who make such remarks as
the above ever stop to consider (1) the acreage of
the Town Hall, (2) the frequency of high-class
concerts, (3) the infrequency of shillings, and
(4) the last crop of colds. The writer knows
one man who had tickets for Saturday’s
feast but refrained from using them, as he had an
awful cold, and knew that, if he ventured to make
the pilgrimage to Row B, he would sneeze 45
times during Beethoven’s. Sonata in C sharp
minor, and thereby madden several hundred rapt
enthusiasts and make them say D ! There are
occasions when absence is the sincerest form of
flattery—or words to that effect.
* * * *
Sydney Tivoli has taken unto itself a new
beer-garden first part, labelled “Heidelberg”;
but no great rush of business is likely to result
from it. The acquisition of a new first part by
the Tivoli has merely a scientific interest to the
theatrical critic in his observatory ; it shows that
an immense period of time has elapsed since the
previous first part, and that another immense
period has been entered upon. The whirling
Kremo people came back on Saturday night to
fill the gap left in the programme by the depar-
ture of Juggler Morris Cronin ; their somersault-
ing return season is billed to last a fortnight.
Gracie Grahame, the packing-case of mischief, is
still available; as also are Tom Dawson, the
Quealys, Woods and Fraser, and Olga Grey.
Barnes and West, the fashion-plates, are due to
pack their beautiful rags at the end of this week,
and cease soothing the gallery with the rapid
clatter of their talented feet.
* * * *
At Her Majesty’s (Melb.) the noblest gee
in the noble marquis’s stable will keep on winning
until further orders. Its escape from destruction
by the gentle midnight express is narrower than
of yore, but the animal lives a charmed life and
is invariably taken from the horse-box in time.
This is the fifth week of “The Whip,” and it is
galloping with a machine-like action which is
beautiful to see. After Cup week the pace will
slow down, no doubt, but in the meantime some
thousands of visitors from the country will back
this good thing; and all tickets issued on the
event will be duly collected at the entrance to
J.C.W.’s grandstand.
* * * *
“ The Brass Bottle ” is leaving the Prin-
cess’s, so all persons in need of mental refresh-
ment from that strange drinking vessel must
hurry up before the Green Jinn is quite gone. On
Saturday the Arion Operatic Society will drop
into the” Princess’s and stay three nights. The
amateurs bring “ Dorothy ” with them, and their
intentions are strictly charitable, it seems. They
are by way of keeping the theatre aired in readi-
ness for J.C.W.’s new comic opera co., which
opens on Derby Night in “ A Knight for a Day.”

GREY HAIR MAKES YOUR FACE
LOOK OLD.
A young face looks old if it is surrounded with
grey, faded or dead-looking hair. Allen’s
Mexican Walnut Stain will make from 10 to 20
years difference in your appearance if you are
grey or prematurely grey. It is perfectly harm-
less, and easy to apply, and will quickly restore
the hair to the natural shade of youth or younger
years. . ,
Allen’s Mexican Walnut Stain is not to be
confounded with the common or garden hair
restorers,” which deceive nobody but the deluded
users. This unique preparation is offered as a
superior stain, and its composition has been
arrived at only after a long series of experiments.
No injurious effect will be produced, provided the
simple directions are fob owed out it is quite free
from Lead, Mercury or Sulphur. It is equally
suitable for ladies’hair or hair of the beard or
moustache, and will not wash off or stain the linen.

Ardley. Registered in the Patents Office of the
Commonwealth of Australasia, and sold by all
reputable Chemists.

*# # *
The King’s announces a change. “The
Winning Ticket ” is to be presented for the last
time on .Friday, and a drama, concocted from the
Crippen case and christened “By Wireless
Telegraphy,” will be staged on Saturday (22nd).
Friend Crippen, by the way, has not yet ceased
to be a possible claimant in libel actions, yet he is
already the hero of a Yankee drama, and now
Anderson is about to convict the world-renowned
dentist on circumstantial evidence.
** * *
The voice of Lydia Yeamans-Titus, singing
of Sally and chanting the recitative of the Baby,
has been added to the programme at the Opera
House. Also, Titus accompanies the lady, as of
old. The Newman aeroplane is another recent
arrival, and for the rest there are all, or nearly
all, the people who have been the latest attractions
in their respective turns during the past month.
Whereof Walter C. Kelly appeals specially to
them who know a gifted monologue entertainer
when they get him. There hasn’t been a Law-
yers’ Institute Night at the Opera House as
yet, but it is certainly up to the learned
profession to muster in its wigs and gowns at
Rickards’s whilst the Virginian Judge is holding
court there. De niggah lady, who talks of return-
ing to the charge with de peace of Gord in her
heart and her husband’s ear between her teeth, is
one of the choicest plaintiffs on Judge Kelly’s
list; but the Italiano banana-vendor, the wild
Irishman and the mild witness who thinks that
48 hours will be plenty are a great trio. The
three Italians in Randolph Bedford’s finest
smoking-room story pale by comparison.
** * *
The inaugural frivolity of The Follies was
heard at Taits’ Open-air Theatre, St. Kilda (Vic.),
on Saturday evening. A big house viewed the open-
ing performance, and bestowed applause on the
company, the scenery and the gentleman who
toyed with the keys of the piano. The pro-
gramme was of the usual gentle character, where
the banter wears 240 z. gloves and the sentiment
is subdued to a sigh. The meatiest item was
Henry Ross’s “ Take a Pair of Sparkling Eyes ” ;
Henry took them with the joy of a hungry mid-
nighter lifting a brace of trotters. Howard Hall
warbled “Stonecracker Jim” in a cultured bari-
tone ; Miss Grace Darley and Edwin Grainger
gave the “ Fairy Wand ” duet from “Maritana”;
and, among others, the most blessed was J. M.
Campbell, the pantomimist, who slew Gravity,
and raised a continuous Giggle over its bones.
** # *
“ The Call to Arms ” is the brightest item
in West’s (Melbourne) Catalogue of Strolling
Pictures. Digging up a bit of the Middle Ages,
it supplies a parade of loves and hates, fights and
plots, wild gallants and base emotions. _ “ His
First Contract,” which unwinds a story of
sonation, has all the elements of French frivolity ;'
while “ Over the Garden Wall,” which narrates
the beginning and end of a stale romance, is com-
mended for a certain meek simplicity. Included
in the strollers is also a beautiful picture of
Nuremberg, whose appearance of age and quiet
fills grave folk with a feeling of cloistral peace.
** * *
Sfc. Kilda (Melbourne) will add to its array
of electric lights next week. On Monday (24th)
Branscombe’s Jesters return to Arcadia for another
summer season. Some of the Jesters will be as
before, and others will be new, and the glittering
stars above will shine upon a galaxy of refined
attractions.

Needless to mention that the circus is
coming to Melbourne. In previous years Wirths’
show came with the Caulfield Cup, but this time
it begins on regatta night. The advt. stands
amazed at the convocation of arenic talent. Im-
mense attractions are breathlessly hinted at. lor
instance, Lizzie the hippopotamus will be there.
A while ago it was stated that Roosevelt had
specially captured the hippo for Wirths. but later
advices represent her as having been purchased
in Germany.

The long Adelaide beach has broken out
into its summer crop of Pierrots, at Glenelg,
Henley and the Semaphore. The weather fairly
stood on its head to welcome them. On Monday
of last week snow was lying about within 10 miles
of Adelaide; on Friday, it was 80° in the shade—
and felt hotter than that. Bert Gilbert is still
going strong at the Royal as the duke who was
too sensible to want to be King of Cadonia.
The piece is not nearly as funny as it might be.
Miss Emma Temple, for instance, has a mere
skeleton of a part; and Gilbert and Victor,
Prince and Lissant, with Miss Godwin and Miss
Sargent, mostly have to work up their own
humor, wherefore they prattle about semi-private
jokes, and enjoy themselves immensely. The
best song in the piece' is Clayton’s ‘‘intense ’
ballad, “ There’s a King in the Land To-day ;
and the finale is a real dramatic moment, instead
of the usual “ Shut up—it’s ten to eleven.
Powell and Miss Vesey are making merry at the
Tivoli; also Carl Howard, a new arrival who
mixes magic with mirth. Cronin, with his
weird assistants, is expected back at any moment.
The other Adelaide shows are either concerts or
pictures —both too numerous to name in detail.
An official epitaph has been prepared in
view of the approaching end of the Tait railway
regime in Victoria. Naturally, its authors have
set it to the music of self-approbation ; but still
they have not deprived it of a certain humor.
For instance, in talking about railway accidents
during the past seven years, the pamphlet observes
that, “apart from the Sunshine and Richmond
disasters, thenumberof fatal railway accidents for
which the Department could be held responsible
was infinitesimal.” This is really a charming way
of putting it, much better than saying, for in-
stance: “We killed a few hundred people in two
frightful smashes; but you will please credit us
with the fact that we didn’t have that kind of
disaster every day.”

“Henry Horsecollar” writes :
What should be done with the Society of Friends in
wartime? That body is opposed to war, and it has re-
cently gone so far as to declare that it will rather be put
in gaol than go in for military training and learn to de-
fend Australia in case of need. A Quaker, in short, is a
very good man who, if he sees a Chinese invader disem-
bowelling his mother, will, on principle, just let his
mother be disembowelled. So the question naturally
arises : If a Quaker thinks it wrong- to defend himself
(and incidentally his country), is it right for anybody else
to defend a Quaker? Even if he doesn’t fight, his
existence is part of the cause of the other fellow
fighting—and that seems contrary to his faith.
Perhaps the most logical idea would be, when the in-
vasion conies, to put all Quakers in the space between
the two armies, and let things sort themselves out. I
don’t know for certain how they would sort themselves
out, but I presume the Quakers would surrender to the
Japanese or Chinese or whoever the invaders were. And
then, if these persons insisted on the Quakers fighting-
well, it would, I expect, be a case for legal argument.
Town Clerk Ellery has started to worry
the Adelaide City Council to enlarge the Town
Hall. The usualS. Aus. habit has been followed
here of getting in early and putting up something
good—and then letting it stand unimproved while
slower starters gradually pass it. The Town Hall
of Adelaide is over 40 years old, and does not
now seem so enormous as it did in the 60’s. The
main hall upstairs has another smaller one behind
it, and this can be thrown into it by the simple
process of taking down the dividing wall, and
running the stage and organ back to the rear wall
of the building. The rest of the structure is
simply starved now for space, though at first it
seemed so vast that some of it was leased to a
bank. This space has long been resumed, and
still the corporation offices are clamoring for
more. The Ellery plan is simply to mop up the
neighboring Prince Alfred Hotel, which was built
to correspond with the Town Hall exterior. So
it seems likely that at the end of 1914, when the
lease falls in,' the Prince will follow the Globe to
wherever-good-hotels-go-to-when-they-die.
I 0 U Ward’s Government is having
serious trouble over the housing of Maoriland’s
new figurehead, Islington. Eirst it was proposed
to have only one official residence, the new shack
at Wellington. Government House at Auckland,
dating from the early days of Auckland s glory as
the capital, was to be closed, and the ground
handed over to the University College and
Grammar School. But such a shriek went up
from “ the neglected North,” led by the local
snobocracy, that Ward couldn’t think out excuses
for his new loan ; so he retired from the position
with as much strategy as lie could muster up on
short notice. Meantime most of the furniture had
been shipped to Wellington, including the bed
on which the Duke of Edinburgh reposed ; and
Auckland won’t be happy till it gets that bed
back. Now, to add to the upset, Islington and
his camp-followers have been obliged to flee for
their lives from the new Wellington residence on
account of tho outbreak of a mysterious sickness
among the kitchen guard. The sanitary pro-
fession of Wellington is nosing around the drains
to find out whaffor, and the harassed Ward is seri-
ously thinking of passing an Act to exclude
Governors as undesirable immigrants who cause
more trouble than they are worth.
The most amazing item for Cockney readers in J. C.
Williamson’s yarn with London Referee was the mention
of the fact that his firm sends every year a complete
panto, production and a co. of over 100 people to Maori-
land, which is five days* sail from Australia. This
must appear incredible to the Refkree public. •
Towns no bigger than Auckland, Dunedin or Wel-
lington are outside the field of the biggest theatrical
ventures in England. Their proportion of theatregoers
who can afford to pay more than sixpence or a bob is
relatively small—thanks to a long course of Freetrade
and other blessings.
AT POVERTY POINT.
“ Theorbo ” : Bosina Palmer has just signified her re-
tirement from the Melbourne concert platform. She was
the eldest of Mrs. Carandini’s five daughters, all song-
birds. It is curious that the five musical Misses Corrick
happen to be in Melbourne just when the last of the Car-
andini Five abdicates. Mrs. Carandini was an Australian
pioneer in opera, like Mrs. Guerin, afterwards Mrs.
Stewart, Nellie’s mother. Mrs. Carandini even acted
tenor parts, like Fra Diavolo. Her husband, Marchese
Carandini, was an expatriated Italian patriot, but she
was English. The daughters were Bosina, Fannie, Lizzie,
Tessie (or Temmie) and another. Lizzie married Cot-
terell, the entertainer. They formed an efficient troupe,
under the direction of Sherwin —a tinpotty tenor, but
a capital business man. He lost an arm in shooting,
and tenor Beaumont lost an eye in the same manner.
Sherwin sang with a wooden arm and hand, and people
were not the wiser. Few Australian benefits have been
so lucrative as that given to the Carandinis in Sydney,
under the Duke of Edinburgh’s auspices. Eliot Yorke
appeared in a scene from “Bichelieu,” and Edinburgh
made him up in the dressing-room. Bosina Palmer says
Edinburgh was more than pretty good as a violinist.
Santley and others expressed surprise that she did not go
to Europe, but her plea was the care of her large and
small family. She has been much the best vocalist of all
the Carandinis. Her father was musical and a splendid
dancer, appearing as harlequin in the first Melbourne
Theatre Boyal Christmas pantomime, 1855.
“ Sceneshifter ” : “Tom Jones,” like most modern
comic operas, lifts the baritone to the place once occu-
pied by the tenor. The tenor once had all the “ sweet
stuff”; now he shares it, and generally gets the
worse half. And sometimes he does not even get
a chance to share. In the days of “Les Cloches
de Corneville,” “Paul Jones,” “Dorothy,” “Bip Van
Winkle” and “La Mascotte,” he disputed to a
certain extent the honors with the baritone. With
the advent of Slaughter’s “Marjorie,” in 1890, he
surrendered. In “ Marjorie ” (which would bear revival
nowadays) the baritone—a dashing Earl, one of Charlie
Bvley’s r6les in Australia—has all the acting and nearly
all the singing. The tenor here degenerates into a love-
sick puppet. Later still, he was summarily dropped
altogether, as in “ The Arcadians,” “ Miss Hook of Hol-
land,” “ Tom Jones,” “ Floradora,” “ Country Girl,” etc.
I know this theory has been upset in cases like “ The
Little Michus,” “ Ma Mie Bosette,” “The Duchess of
Dantzic," “The Lady Dandies” and “ A Waltz Dream,”
where the tenor came into his own again to some extent;
but, all the same, the tendency is to promote the baritone
into the boss rdle.
“ 0.K.”: Strange that Bill Anderson doesn’t adver-
tise his King’s Theatre as an Australian factory
rather than as “ the home ”of melodrama. Anderson is
not the first regular producer of plays made in Australia;
George Darrell, more than a quarter of a century ago,
followed up his success with “ The Sunny South ” by
staging a number of his own plays. But the industrious
George was not a permanent manager with a theatre to
his name. Anderson was the first recognised im-
porter of melodrama goods to start, as it were,
a factory in connection with the warehouse. The
new industry began with “The Squatter’s Daughter,”
by Bert Bailey and Ned Duggan, who have made thou-
sands apiece out of their authors' rights. Anderson
next produced Ambrose Pratt’s adaptation of his own
“ Thunderbolt.” Then Bailey and Duggan interrupted
the course of imported bellowdramas with another joint
effort. Next came Australian plays by Bandolph Bed-
ford and Tom Smith, and after a further lapse into the
import trade, Anderson broke out again with an Aus-
tralian hippodrama constructed by a third mummer of
the co. (Temple Harrison), after designs by Anderson.
Now, having got fairly going as his own part-author, in
collaboration with a man on the premises, Anderson is
about to stage a shocker constructed by his foreman
mummer, Boy Bedgrave. Obviously Anderson is in a fair
way to increase his business by attracting a superior class
of customers with the “ Made in Australia” ticket, whilst
gradually improving the taste of his unsophisticated
supporters who have been suckled on British beerdrama
and weaned on local “ tanglefoot.” He couldn’t possibly
create a payable demand for local literary drama at
present; but he has already got the sympathy of people
who will follow the Australian-made drama, crude
though it be, if the main body of the Andersonian
clientele will permit it to advance slowly. Already the old
habitues of the King’s, mostly young in years, have
abated their demands for gore. “ The Winning Ticket ”
has been running for some weeks without a murder in
it, and if the plot is preposterous, the dialogue
is at least reasonable. Also, it has been some-
what improvingly elaborated during the run, which is
what never happens to an imported bellow-drama. The
state of things is progressive. If every member of the
co. carries an author’s baton in his knapsack, Anderson
should be able to recruit young talent that gets no
chance of making a rapid rise at houses where the plays
are always imported and “complete companies” are
often imported with them. An apprentice in the Aus-
tralian drama-factory can hope to get his invention
taken up by the boss, and may dream of a not distant
day when his thrilling story of domestic interest (not
necessarily local) will be “staged under the personal
direction of the author.”
“Stargazer”: Artistically, Anstey scores a miss with
“The Brass Bottle.” The effort fails in conviction. It
is not satisfactory as farce, nor does it fill the gap from
the point of view of the actual. The supposedly magical
transformation in Act 1 is only a very commonplace
stage effect, after all, and is worked nightly in many
plays that make no pretence of starring miracles. The
trouble is that with a farcical idea that has practically
no limits, Anstey does not succeed in being humanly
comic; the joke is not well sustained, and succumbs
before the last act is reached. Beside “Niobe,” for
instance, where the coming to life of a marble maiden
fills the house of Peter Amos with woe and worry, “ The
Brass Bottle ” is a thin jest, the characterisation in-
different, and the complications wanting in ingenuity
and vital concern. Compare “Vice Versa” with “The
Brass Bottle ” to discover the demoralising effect of Lon-
don Punch on a man of fancy.
George Rignold completed his 71st year on October 18.
He has a picturesque home far up Middle Harbor, from
which he makes only very rare descents upon the city.
Sarah Bernhardt will be 66 this next Saturday.
Hall Caine, who has the keenest nose in Great Britain
(with the possible exception of W. T. Stead’s) for an
advt., has struck a snag in his booming of Tittell
Brune. He was not satisfied with giving his own opinion,
but dragged in the late Clement Scott, and deplored that
he was not present at the first performance ; in that
case, says Caine gratuitously, the lady would by this
time be the most discussed woman on the English stage.
Then Clement’s widow took an unexpected hand, writing
to the papers to protest against opinions being fathered
on to a man who “ no longer has the power to repudiate
them. But H.C. and T.B. and the play get the advt. all
the same.
“Cockaigne”: Beerbohm-Tree’s sumptuous Henry
VIII. is lucky, chiming in with the gabble about that
molluscous Alfonso proving another Bluff King Hal. It
may bear transplanting to Australia, for the props, as
was done with Irving’s “ Romeo and Juliet,” when Cates
acted Romeo. The ecclesiastical interest is strong, as
v, ith “ The Huguenots ” and “ Count Hannibal.” Literary
circles are divided as to whether Shakspeare wrote
“ Henry VIII.” Most of the blank verse is devoid of his
masterly stamp—merely prose cut into swathes. Note
how awkwardly many lines end. Yet the master-hand
is upon the great part of the drama. Fletcher is
credited with some, and even with the whole, which is
preposterous. Shakspeare may have been hurried into
collaboration, as Corneille helped Moliferewith “Psyche.”
Another question is, When was “Henry VIII.” produced?
Critics divide again. Some say under Elizabeth, others
under James. Perhaps under Elizabeth, and then touched
up towards the close for James. The Globe theatre was
burnt down, while Shakspeare lived, during the per-
formance of a plav about Henrv VIII. ; but that one was
called, “All is true.” Doubtful whether Shakspeare
was a loser; he doesn’t seem to have been hit hard by
any financial disaster. Henry Irving, sen., declares that
Shakspeare wns never a manager—only an actor with a
large share in the Globe.

“Jean S.”: “The Chocolate Soldier,” founded on
Shaw’s “Arms and the Man,” is said to retain little of
the original atmosphere. The German adapters have
not even stuck to Shaw’s names or the number of his
cast. In “ Arms and The Man ” there are princi-
pals-Bluntschli, Kama, Catherine Petko ff , Paul Petkoff,
Nicola, Louka and Sergius Saranoff. In ‘ The Chocolate
Soldier” there are 15, all disguised. The art of attaching
“ off” to a sneeze and “ ka ” to a snort has been employed
to christen these illusory personages. Among the gentle-
men are Casimir Popoff, Alexis Sparidoff and Massakroff ,
and among the ladies are Laska, Mannska and the in-
evitable Katinka. Beneath these names the indentities
of Bluntschli, Ra'ina, etc., are as effectually concealed as
Bernard Shaw’s connection with the whole composition ,
for when “The Soldier” was run up at the Lyric
Theatre, London, it was coolly described as ‘an Englished
version of Bernauer and Jacobson’s parody of a well-
known comedy.”
“L.H.P.” : This upheaval in Portugal has set me count-
ing the plays and operas that have their scenic home
thereabouts. Spain, France and Italy, with Germany,
Turkey and Switzerland as good seconds, are used over
and over again ; but with a fairly good nodding acquaint-
ance with opera and drama, I can only recall two in-
stances of a Portuguese setting, and both of them are in
the musical line. Meyerbeer’s “ L’Afncaine (The Afr i-
can), with its absurd plot but glorious music, opens in
the Royal Palace at Lisbon, makes the prison there do
duty for Act 11., and then shifts the incidents to Vasco
de Gama’s ship at sea and Madagascar. In the old Lyster
days “ L’Africaine ” created a boom with a double cast—
Henry Squires and Lucy Escott in the leads one night,
and then the Australian tenor, Armes Beaumont and
Fanny Simonsen. As the representative of the Portu-
guese navigator who found out the Cape route to India,
the Australian is at least as good as the imported man.
“ The Crown Diamonds,” one of Auber’s charming comic
operas, is laid entirely, throughout its sparkling three
acts at Lisbon or thereabouts ; and this fine old-time
favorite figured both in the Lyster repertoire, and in the
shows subsequently controlled by Martin Simonsen.
There seems to be a wide field open for the librettist of
musical comedy, for Portugal, as far as the stage goes, is
an undiscovered country.
“ Vulcan” : Fired, apparently, bv the recent triumphal
progress of our other Nell-she of the nightingale throat
—“ Sweet Nell ” Stewart purposes, after her return from
Maoriland, making a tour of “ Outback ” N.S. W. with the
“Old Drury” drama. Where Melba can profitably go,
argues the other Nell, she can go ; and the bucolic clamor
for inclusion “in the ports of call ” promises encourag-
ingly. Nothing so comprehensive has been attempted
before, and the experiment, successful or not, will be a
mighty expensive one. Melba, travelling “ light ’’ with a
co. of half a dozen, is no criterion for “ Sweet Nell ’ with
a co. of over 30, an orchestra and scenery—not to mention
the spaniel.

strolls in a limelight hitherto unexploited by matin6e
idols. He and wifey take the pram along with baby gurg-
ling among the fripperies. It is usually papa who wheels
the chariot, too. —“ Melbourne Chatter," 8., %91'Jj'lO.
None but the brave deserve the ad.
That he so nobly earns
Who plays the good old r6le of “ Dad ”
A mummer bold, who yearns
To do away with stagey sham,
And on the block to push the pram !
He has a wife. He will not pose
As single—a pretence
Sometimes indulged in by male pros.
To please an audience
Who like their hero to be “ free ”;
He strolls round with his familee !
The “ limelight ” and the “ centre ” he,
As hubby, seems to scorn ;
The push known as “ Society”
He leaves alone, forlorn.
The only “ push ” he cares a d—n
About is pushing baby’s pram 1
Who knows, a fashion he may set !
All married mummers soon
May with their wives be often met;
And, ev’ry afternoon, (
Of footlight favorites, a flock,
We’ll see pram-pushing on the Block !

SOCIETY
PENFOLD'S
WINES
One of the cheerful happenings of last
week was a furious letter to Sydney Telegraph
signed “Insurance Underwriter.” At the Angli-
can General Synod on the previous day, Arch-
bishop Clarke, of Melbourne, had moved
that the Church do its own under-
writing, as no one in this peaceable
country wanted to burn down church
buildings, and the companies that
got the business made large profits
out of it. This Socialistic move
maddened one of the Private En-
terprise faithful, and he spake
heatedly as follows to Editor
Ward : “ The moment the Church
takes up fire insurance, I withdraw
the whole of my subscriptions. I
will cease to pay rent for mv pew
at church, and I will decline to
subscribe to any offertory.” It
will be noticed that the blazing
saint does not say he intends to
cease churchgoing; he merely
threatens to discontinue paying
the voluntary premium to keep
alive his insurance against the
risk of Sheol, whilst hanging on
to the policy with both hands.
It would be interesting to obtain
the opinion of (say) Peter on the
point. Is a man who gets his
living by mortal fire-insurance, and
who insists on insurers paying
their premiums regularly, entitled
to participate in the benefits of
immortal ditto, when he has given
formal notice in writing to the
editor of a great daily that, after
a certain date, he will cease to send
his quarterly cheque along to the
Vicar or Resident Secretary? If
Peter declines to express an
opinion, perhaps Aged Nicholas
might be persuaded to give a ruling.
Senator ’Siah Symon, who got up a.shilling
sweep or someth ingsuch to buy afew gold plates and
dishes for the officers of H.M.S. Commonwealth,
recently sent an invitation to the battleship to
come to Australia and take delivery of the
goods. Symon’s idea was apparently to present
a plate, say, in Sydney, a dish in Melbourne, and
a mug in Hobart, and thus spin the thing out and
make the business talked about. However, the
Admiralty has informed him that it can’t spare
the warship for six months, owing to the uncer-
tainty of Kaiser Bill’s relations with Providence.
Further, the trip would cost £3OOO for coal alone,
and £3OOO is not easy to get in a Freetrade coun-
try where one of the staple occupations is hunting
for a job. So Josiah has been advised to send the
crockery parcel by post, and make the. most of
the receipt when it arrives. Which seems a vague
and unsatisfactory reward.
The Karachi (Indian) Argus (7/9/T0) thus
describes the murder of Mrs. Dost Mahomed (the
white Australian woman who married a nigger)
in a bungalow at the native village at Malir:—
The murder occurred after midnight apparently, in
the small hours of Sunday morning, in darkness. There
seems to have been a desperate struggle. The deceased
was somewhat powerfully built, and it is alleged that
she bit off part of the band of one of the assailants, a
Brahui, one Dosta, whose whereabouts have not yet been
traced. There were many wounds; an eye-witness
informs us he counted II deep stabs, made forcibly with
a heavy, sharp instrument, in one case a rib having been
severed with the force of the blow ; altogether a clumsy,
brutal murder. All the wounds were low down, most on
the left side, well below the heart, as if that had been
aimed at. Probably none of the wounds were imme-
diately fatal. A girl child sleeping with the mother was
cut over the knee by the same knife that killed the
mother. The other children were sleeping on another
bed close at hand. Two men, it is alleged, afterwards
made their escape into the jungle through the unfastened
door.
All of which The Bulletin prints for the encour-
agement of any other white woman who may
think of becoming the mother of a loathly Asia-
tic’s half-caste brats.
* ■ * * *
“ Leno ” : If Foster Fraser could be
dumped down in Wonthaggi, the baby town be-
side the Victorian State coal mine, his idea of the
Australian’s lack of energy, etc., would get a
bump. Although only about four months old, the
progress made in building the town is marvellous.
Wonthaggi has about 3000 inhabitants, the busi-
ness portion is lined with shops, the residential
part contains about 200 fairly well-built cottages,
and new places are going up every day. There
are three newspapers, four churches, a skating
rink and a theatre. But there are no hotels. A
large two-storeyed brick building nearing comple-
tion is labelled “Hostel,” and the owners are
hoping to obtain for it a “ club license.” For
water the Government has temporarily laid pipes
along the surface ; for beer the holder of a two-
gallon license delivers the liquor per dray. Coal
mining is a thirsty game ; and as the beer cart
can’t call often enough, there has been a great crop
of sly-grog shops at Wonthaggi. These shops are
known to the initiated as “The Blue Pig,” “The
Little Pig,” “The White Pig,” etc. Always
the name has “Pig” in it. The police spies,
working as miners, have been kept busy, and in
one week five “Pigs” were turned into pork.
’When the five were captured they immediately
drew up a circular letter to all the business people
of the town, asking them to organise a fund for
their defence, which looked like a delicate way of
suggesting that the captives were not the only
ones in the sly-grog business. N. S. Wales
needn’t have gone so far as Maoriland to learn
what happens when there isn’t a licensed pub in
the neighborhood.

HE: “I believe you are a man-hater, Miss
Hobble? ”
SHE: “ Oh, clear no! I like men. They make

“The Pariah”: The Lone Hand con-
troversy on “Are Australians Disliked?” re-
minds me of what happened one evening at the
Y.M.C.A., Hongkong (snooker was the attrac-
tion). Manderson, a Victorian, was fluting con-
siderably in praise of Australia, when the head of
the Postal Department wanted to know why so
many Australians were on the China coast if Aus-
tralia was such a wonder. Manderson had no
answer ready, but a one-armed Australian cut in
with, “Well, up here we’ve only you fellows to
rustle with, so it’s easier to get to the top.” We
didn’t call the one-armed man a liar, but we
told him afterwards that he might have put it
differently.

“• Woodburn ” wants information :
Three months ago I arrived here from England. To
get a knowledge of things generally, I have been reading
the Sydney papers at the local School of Billiards. As I
am greatly perplexed over some matters, I appeal
to you. The dailies (including one that wanders
forth on Sundays) say that the Commonwealth
Government is going to make this country im-
possible to live in, first by its note issue, second
by its land-tax, third by high Protection. In other
columns they give accounts of Australia’s unprepared-
ness for war, and they state that a fleet off the coast and
battalions ashore are an immediate necessity ; also the
transcontinental railway should be built, and it should
wander around the country a good deal before it lands any-
where. Now this is where I’m fogged. They don’t say
where the money is to come from, yet every time the
Government reaches out for money they cry “ Ruin ! ” I
thought probably the Lord Mayor’in time of peril might
call a meeting to raise a war fund, and that this was the
before-mentioned papers’ idea. But on telling Bill he
laughed and said “ Dreadnoughts.” What did lie mean ?

The underwriters lately dropped a memo,
to the Victorian Marine Board explaining that,
though their business was to take
risks, they had bad dreams whenever
they thought of the lighters that lum-
ber in and out of Melbourne port.
These arks are nearly all worn-out
wind-jammers, their ages ranging from
40 to 1)6 years. No vigorous barnacle
attaches himself to one without run-
ning the risk of holing right through ;
and when they go to Geelong or any-
where out of sight of Melbourne, they
meander cautiously along the coast so
that the bargee and his family may
have a chance to wade ashore. Yet
these old coffins frequently carry car-
goes wo: tli £lO,OOO or £i5,000. The
underwr ters said that their rates
didn’t p ovide for this sort of risk,
and tb y wanted the tubs inspected
occasii nally. They had already ap-
plied to the Harbor Trust, which had
disowned all responsibility; and now
the Marine Board has decided that
it has no supervisory power either.
Until the Federal Navigation Bill is
passed, the coffins, therefore, seem to
lie beyond the law, which is a very
bad place for any coffin to get into, and worse for
the people who happen to be occupying the coffin.
* * * *
“F.C.”: The Vic. railway bosses may
not keep the fourth commandment themselves,
but they at least discourage others from breaking
it by charging 50 per cent, extra freight on all
milk carried per train on the Sabbath. As the
cow persists in yielding her juice on that day, and
as the average infant demands the same nourish-
ment on Sundays as on week days, and as Satur-
day’s milk has the same bad habit of going mouldy
as any other day’s milk, the Sabbath fluid simply
has to be railed to the city—which is possibly why
the extra rate was put on. So far, there has been
no insinuation that the drivers and porters’
wages have been raised to correspond.
After a three years’ trial the Vic. Govern-
ment has decided that the making of wire-netting
in Pentridge prison is a failure, and that the stuff
costs more than it is worth, which conclusion is a
caustic comment on the management of the in-
dustry and the system generally. The goods sold
readily at market rates; but they cost 10s. per
mile above those rates to produce, and, owing to
the recent rise in spelter, they promise to cost
235, per mile more. The department excuses its
failure by saying that the “labor’’ is unskilled
and intermittent; but as it has no minimum wage
to pay, and can work its men 12 hours a day if
they refuse to do a fair thing in eight, it looks
very much as if some one was too weary to bog
into the business and run it enthusiastically. It
is so much easier to set a man to scrub one small
floor or wash down two yards of paint in six hours
and lock him in a cell for the rest of the 24. But
it isn’t good for the man himself, or for the State
that has to keep him, or, in fact, for anybody.

BROTHERS—BUT NOT BROTHERS-IN-LAW.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
Colonel Onslow said tliat, although the Japanese had a
different civilisation, they were just as good as we were.
“ How would you like one for a brother-in-law?” the
candidate was asked.
“That has nothing at all to do with the question ” was
t he answer.” —Daily paper.
I simply love the gentle Jap ;
Ilis morals I revere ;
In brain and grit and character
lie is without a peer.
And yet, I’d rather die than see
Him wed into my familee.
Ilis prowess on the tented field
Inspires one with contempt
For mere white trash ; he from our fear
Of Death is quite exempt.
Withal, I would by no means care
A battle-tent with him to share.
His manners are above reproach,
Their sheer perfection makes
The crude, rough Briton blush with shame ;
And hopeless envy wakes
In folks wlio’d see him hanged before
They let him pass their club-house door.
Hail to this human Paradox
Whom Jingoes love, yet shun,
Who’s known as “ friend ” till he attempts
To act the part of one !
Afar, he is a Superman ;
Close to, an Outcast, ’neath a ban.

309-311 GEORGE STREET, SYDNEY.
The Victorian Premier should now repent
somewhat of the stupendous verbal testimonial
which he gave to Railway Commissioner Tait
when that eminent cigar-conflagration threw up
bis job. The case of signalman McDonald, who
was proceeded against on account of the Rich-
mond wreck, has fallen down dead on the front
step of the first court it got to. And the reason
of the corpse was that McDonald, in the magis-
trate’s view, had been guided by such a vague
and incomplete regulation that, even if his read-
ing of it did bring about the great smash, which
was by no means certain, it was impossible to say
uiat he had broken or departed from the article.
Ihemagistrntelookedatitfrontways, backwaysand
sideways, also endways, and his opinion was that
if he had been signalling under the same circum-
stances he might have done what McDonald did
or he might not, and either way he wouldn’t be
very clear about his reasons. ”It didn’t seem to
be the sort of regulation that nailed a man tightly
down to anything; rather it left him to his own
judgment, so that whatever he did would be the
wrong thing if it happened to turn out wrong,
whereas if it turned out right it would go to prove
the excellence of the blessed regulation. So
either there is a very hazy magistrate for whose
appointment someone is responsible, or there
is a very hazy regulation, and somebody
made it, and somebody appointed the man
who made it. These things being in the
balance it would have been wiser of Weary if he
had postponed his eulogium of T. Tait. The
position seems to be that that gentleman devised
an almost perfect railway system with a dreadful
tendency to accidents, which is suggestive of the
case of the rigidly honest man who stole a horse.
* * -r *
There doesn’t seem to be any sting in
revolutions these days. The cables kick up many
columns of dust; but the excitement slumps
badly when one reads that the bombardment, the
street fighting and the rioting, all combined,
resulted in a casualty list about as big as the one
the ambulance people compile after a king’s
funeral or some other free, overcrowded spectacle
to which infirm people rush at an excessive speed.
The Portuguese disturbance boils down to a
certain amount of flag-trampling of the Mafeking
variety, three dozen speeches, salutes by the war-
ships, frantic cheers, the flight of Manoel with
the photo of his actress and other State papers in
his pocket, a full-uniform visit of the British
Consul to the Republican leaders at the Town
Hall, and a little fighting—followed, when the
revolution was all over, by the usual bad time for
the monasteries. The promptness of the British
Minister looks as if he had sat up all night, wait-
ing for the performance to begin. It must have
been a long, dreary evening ; most likely lie put
in the time reading an old newspaper, with occa-
sional interruptions to ask some wayfarer, from
the second-floor window, if the upheaval was on
yet. His only anxiety, when the firing had died
down and the cheering had started, would be to
find out whether he had to drive to the palace
and congratulate the king, or hurry to the Town
Hall and warn a revolutionary in a plug hat that
capital would fly the country unless he went slow.
Portugul has, for the present anyhow, got rid of its
monarch by painless extraction. ” A little blood was
lost; but you can’t avoid that in these operations.
As a spectacle, it was a failure ; there were too
many belltoppers in proportion to bombs.

“ L.” : The other day I was in the casualty
room of the Melbourne Hospital for five minutes,
and in that time saw two men carted away to the
ward. Looking at the book of entries, I learned
these facts: —“John Smith, wharf-laborer, con-
cussion ; fracture (?) ; struck by falling timber.
William Jones, laborer, broken leg ; fell 20ft.
from scaffolding.” Heard also that these fatted
gentry earned about 425. a week. Then I went
on my rounds and heard a Tory orator say that
the Labor party was greedy, grasping and tyran-
nical. ’ I was properly alarmed. lam amazed at
the brutal selfishness of John Smith with the con-
cussed head, and William Jones with the frac-
tured femur. Is their
arrogant rapacity to
receive no check
from the champions
of human liberty ?

“ S.S.” : So far as I have seen, not a single
Melbourne paper has given attention to the
Mackennal memorial to Robert Brough, presented
by Brough’s widow to the Vic. National Gallery.
This handsome gift cost £5OOO, and is worth it.
The,,.memorial is not grand, but it is exquisite.
The figures of Comedy and Tragedy are two-
thirds life size, and have a cameo grace and mar-
vellous refinement. Tragedy in particular is a
striking figure. The delicacy of it is almost
flower-like ; but, despite that, there is profundity
in face and pose. Beauty and grief are blended
here as only genius can blend them.
* * * *
Prahran, which prides itself on being the
“second city of Victoria,” has decided to throw
up the pretence of being civilised ; at least, that
is the effect of its latest decision. For many years
a bridge has connected Chapel-street, Pr’an, with
Church-street, Richmond ; but, like other “per-
manent and reproductive” works, it refused to
lust for ever, and latterly several engineers have
told both councils that the contraption is liable to
fall into the Yarra at any moment. Having
naturally made no pro-
vision for a new
bridge, both coun-
cils now declare
that it is a “na-
tional” work, and
want the Govern-
ment to build one ;
but Weary and
Watt, being pretty
hard up, say “No.”
So P’ran, fearing
actions for compen-
sation and damages,
has decided to close
the bridge, and has
told the citizens, in so many words, to
swim across. It is, of course, a blustering effort
to force the hands of the Government; but, can
anyone say what councils like that of P’ran exist
for?
* * * *
The penal system of Victoria isn’t exactly
a success as a reformer; but the prisoner who
dodges any extra punishment which the system
thinks is coming to him has to be pretty fly. The
other day two men employed in the bakehouse
were noticed to be adorned with black eyes,
swollen noses and other evidences of a strenuous
encounter. Fighting in gaol is, of course, a
heinous offence; but as both men assured theofficeis
that they had sustained their damages through
falling over the wood heap, and as there were r.o
witnesses to the contrary, they made sure of escap-
ing further phnishment. But they didn’t know
the System. They were promptly charged with
“having their faces marked and not reporting
same to the officer in charge,’’and were handed
an extra seven days’ apiece.
While the Yic. Employers’ Federation is
girding at the promised provision of plain gaol
for “reputable” law-breakers, individual em-
ployers, like dairyman Vanderbilt, of South
Melbourne, are doing their best to prove the
necessity for the cold-cell treatment. Sundry
milk inspectors lately called at Vanderbilt’s dairy,
and among a dozen other milk cans observed one
containing something that shrieked for notice.
It proved to have been a can of frozen milk, but
that was a long while ago; now', in its ripe old
age it “wasin a rotten state and emitted a most
offensive smell.” The inspectors were kindly, and
urged Vanderbilt to throw it away ; but lie was a
careful man —“he wasn’t going to lose it; he
would sell it to a baker for scones.” His thrifti-
ness cost him £l5; and whether the Employers’
Federation thinks the fine sufficient, or whether
it fancies that such a case calls for gaol, depends
probably on whether the Employers’ Federation
is in the habit of eating shop scones or not.
* * * *
A correspondent of Sydney Telegraph
sprang off his tail last Monday'with a proposal
that “ our great Premier Wade ” should be “im-
mortalised ” by having his oil-painting hung in
the Sydney Art Gallery per medium of a bob sub-
scription. But the scragging of a portrait in an
unfrequented apartment is likely to bring about
a very temporary form of immortality. Why not
do the thing properly with a heroic figure of
Wade at the entrance to Sydney harbor—a figure
75ft. high, holding up a light with one hand and
holding its nose with the other ? The great work
might be hollow, and inside it could be stored
files of the Tory papers covering the period
of Wade’s premiership. Parliamentary docu-
ments, etc., these priceless archives being
accessible by a fireproof door in the stomach
of the image. Then Wade Would have a certain
amount of immortality. When strangers entered
the Heads they would ask, with curiosity, what
the amazing thing was; and the steward, the
cook, the slushy, the deck-sweep and the coal-
passer would reply frankly that they didn’t know.
The Westralian Government, having no
other accommodation, proposes to house immi-
grants at the Perth police-station. The, idea
recalls the action of S. Aus. Governor Gawler in
1839. There had been a rush of immigrants, and
many were in a sore way ; so they went in a body
to Government House. Gawler looked them over
and then put them to work building a prison.
The place still does duty as the Adelaide Gaol.
* * * *
They are digging for Ethel Harris in the
backyard over in Perth (W. A.), but, up to time of
writing, the lady has eluded all search. The story,
so far as it has gone, presents a curious resem-
blance to the Hawley Crippen narrative. Ethel dis-
appeared the same as Mrs. Crippen. She was said
to have gone to Mount Gambier, just as the other
lady went to California. Her ring is alleged
to have been seen in the possession of Sarah
Jane, whom the man in the case promptly mar-
ried, just as Mrs. Crippen’s gems were seen
decorating the person of Ethel with whom the
man in the case didn’t bolt promptly enough.
And in each instance the police started dig-
ging. Last Monday’s Sydney Evening News (the
sheet that specialises in details concerning the
appearance of the remains) announced the un-
earthment by the Force of ruined garments
in the yard where Ethel once suspended her
washing. When the bobby intelligence be-
gins to suspect that a domestic tragedy
has happened, it always proceeds to dig
in (1) the cellar, (2) the yard, (3) under the
hearthstone. It never by any chance looks on the
roof for the remains. Yet if a murderer happened
to own a very tall house on a hill, he could hardly
do better than moor the remnant of his victim
securely on the slates and await events. Aero-
planes and balloons are an almost infinitesimal
danger at present, and only an extraordinary cop
would start digging the upper atmosphere for a
problematical cadaver.
WE DO NOT KNOW.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
new parson erupted in Scots’ Church, Melbourne,
the other day, and was duly press-boomed. And this is
the porridge he served out to his Presbyterians : “ What
was Christ like ? We do not know his height, his build,
the color of his eyes, the color of his hair (except the
tradition that it was red), the sound of his voice, whether
tenor, bass, or baritone,” etc. Quite a long sermon could
be preached on these lines.
We do not know what hue were Adam's eyes,
Or what was Eve’s circumf. around the calf,
Or where Ulysses learned to advertise,
Or who provided Pegasus with chaff ;
And whether Homer wore a No. Nine
In sandals, or was proud of trilbies small;
These are important facts we can’t divine
At all.
Regarding famous folk of later days—
Joe Chamberlain, John Wren or Julius Knight—
We re well equipped with knowledge ; know their ways,
Their debts, their tailors ; what they sup and bite ;
How oft their barber gets Ins due reward,
Their favorite pastime, poet, novel, play ;
These things the Woman’s Journal doth record
Each day.
But we are ignorant what shape of nose
Adorned the visage of the Nazarene,
Nor are we told where Xerxes bought his elo’es,
Noah his wines, or Job his kerosene.
These questions do for parsons here and there
To fill their void with oratorio flam ;
And yet we do not know, nor do we care
A damn.
VICTORIA.
L.
THE WOWSERS ARE DOWN AND OUT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Oh ! fill me a glass of the Riverine !
And sit on your lover, my sinful queen ;
We’ll drink to the days that have always been
The wowsers are down and out 1
Without a fear of a “ warning,” love,
We’ll have a swim in the morning, love,
Where the rollers break in their scorning, love—
The wowsers are down and out.
We’ll sun-bathe there till the noon, and go
In the afternoon to a sinful show.
With wine to follow, and joy shall flow—
The wowsers are down and out.
We’ll moan no more for the rarity
Of Christian (or heathen) charity ;
The Truth has come in its clarity—
The wowsers are down and out.
We’ll breathe with freedom on Sunday now,
While laughing at old Mrs. Grundy now—
Oh, there shall at least be one day now
The wowsers are down and out.
N.S.W.
HENRY LAWSON.

ABORIGIN-
ALITIES
A PESSIMIST.
FIRST DIRTY GENTLEMAN : “ Why not try the
back, mate?
SECOND DITTO: “ Wot’s the use! Whenever yer
arsks for bread they points to the axe.”
“The Scapegoat ” ; If a referendum were
taken of bird-lovers as to the friendliest bird in
•the bush, I would plump unhesitatingly for the
thrush (Australian mavis). His coat cannot com-
pare in brilliance with that of some other
feathered aboriginals, but he is a sleek, well-
groomed rogue, and his clear, bell-like note is
unsurpassed for purity of tone. A pair has been
housekeeping with me for the past three years.
The male bird arrives regularly towards the end
of the winter, and having made sure that last
year’s domicile is habitable, flies away, returning
early in the spring with his bride. He and I are
on the most friendly terms, and he has no hesita-
tion in flying through the’window to pick crumbs
from the' table. I don’t know the lady very well,
principally because she’s not the same lady more
than once a year.
SUPEREBOGATION.
FIRST NOBLE DOSSER: “ Those fellers lays in ther sun ter get brown!”
SECOND DITTO: “ Yers; but whatter they wanter git wet fer?”
“ Snakebite ” : This alleged toughness of
the Australian black is all rot. A nigger so
quickly recovers from a bad gash or wound simply
because he does not know its danger. If he gets
it into his head that he is going to die, even from
a slight injury, die he will, and in a very short
time. My mate and I had Tommy working for
us in the Territory. One day Tommy got a bit of
a rip on the chest from a snag in the river. It
looked nasty, but it was really a very trifling
damage. As we fixed up the cut, my brother,
who is the humorous ass of the family, brayed in
jest: “My word. Tommy, you bin settled
this time.” Next morning we found Tommy
dead in his camp. He just died because he
thought he was going to. Be the nigger
in “Ossian’s” par. (B. 21/7/’10) who got
the spear wound right through his body, at
Pinkenba (Q.). This may or may not have hap-
pened ; but something similar certainly did hap-
pen lately to a white man. During the building
of Sandover’s, in Hay-street, Perth, a crowbar
dropped from the top storey and struck a man
who was working lower down. The sharp end hit
him just above the collar-bone, went through his
body, and came out of his back just below the
waist. Of course, they had to pull it through.
The bar missed all the vital organs, and the man
is again doing his eight hours a day.
* * * *
“J. 5.8.”: “E.S.S.” (B. 29/9/10) says
there should be good money in a reliable wet-
sheep tester. True bill—if he will invent one
which can be relied upon to prove that wool is
dry when it’s wet. In 1896 the owners of Narro
mine (N.S.W.) used the Austral
Wool Tester, and it was so reliable
that at the end of the shearingseason
it was given an hour’s notice to quit.
The test most shearers rely on is the
appearance of the comb after a few
sheep have been shorn. In the case
of wet sheep, if the machine is run-
ning cool, no yolk collects on the
back of the comb; but if running
hot the yolk gathers in large quanti-

wet sheep are generally not whether
they are too wet to shear, but whether
they are dry enough, and a good many

dry sheep in the shed out among the

ran a shearing contract at Kama-

ceived in the same manner as the rep.
“E.S.S.” mentions. On the Culgoa
(N.S.W.), in ’9l, I also saw a team
of shearers refuse to shear a mob of
sheep on the ground that they were
wet, after a month’s fine weather
with the mercury at anything from
110° up. The boss was furiously derisive ; yet
when those sheep came back into the pens they
were wet —for the rousies had run them through a
swamp by way of making a short cut.

receipt of a 2d. stamp, some asbestos packing

“Werrimee” describes the uncivilised
duck:—
The whistler and the nowee (plained whistler) are the
only ducks that habitually settle in trees. They are
good game for the gunner. Many of them can be dropped
with one barrel; and, even with dead mates as evidence
of the unhealthiness of the neighborhood, others will
circle about the shooter. They have not the wing-power
of other ducks; neither are they so good eating; but
they are good swimmers and divers. Their general color
is brownish black, marked with buff; breast buff,
barred with black; chin, flanks and under tail,
white ; wing coverts and abdomen, chestnut.
Both whistler and nowee make their nests in
grass and herbage, though the whistler lady occasion-
ally housekeeps in a tree, laying 10 to 12 eggs. The
nowee is greyish-brown, darkening on the rump; sandy
red on the wings. A band of the same color crosses the
whitish abdomen, while a band of buff crosses the
brownish-black tail. The whistlers are the easiest of all
ducks to kill; the musk duck is the hardest, with the
gnaroo (wood duck) a close second. The last-named may
often be seen perched in trees, but mostly on low limbs
over water. It nests in tree-spouts, laying eight to a
dozen eggs. The white-quilled pygmy goose, or goose
teai (Q. and N.S.W.), also nests in tree-spouts, and like
its ally, the loonbi (green pygmy goose), perches in trees.
The general color of the loonbi is green, with white neck
and abdomen. The total length is a little more than a
foot. The other is much like it, but has white pinions
and freckled flanks.
“Leo Lear”: Where did the scientific
people obtain the information that ants have
brains? Not from studying the beast, 1 lay.
There is a meat-ant citadel about 200yds.
from my back door, and daily the inhabitants
make a pilgrimage down the hill, on to a log, over
the creek and up the bank to my rubbish depot.
Then they commence the homeward journey, witti
iunks of material about four times their own size.
They have performed this journey month after
month, and never seem to think of .moving their
residence nearer the tucker emporium. Again,
watch a meat-ant’s nest on a road. At great
expense of time and muscle they burrow out
a city in the hard surface, and every passing trap,
dray or cart runs over their roof-gardens, and
bursts up the metropolitan waterworks and slays
hundreds of J’s.P. and other eminent citizens.
What does the intelligent ant community do.
Merely turns out in a body and pursues, the
wheel of the devastating vehicle with infuriated
ant language and empty ant salmon tins. -The
which being done, the party returns to patch up
damaged earthworks and re-build the public
libraries ready for the next waggon to flatten out.
This goes on for years ; the site is never deserted.
The only human intellect that an ant’s intellect
approaches belongs to a Peace Society.
“ The Loafer ” : They are not all coarse,
mannerless bloques who inhabit the waste spaces
Back-o’-Beyond. I recall one small merino-raiser
who religiously “mistered” all his shearers and
rouseabouts. As thus: “I should be greatly
pleased, Mr. Woolhawk, if you would kindly pay
just a little more attention to the trimming of the
legs of your sheep” ; and “Mr. Loppy, I should
be extremely grateful if you would oblige me by
picking those dags a trifle cleaner.” One Cobar
(N.S.W.) squatter held converse with his shearers
through the written word alone. If a man “toma-
hawked ” he would find stuck to his pen-door next
morning a note to this effect: “No. 15. —Sheep
very rough. Please shear cleaner.” If No. 15
reformed he would find in the same place on the
following morning this laconic message: “Im-
proved. Thanks.” On the Warrego (Q.) I struck
a station boss who handed me the gratuitous pan-
nikin of dust in a neat paper bag with : “ If you
•please, sir.” When travelling “on the never
one appreciates little touches like that.”

“A. Kanak” : Concerning “Medico’s” par
(8.8/9/’lo)re the female patient and thesecretdrug.
In the days before Australia was white, Schlinter,
an enterprising German of Bundaberg (Q.), did a
lucrative trade in beads, etc. (as a side line) to the
“ pore Kanaka.” The main line was rum, retailed
at a shilling a nip. A new and aspiring John
Hop sought renown by bribing the Kanakas to
store some in their mouths, with a view to a con-
viction for sly grog-selling. Result, Schlinter
fined £25. To make good this amount would take
weary months of dealing in beads and red hand-
kerchiefs ; so Schlinter had again to resort to the
shilling nips. But they were now poured into a
pint-pot attached to a chain on the wall, and as
the Kanaka was departing through the only door
he had to submit to a
violent swipe across
the bingey, for the
d slodgingof any
liquor whoh hud
not found the
way to its
proper recep-
tacle.

“Birnie”: Which Australian tree gives
the coolest shade ? (B. 29/9/10.) My note (it’s a
p.n.) goes on the kurrajong. In the bad summer
of ’92 Hooked after 10,000 weaners in a paddock
between Byrock and Brewarrina; and I’ve sworn
by the ’jong ever since. We used to wonder why
its light shade was cooler than the dark one of
the wilga or pine, and finally reckoned it must be
because there is more moisture in the leaves of the
kurrajong.
GERRINGONG.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I wonder if the red blood dances through some young
heart like wine
As, in the green Shoalhaven springtime it pulsed a-fire
through mine?
I wonder if, when Morning marches his cohorts brave
along
I he purple hills of Cambewarra, they echo to the song
Of some gay lad whose “love lies dreaming ” down there
in Gerringong?
Oh, I have wandered o’er the borders, and many lands
I ve seen—
The valleys of New England shining, the Queensland
canefields green,
The black-soil plains in brown leagues rolling, the plains
of Riverine.
But though in visions, wide and splendid, Australian pic-
tures throng,
The fairest star of all my dreaming still burns o’er Gerrin-
ftong.
Yea, I have answered to the longing. It lured me far and
wide,
Where dusty swagmen plod the Distance, where bearded
bushmen ride.
I’ve heard, along theGippsland ranges, the magpie’s morn-
ing song ;
I’ve seen the sunset shadows lengthen through woods of
Dandenong—
'But ah, the dew upon the clover that shines by Gerrin-
gong!
And now in dreams I see the palm trees, high waving to
the breeze,
And hear, on curved Shoalhaven beaches, a surf-song of
the seas,
The creeks, from silver harps outpouring their constant
symphonies ;
And all the glory of the southland and all her fervid song
Of love and youth, in recollection, come back from Gerrin-
f»ong.
I wonder if some boy is yearning beneath the fig trees
brown,
As Fancy paints in pictures templing the pleasures of the
town.
If in his soul the distant bugles with onward marches
strong
•Of Glory and Achievement call him to join the city
throng.
While Love and Faith, alas, lie dreaming in drowsy Gerin-
gong !
( wonder if a girl is waiting beneath the coral red,
That like a wounded heart is bleeding in flowers over-
head ;
While all the marvel of the morning, before her eyes
outspread
The green delight of pastures gleaming, the picture and
the song
Have grown to her but ghosts of Fancies —that died in
Gerringong.
No more—though summer follow summer, and spring
trip after spring,
Though clear among the scented lilies the joyous blue-
caps sing - ,
Though from the little painted chapel a cynic shrill ding-
dong
Of wedding bells at last may gladden the gossips in the
throng—
That Coral Tree shall be their trysting in green, old
Gerringong.
I wonder if, when Life’s rich dishes pall one by one on
him,
Among the aloes and the ashes, in day-dreams faint and
dim,
That boy will watch the sun uprising across the water’s
rim,
And in the clear Shoalhaven morning, heart wearied,
hear the song
Of Youth that long ago lay buried for aye in Gerringong.
VICTORIA.
E. J. BRADY.

“ Eddy son ” : I was working at the
Parkers at the time, and had gone into the State
forest to massacre rabbits. Carrol was with mo.
After a half-day’s wandering and much murder,
we came upon an unoccupied tent. We made
use of the camp ware and the water-bag for a
midday meal, over which we spent an hour. Then
Carrol suddenly discovered the proprietor of the
rag residence. A man’s head was sticking from
a round hole in a hollow gum about 50ft. from the
ground right above us. We called out, but re-
ceived no answer, and all appeals to the stranger to
“have a bit o’common ” were of no avail. We
went on, intending to return that way and inves-
tigate further. When we got back at dusk, the
man was still in the hollow butt, and poked his head
out of the same hole to watch us. We addressed
him on the folly of his ways for half an hour.
Then he must have grown weary, for he wagged
his head out of the hole again, and said wearily :
“Oh, do go away, you d d fools 1 Can’t you
see I’m a ’possum V” We went away, and sent
the constable. The constable smoked the 'possum
out, and locked him up till he beoame a man again.

“ C.V.E.” : Recently met “Overland
Jack,” one of the toughest of the Australian
prospector breed, at Marble Bar (W. A.). He had
just humped the “knot” from Port Darwin
(Northern Territory) to Wyndham (nor’-west
W.A.). Although he had travelled through
country infested with “bad” niggers, he was
never troubled by them, for the reason that they
thought him koonemi (mad). He consistently en-
couraged this belief by lighting a big fire at night
when he suspected Binghi was entertaining un-
charitable thoughts; then, stripping himself
naked, he danced round the blaze for half an hour
or so. After the performance he turned in, and
slept without fear till morning. Occasionally,
during the day, he repeated the performance
round his swag. As a further safeguard, he is
6ft. 2in. in his socks.
