“ Wyndy Ryder ” : White Australia ?
Most decidedly. A few weeks ago I reached a
station in the Kimberley country (W.A.), where
shearing was in full swing. Looking over the
board I managed to piok out five white shearers
from a horde of black and brindle men, women
and children employed on the premises. A mob
of gins were wool-rolling, picking pieces, etc. j
bucks and piccaninnies were picking up, and
three bucks were doing the pressing. Another
tribe was scattered over the landscape bringing in
sheep, with the assistance of a white boundary-
rider. Passed the
kitchen on the way
out, and observed Hop
Sue at work, assisted
by two lubras and
several naked brown
youngsters. Subs e-
quently visited another
shed, where there was
still less White Aus-
tralia. Bucks were in
the majority on the
board, and there must
have been several
tribes of Binghi scat-
tered round the place.
The nigs, are all em-
ployed on the permit
system—a permit issued
by a Resident Magis-
trate or Protector of
Aborigines to Mr.
Jumbucktowork Polly
or Billy for 12 months
in exchange for
clothes, tucker and
tobacco and Bi ngh i
earns all he gets.. Flee-
ing to Derby from this
station, on the road
I met Ah Sin coming
out of the town with
his wife and family
of four, said wife
being a cross between
black and white. Blew
into Derby with a
nasty taste in my
mouth, and charged
the nearest pub. Be-
hind the bar was a
big buck American
nigger. As far as I
can see, the few White
Australians in the
Commonwealth will
soon have to form a
union and apply for a
Wages Board.
“ Cict ” : Machinery triumphs even in re-
gard to the curse output; and the ox, which from
the time of the Pharaohs headed all others as a
producer of raw and gory language, must give
place to the traction engine. With the ox there
was a limit to the demand made on the bullockv ;
the team would do something in the long run, and
the need for blasphemy would expire. But what
prevails it to punch, poke, yell, curse, fume
and sweat at a refractory engine ? It can’t gee-
back, come-hither, whoa-thereoreven get to blazes.
If it becomes bogged, or cants over, or slithers
half-way down a cutting, or runs short of wood or
water, all the bullockies that ever steered a dray
might stand round and curse till the atmosphere
would melt lightning without driving the iron
cow forward a yard or even five-sixths of an inch.
The brute is sworn at from the moment it leaves
its garage till it reaches the scrap heap; every
driver, rider, pedestrian, shire council, hawker
and dog heaves at it sulphuric remarks ; road in-
spectors and navvies follow its trail of ruin, and
cry aloud to Heaven and the local Jay Pay to
smitethedashed thing;andlongafter midnight the
lights are burning in the Recording Angel’s office,
where all the staff is working overtime and
mourning over the contraption that keeps them
off their roosts.
A few days ago, while en-
gaged in the art or profession of trapping, I found
a fair-sized black snake caught by the middle in a
steel trap. The snake was almost dead, but not
nearly so dead as he was a few moments later.
After killing him I set the trap up in the same
place, without cleaning the jaws. Next morning
I took out an iguana that couldn’t have been more
fatally dead. Now, as I get one or two go.’s in my
traps each morning, and this was the first one to
be so uncompromisingly defunct, it seems to me
that the snake must have bitten the trap, and in
doing so had left enough poison to kill the
wounded gohanna. The moral of which, to fellow
spreaders of ironmongery, is : Be sure to thor-
oughly clean a trap after it has held a snake, else
when you start to go round your next catch you
may find yourself dead. And the annoying part
will be you won’t know what killed you,

“Machete”: Great is the love the
Papuan bears for his dog. In many parts of the
big island an amazing consideration is shown for
the tyke’s comfort and happiness. Seldom does
his owner chastise him with a brick or other
missile, and, if a well-meaning white essays
altruistically to take a hand in the brute’s moral
reform, an amazing flood of Papuan blanky and so
on eventuates. As a pup, the
Papuan dog shares with his two-
legged brothers and sisters any
of the maternal fount that may
be going. He is carried with
them, together with taro and
other oddments, in a comfort-
able net bag on his foster-
mamma’s back. In the Musa
river villages, where the houses
are on high piles, Fido is pro-
vided with a special opening in
the floor. From this a trough
made of a sago pal m trunk slants
to the ground, so that he may
convey his burden of fleas up
and down in comfort, while his
perspiring proprietors have to
climb an inadequate notched
post, when going in to dinner.
I have seen a dog sit on one
bank of the Mamba River, and
wait, with an occasional curse,
while his old mistress hurriedly
untied a canoe and came across
for him. For all his pampered
life the Papuan hound is a sad,
disgruntled sort of beast. The joy of life is not
in him. Insects and superciliousness are his main
features.

the way of mv legs. He apologised, and remarked that
the saddle was a nuisance. He explained that
he had brought an old one down to be done

a new one. Also, the saddler had promised to
send the old saddle down to the train, but it hadn t.

“ Quidam” hails the mainland :
The possibilities and advantages of King Island, lying
about half-way between Victoria and Tasmania, have
only recently been realised by the people of the former
State; but they have made.the most of the discovery
since, and practically the whole of the available land has
already been taken up. A widely-held impression out-
side the island was that it was simply a sandbank, and
the comparatively few inhabitants of the place apparently
had such a good thing on that they didn't trouble to
correct the error. The impression was ludicrously wrong.
Part of the west coast of King Island is sand, grassed
with melilotus (a species of lucerne) and spear-grass,
and it is in fattening cattle on this land and
exporting butter from cows grazed on the same
country that the old residents found their principal
source of profit. Last year 2300 fat cattle and 66 tons of
butter were exported. But the interior and east coast
of the island, comprising chocolate, black and peaty
soils with clay subsoils, carry timber that is a source of
wild surprise to people from manyparts of the mainland.
There are blue gums and blackwoods 250 ft. high and-
from 4ft. to 7ft. thick, and other forest giants 300ft-
high and 10ft. to 12ft. thick, and miscellaneous twigs and
herbs of similar tonnage. Most of the big trees are dead
owing to bush fires, but there are plenty more growing
up; and when the speculator is told that most of the
PREHISTORIC DENTISTRY.
“ Open wide, please, and don't make the slightest
noise, or you may discourage the others."
standing trees sprouted within the memory of white men
visions arise of the gigantic timber-growing industry that
may be fostered on Tasmania’s detached molecule, which,
by the way, is not so small by a hatful as it looks on the
map. The island’s rainfall is 35 to 40 inches, and in winter it
rains most of the time ; but it is nice, warm comfortable
rain, as rain goes. The temperature is rarely below
39deg. in winter or over 85deg. in summer. There is a
larere pub on the island, post office, doctor, police station,
butter-factory, stores and two lighthouses. There might
also be a church, but I can’t swear to it. At one time
there used to be only one lighthouse (at Wickham, on
the northern end of the island). Oversea boats got into
the habit of mistaking it for Cape Otway light, and at
times severely assaulted the island under the impression
that it was Bass Strait. In 35 years there were about 39
wrecks, and over 1000 lives were lost. Since the erection
of the second light the immigration of corpses has fallen
off to an alarming extent, and the dead birthrate is now
almost nil. The population of the island is about 900,
and they are a happy, hospitable people, who are not
afraid of To-morrow worth a cuss or even tuppence.
BLUE-RIBBON BROWN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Brown is a single man of means,
And there are several girls, with Look 9,
VVho’d like to “ save ” him (and his beans)
From Drink’s infernal tenterhooks.
So, sometimes, on these warm, wild nights
When we are taking beer by tanks,
Our souls up-flown to joyous heights,
Brown says, “ Mine’s ginger ale I Yes, thanks I ’
This good mood lasts some time with Brown,
And he goes on with ginger ale
Although wild, weird bar-noises drown
His plea for liquors, soft and pale.
But, as the restless, quick clock hands
Move on ; as yarn and chestnut sere
Pass swiftly round Bill Brown demands
At last, in innocence, “ Long beer 1 ”
It’s then that Brown somehow forgets
About his sudden, holy fear
Of big, strong drinks —the old Brown sets
All boldly out to swim in beer.
He plunges in, and, with a will,
Sticks to his new-found brew, and now
Mops up his loud, uproarious fill,
The wrinkles vanished from his brow.
Yet, later on, across the night,
When Brown has had at least enough,
He tells the Law, loud-voiced, “ ’Sorright 1
I’m zhinkin’ zhinzher-ale—shof’ shuff I”
VICTORIA.
FRANK HENTT.
THE INDIGNANT DINNER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When they tell us that all the giving of all the
Christians in the world amounts to a penny a head for
all the heathen, it is beastly bod business; and if we
don’t go home ashamed of oursehes, then we are beyond
hope. —lndignant Baptist minister.
What do I owe the heathen who loafs ’neath his bread-
fruit tree?
What do I owe the heathen, or what does the brute
owe met
What shall I send him at Christmas? A fatted
missionary ?
Why should the gander upbraid me, hissing within his
cage ?
Is he so keen to waddle off Life’s unsavory stage
That he calls on me thus to buy him, ruffling his feathers
in rage?
Back to your peas and pollard : More corpulent you
must get 1
Eat all they put before you ! Quack gaily and do not
fret,
And I will buy you at Christmas— the time is not npe
just yet!
What do I owe the heathen who waveth a palm-leaf fan ?
Must I vary his diet—which is vegetarian?
Then will I send him at Christmas this missionary-man 1

PERSONAL
ITEMS
T. J. Davey displaces Burston on the
mayoral throne of Melbourne. The new man,
who takes his job with monumental seriousness,
has made municipal life his one indulgence. The
electors of Lonsdale Ward made him a City
Father in 1891, and he has remained a Dad ever
since. It may, at least, be said in his favor that
he has the virtue of self-effacement. For 20 years
he dodged the chief civic position with a humility
that almost looked like pride.
JAMES McGOWEN— PREMIER SOON.
Sydney journalist Charlie Bean’s book on
Australian wool (“On the Wool Track”) —are-
print of articles in the S. M. Herald —gets a
good notice from London Spectator, which
praises the writer’s “strong, vigorous style.”
Unfortunately, the usually correct Spectator
makes a bad misprint, and talks of “C. E. W.
Bran.” Bean has left Sydney to look after the
Herald’s copy from London, and starts off with
a series of readable yarns, “Via America.”
Education-Minister George Fowles, of
Maoriland, who represents the Dominion at the
opening of S’Africa’s Parliament, is a draper by
occupation and a Cold-Tea apostle by persuasion.
He is also a single-tax enthusiast, and gives free-
hold farmers the shivers. Fowles has “worked
up from the ranks,” but, as often happens in a
case of that sort, is not too sympathetic toward
Labor ideas ; and he is reputed to have hopes of
a knighthood. By the way, his father, who died
lately, was a fairly well authenticated centen-
arian.
Parson Leyton Richards, from the Cold
Country, is Heaven’s new ambassador at the
Collins-street (Melbourne) Congregational taber-
nacle. Richards, a man of comparative youth,
has been for some years entangling the Devil at
Peterhead (Scotland). His tongue is restless with
wild rhetoric, and he is a man of terrible zeal
that will be a striking contrast to the serenity of
his predecessor, the cherubic L. L. Bevan.
A wonderful old stayer is Alec Forrester,
secretary of the Amalgamated Mining Engineers
Association of Victoria. He is a Scotchman who
first sniffed the cold air 82 years ago, and he drove
an engine until he was 70. He came with a de-
putation to Premier Murray the other day, and
left that lethargic individual gazing in wonder
that a man should be able to work so long. What
Forrester wanted, by the way, was a wages board
to give engine-drivers something better than 455.
a week.
George Reid, according to the cables, has
been “at work” in London. The unusual
activity of G. Feed is in connection with the
attractive question of MORE MEAT. George
says he seldom reads ; so somebody has evidently
told him that the continent is short of meat; for
he has temporarily thrown down his napkin and
left the banquet table to the waiters and the rats.
The idea of anyone going short of his rations
seems to have shocked Australia’s Pie Commis-
sioner to the very depths of his being and started
him going.
Sydney’s Mitchell Library has just re-
ceived the only known portrait of Barron Field,
one of the first two Judges of the Supreme Court
of N.S.W. The likeness doesn’t show him to be
nearly such a splendid person as a suburban
mayor of the present day. In his time Aus-
tralia had three courts—a Governor’s Court, a
Supreme Court and a Lieutenant-Governor’s
Court, which sat in Hobart and hanged enthusi-
astically. The Governor’s Court dealt chiefly
with land cases. Barron Field, who sat in the
Sydney Supreme Court, was not a bad Judge.
He, at least, hit culprits fair and square. But he
was touchy, and demanded assent to the idea that
a man of his calibre was thrown away in such sur-
roundings. To console himself he indulged in
verse-writing. He wasn’t the worst of his time
in that line, but the style is now out of date. Two
or three of his prettiest verses he wrote while
waiting for verdicts in death-sentence cases. But
a day came when he heard that Francis Forbes
was commissioned to inaugurate a new Court
system, under a new Charter of Justice, where-
upon he declared that N.S.W. was doomed to riot
and republicanism, and, packing up his effects,
he got away in a boat before the avalanche fell on
the land.
After a feverish spell of Thomas Tait, the
Victorian Ministry has decided that a local man
may easily be the best appointment
for Chief Railway Commissioner. The
points about the freshly imported man
are that he knows nothing of the
country, and that the country knows
nothing of him save that he is recom-
mended by people of whom the country
also knows nothing, and the finding of
the court is that the position isn’t
good enough. So Fitzpatrick, who
was Acting-Commissioner before Tait
came, is to have the job that he should
have had in the first instance. But
though bitter experience has cured
John Weary of the importing disease
in this one instance, he still hangs on
to the old complaint wherever possible.
There are two plans for harbor im-
provement in view. One is by an emi-
nent Maoriland harbor engineer, who,
after spending weeks in personal in-
spection, recommended the cutting of
a short, straight channel from Mel-
bourne to the sea, and the abandon-
ment of the old, long-winded, crooked
Yarra as a water highway. The other
is from an English engineer who was
never in Australia in his life, but who
has looked at the case on paper, a-nd
recommends that the endless dredging
of the weary miles of serpentine Yarra
be resumed. And Weary John says
he is going to stick to the imported
opinion supplied by the man who never
saw the place.
Ex-Sal’ Army man Bruntnell, who
gets into the Assembly for Annandale
(N.S.W.) by a 200 majority, has had a
stormy time. As far back as 1904
Owe’Sullivan licked him at Belmore
by about 800 votes. He got in for
Surry Hills at a by-election in July,
1906, with something like a 150
majority, and he was cast out by 900
votes next year, same as if he had been a devil
in the New Testament. And after that, though
lie was a persistent trier, there wasn’t a gleam of
success for the ex-big drummer till last Friday.
Several times last week the cablegrammer
mentioned that Mrs. Clara Warren had got a
divorce in London from “ Bill the Bludger, a
notorious Australian adventurer.” The lady is a
mystery, but Bill —a good-looking man of about
45, who” would steal anything from a sixpence to a
range of mountains —is still remembered by the
force in these parts. When the Australian takes
to the confidence game he is usually among the
world’s best; and, curiously enough, some of the
swiftest men at the business—men who have per-
suaded kings to let them carry the crown round
the corner as a sign of good faith and have never
come back—have hailed from peaceful Bathurst,
of all places.
London Morning Post announces that
“Major F. J. Carandini, ex of the sth Lancers,
has received permission from King George V., by
Royal warrant, to use and bear the titles of
Marquis of Sarsano, Patrician of Modena and
Noble of Bologna, to which he succeeded on the
death of his father. He will in future be known
as the Marquis of Sarzano.” The Major’s father,
having lost his estates, came to Australia in the
mid ’4o’s, and set up as a dancing master. He
married Marie Burgess, a singer, and the family
of Carandini became famous on the Australian
stage and concert platform. When the ducal
Edinburgh was out this way he interested himself
in Frank James, the only son, and had the lad
entered at a military training school in England.
He may adopt his father’s title; but, titles and all
thrown in, he will still owe more to his mother
than he ever did or will to his other parent.
Fraser E. Churchill, successfully cockying
nowadays in the Molong district (N.S.W.), was
25 years ago, the crack amateur oarsman of the
Cold Country. For four years he rowed with the
Eton crew, and three years with Cambridge.
Not long ago, he was visited by the man who was
coxswain in ’a crew which won at Eton 30 years
ago. Their weights then were <st. 101 b. and
12st. 51b. The coxswain had fattened up to
19st. 101 b., and Churchill to 13st. 51b. Churchill,
who' came to Australia as a jackeroo, after-
wards bought Brymedura, and has applied his
horse-sense very successfully to fodder conser-
vation.

Ernst-Carroll, who has been appointed
hon. Australian Commissioner in Neuchatel, once
resided in S.A., and was afterwards Professor
of Geology in the Tasmanian University. For
years he has been enthusiastic in keeping Aus-
tralia before the Swiss, and he has boomed it at
the Neuchatel University of Commerce, which
attracts hundreds of students from all parts of
the world. His father was Speaker of one of the
German State Parliaments. The second name,
Carroll was taken when a relative left him a com-
petence.
The self-killer often takes it into his head
to write a letter to the coroner; and if such
epistles are ever to find a place in books like
E. W. Cole’s Complete Letter-Writer, a model
was lately left behind by a 60-year-old suicider in
Melbourne: —
To the Coroner, 4/10/’lO. No sleep. No food. Finis.—
H. T. P.
You couldn’t find anything more comprehensive
and concise, even in the Acts of Parliament that
Charlie Kingston used to draft.
McGowen’s first choice of a Lands Minis-
ter lies between Neil Neilsen and J. L. Trefle;
and if one takes Lands the other will probably
take Agriculture. Both know a plough from a
pump. Trefle’s father, still living, was the first
man to grow wheat in the Temora district. The
squatters, of course, told him that wheat was im-
possible, and he’d better go away before he
starved. Trefle, a French-Canadian, said that they
didn’t look too emaciated themselves, and he
would risk it; and the Temora district is now
one of the most prolific grain areas in Australia,
while cultivation has spread out a hundred miles
further west. Young Trefle was the boss plough-
man of the district; but later in life he took to
the pen, and ran one of the Temora papers.
Even George Reid has his uses ; the pub-
lishers of a well-known encyclopaedia are using him
widely in England and America as an advertise-
ment. It appears that Gorge, at the dinner of
the Royal Literary Fund, unburthened himself as
follows: —
I was looking up an encyclopaedia this morning to get
hold of something to say to-night. I am in a flat that
has not got a library, but, fortunately, has an encyclo-
paedia—and may I, as a man who seldom reads, express
my profound gratitude to those who publish encyclo-
paedias. I find I can get a brief up on any subject under
the sun, at the shortest possible notice, by referring to
books of that sort. Give me an encyclopaedia like (advt.).
There you get a bird’s-eye view of everything, a.nd that
is the only sort of view I can take at my time of life.
It is all very well for the publishers, but what
about the country that pays his salary ? The
sleepy old gentleman with his bird’s-eye views,
his weary remark about “my time of life,” and
his damaging admission that he is a person who
“ seldom reads,” is calculated to give the intelli-
gent banqueter the impression that the land
whose accredited representative he is must be a
sort of Cow Flat. George Feed shouldn’t be High
Commissioner for a hustling continent; he ought
to be Consul for Geelong.
New South Wales has a blind orchardist
who trains the apple and the peach to shoot at
Kurrajong, and makes his trees pay up remark-
ably well. He does all the work himself, prun-
ning in the most approved style, and keeping e
soil free from weeds. He has no difficulty in i
criminating between the fruit-bearing brandies
and the non-productive ones, and his trees are
quite shapely.' The producing value of eaca tree
he measures with remarkable exactitude, and
when the branches are fruit-laden he selects e
marketable stuff without hesitation. Ihe neigh-
borhood also credits him with being one ot the
best judges of horse-flesh in the district.
G. R. W. McDonald, who did the unex
pected and wiped out Minister for Lands Mooi <
at Bingara (N.S.W.), will be the pink-clieeked
boy of the new Assembly. Until a few month'-
back, he was in the Justice Department and sucked
in Labor doctrines at Broken Hill. Then tin-
hobble of redtape was too much for him, and In-
started out for freedom and a crust in the Tam-
worth district. He has a neat wit, and has
turned out many a good way-back paragraph.
One of the effective leaflets with which he ate up
his tough opponent showed the Chinese store of
Hip Cow (or words to that effect) plastered with
notices imploring the district to “Vote for Sam
Moore, the Workers’ Friend.”
Postmaster-General Thomas, who is doing
things, wasn’t always suspected of being a fast
goer. He first got into the public’s eye during
the big Broken Hill strike in 92. Though
Thomas was the miners’ president, Sleath and
Ferguson took charge of things, and Thomas was
packed off to Queensland because it was reckoned
that he was too easy-going for a strenuous tussle.
Had he remained on the spot, he would probably
have gone to gaol with the other leaders; as it
was, the Queensland police shadowed him every-
where, and that and the scurvy treatment he
afterwards got from the mine-owners, put into
him the only drop of bitterness in his nature.
The owners had the idea that, having been a mine
manager, Thomas should have ratted to their
side; and the worse they treated him in conse-
quence the surer they made his seat when the ’94
State elections came round. He hasn’t been out
of politics since.
“GENTLEMEN, THE KING!”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“I don’t want people to call me‘Mister.’ I’ve been
christened King O’Malley, and I ought to be called King
O’Malley, without embellishments before or after.” King
O’Malley, who spoke as if his dignity had been outraged
for many years, commenced the new order of things by
“rounding on” the pressmen who had carelessly ad-
dressed him in the orthodox fashion.—Melb. Herald.
“ Stow the ‘ Mister,’ ” said O’Malley,
Mad as anything ;
“ Even though we he not pally,
Always call me ‘ King.’
“ ‘Mister’ or * Esquire ’ is merely
Slush that Tories sling :
Being thus baptised, I’m clearly
Nothing more than ‘ King.’
“Duke or marquis, earl or viscount,
Other blokes may be ;
Meanest men my equals I count
When they talk to me.
“ Not from ‘ noble ’ birth or station
Should distinction spring ;
I’m a pattern for creation,
Yet I’m simply ‘ King.’ ”
In the court or in the alley
None can ever bring
Charge of pride against O’Malley',
Who was christened “ King.”
Here’s a toast for all the masses,
Here’s a hymn to sing !
Democrats, fill up your glasses,
Drink—with cheers—“ God Save the King ! ”
VICTORIA.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
Treasurer William Watt, of Victoria, was a
man who felt only moderately good when he announced
the State’s revenue and expenditure figures for the
financial year to June 30, 1910. This was the position as
compared with previous years :
June, Revenue. Expenditure.
1902 .. £6,997,792 £7,398,832 Def. £401,040
1903 .. 6,954,619 6,759,960 Sur. 194,659
1904 .. 7,319,949 7,339,608 Def. 19,659
1905 .. 7,515,742 7,343,742 Sur. 172,000
1906 .. 7,811,475 7,261,475 „ 550,000
1907 .. 8,345,534 7,679,143 ~ 666,391
1908 .. 8,314,480 7,862,246 „ 452,234
1909 .. 8,247,684 8,240,177 „ 7,507
1910 .. 8,535,747 8,488,667 „ 47,080
£70,043,022 £68,373,850 5ur.£1,669,172
The expenditure is going up in a remarkable fashion,
and apparently the days of big surpluses are done. Even
such surplus as there is has to go, according to the Rail-
ways Special Funds Act, towards railway improvement,
so Willie has nothing to carry forward.
For the present financial year (to 30/6/T1) the
Victorian Treasurer estimates his revenue from present
sources at £8,660,000, the probable return from the Aus-
tralian Treasurer being greatly reduced under the 255.
per head subsidy arrangement. And he sees ahead of
him £8,940,277 of expenditure. The difference repre-
sents a large ahd cheerless deficit, and as William W. has
no love for intense cheeseparing, he proposes to levy
new taxes—a considerably increased land tax, and
a slightly increased income tax. Being a sanguine
man, he puts his revenue, when the new imposts
are fixed up, at £8,964,665, leaving a surplus of £24,388.
The bulk of the theoretical improvement is to arise from
the amended land tax. It is to represent Jd. in the £ of
the unimproved value with certain exemptions, and the
big Victorian landlord, who now pays about £90,000 a
year, is to pay £296,000. The chances are that he will
flatly refuse to do anything of the sort, and will throw
out Watt’s scheme on its ear, and propose drastic
economies, or loans, or both. The Victorian House of
Landlords was unmanageable enough on this point when
there was no Federal progressive land tax in view, and its
general attitude was that no deficit, however vast, justi-
fied further taxation of the big landowner. Now,
maddened by the Federal tax, it will probably be more
solid than ever on this point.
• * * *
Tasmania, whose Government never mentions
Federation without putting its handkerchief to its nose,
expected a deficit last year; and the Treasurer, who
delivered his Budget last week, takes it almost as a
wrong that he didn’t get what he expected. Revenue
insisted on coming in £54,320 in excess of the estimate ;
so there was a surplus of £11,611 instead of the antici-
pated deficit of £21,406. (Sniff.) This current year—
and the Treasurer takes out the hanky again—the
Government expects to raise £980,416, and it knows
it’ll have to spend £1,013,161, so there’ll be (groan) an-
other deficit of £32,745. The chief explanation of Tas-
mania’s troubles is that it refused to pay its way like an
honest, respectable persorf, but borrowed money to pay
not only for all sorts of works, but for all sorts of ser-
vices. At the same time, it allowed great areas of coun-
try to be either used for sheepwalks or not used at all.
The railways, therefore, haven’t had a chance, because
even a prize ram isn’t much of a railway customer ;
so the railways called during the year for £79,594
of help from the general revenue, and the
interest on the other odds and ends of loan expenditure
had to be practically all paid out of taxation. The one
thing above all others that Tasmania wants is popula-
tion, and it will have to get more people, no matter what
happens to land values in the process. It may be very
awkward for the big land-owner ; but desperate ills de-
mand desperate remedies, and Tasmania’s ills become
desperate. Federation isn’t responsible ; it was the old
shameful policy of muddle and drift that landed the
island in the bog, and all the snivelling and nose-wiping
m the world won’t get it out.
* * * *

This State bank has undergone.a change during the year,
having been made a branch of the State-guaranteed
Advances Office. This office includes the State
Land Bank, officially known as the Advances
to Settlers Branch ; the Advances to Workers Branch,
which helps city and other people to become their own
landlords; and the Advances to Local Authorities
Branch. The whole business was put into the charge of
J. W. Poynton, hitherto Public Trustee, and previously
magistrate and lawyer. The change in control has pro-
duced some change in the accounts. The Act provides
for a sinking fund, and that is responsible for the chief
change.
• • • • • •
ThiB sinking fund is, however, a strange bird.
Says the Act (section 30): —“ In connection with each
branch there is hereby established a sinking fund
as security for the capital moneys raised for ad-
vances belonging to the branch. In the case of
each such branch the sinking fund shall consist of a sum
to be paid into the sinking fund each financial year out
of the Advances Account from the moneys belonging to
the branch equal to 1 per cent, of the total capital
moneys raised for the purpose of the branch... and
then outstanding.... Subject as aforesaid, the respect-
ive sinking funds and the investments representing the
same shall be held by the Public Trustee, to be applied
by him as and when the Minister directs in repaying the
capital moneys for which they are a security.” The Act
doesn’t say that the sinking fund shall be built up
out of profits. It simply says that when the Settlers’
Branch borrows, say, £1,000,000, it shall pay £lO,OOO a
year to the Public Trustee. But it need not make that
£10,000; it may borrow it. Or it may pay it back out of
the repayments received from clients. It is a fool of a
sinking fund altogether, and is only calculated to cover
the name of sinking fund with ridicule. Previously
there was a sinking fund, and it had a respectable
character, being made of honest profits. But
it has 1 now been absorbed by the bogus sinking
fund, though the two have nothing in common. The
genuine sinking fund was £44,190; and “a sum of
£57,438 has been added to the sinking fund established
under section 30 of the Act, bringing the total amount
standing to the credit of that account to £101,628.” That
£57,438 isn’t, of course, included among the reserves in
the balance-sheet. It only appears among the borrowings.
The profits show a considerable shrinkage, due
to a heavier outlay for interest. The old loans have
no doubt carried the old rate ; but an additional
£586,000 of temporary advances had to be arranged
to finance the branch. Apparently, this was dear money.
And the branch didn’t really need it all; it had to
borrow £57,438 simply to pay it to the Public Trustee in
connection with the weird sinking fund. Still, there are
no proprietors to be paid out of the profits. What are
referred to as profits are in fact surpluses in ex-
cess of interest, and the whole of the amount is avail-
able for reserves. These reserves amount, as shown, to
£180,326. The advances total £5,662,973 ; so the reserves
are equal to about 6s. sd. per cent, of the advances or
risks. This looks very little protection ; but it has to be
remembered that many instalments have been paid off
the bulk of the advances, wherefore the loans outstand-
ing are far under the market value of the securities.
Still, in case anything very bad should happen to Maori-
land and its property values, the reserves ought to be
fed up much fatter yet. They would have been much
fatter but for about a quarter of a million of loan
discounts which have had to be made good. This, of
course, will make thing’s better in the future, because it
really means that the branch will have to pay out less
interest than it would have done if the loans had been
issued at par.
The expenses are very moderate, and show a
good reduction this year :

Until a couple of years ag’o the cost of valuations was in-
cluded with the charges ; but the work is now done by
the Government’s special Valuation Department. For
the sake of uniformity the fees paid to that Department
are included with the expenses as set out in the fore-
going table. Most of these fees are, however, paid by
clients, and don’t come out of general revenue. No
other State bank in Australia works at so low an ex-
pense rate, and that is mainly because of the superior
size of this bank’s business. It is an experience which
ought to encourage borrowers in the other States to rely
upon the public Cohen ; for the more that get their ac-
commodation from him, the lower the rate of interest
they will be able to get the money for, owing to the
natural reduction in the expense rate as the turnover
increases.

This is the infant among Australian banks ; it arrived in
1888, about six months after the Bank of North Queens-
land. Thus no new bank has appeared in Australia for
the past 22 years—at least, no new bank that has stayed.
The Royal made a pretty bad start; but it had a
cleaning up, wrote off half its capital, and thus
started life afresli with a clean slate and a new lot
of resolutions. It lived a very quiet life for years; it
paid no div. at all until 1897, and then for sometime kept
the payment at 3 per cent. Also it confined its business
to Melbourne ; but a few years back it spread to Bendigo
and Ballarat, and a few months ago opened up at Sydney.
Probably this last step accounts for a good deal of the
big increase shown in the advances. That movement and
others are indicated in the next table :

Yet, though there has been this very solid increase of ad-
vances, the cash position is still strong; liquid assets are
equal to no less than 43 per cent, of all liabilities to the
public. Further, on figures, the shareholders’ risks are
not excessive : for every £1 of capital and reserves the
advances are equal to £3 7s. Partly, no doubt, because
there is not a very large proportion of the public’s money
in the business, the profits calculated on capital and re-
serves combined are not very large either: —

But while not very large those figures are quite large
enough to suggest that there are not many unproductive
accounts in the total of assets. The last little table
shows that the earnings have shrunk a good deal. That,
however, is not a matter for either shareholders or de-
positors and other creditors to worry about. As a matter
of fact, the bank was sailing too close to the wind a few
years back, and didn’t have the reserves it should have
had ; but it improved its position very much by doubling
its paid capital, which now stands at £300,000. And a
considerable part of that new capital is represented by
liquid assets which, though they don’t earn very much,
put the bank in much better position to meet trouble
than it used to be.
* * * *
Ifc would be a lot more satisfactory if the
Mutual Life of New York would issue its accounts in the
same form every year. No doubt the co. is making good
progress, after the set-back of a few years ago, but that
progress isn’t easy to follow. Before the commotion in
1904, this co. had achieved £2,362,285 of new premiums
in one year (1903). With ren'ewal premiums the total
for that year was £11,618,074. For the past year
(1909) the total was £10,972,662; but the new
business figures are not set out. The shrinkage is due to
two causes—(l) the American insurance legislation, im-
posing restrictions on the quantity of new business and on
the expense rate, etc.; (2) the heavy surrenders and with-
drawals. But here again there is a variation from the
old form of the accounts. It used to be possible to say
what the surrenders amounted to. Now surrenders
and endowments are lumped, which is a most unsatis-
factory way of doing things. This shows the movement
as far as it can be followed :
1903. 1909.
Death claims £3,965,020 £4,531,493
Endowments and surrenders 1,765,638 3,927,641
So while the death claims have increased by about 14 per
cent, in the six years, the endowment claims and sur-
renders have increased by 122 per cent. The endowment
claims may have increased more rapidly than the death
claims; but it is almost certain that the surrenders are
mainly responsible for the big jump in the figures. For
the previous year the surrender figures were made avail-
able :
1903 £813,770
1908 .... .. 3,034,569
In the wild days of American insuring, over-insurance
was common; and over-insurance is almost as bad as
under-insurance. Further, in those wild days equally
wild estimates of profits were put forward. The position
is really much sounder than it used to be ; but when the
old estimates were shown to be untrustworthy, there
naturally followed dissatisfaction, and a demand on the
office to know what it would pay to end the business.
Under the new American law it has been
illegal since 1906 to write “deferred dividend ” policies ;
the annual bonus system, always practised in Australia,
now prevails also in the U.S. The cash distribution as for
last vear was £2,546,561, and, in addition, £885,412 was
added to the fund out of which bonuses are added to
deferred div. policies as the latter complete their dividend
periods. This deferred-div. fund amounts now to
£15,192,420 ; the whole of this amount will be added to
policies during the next 16 years. The cash and deferred
dividend (or bonus) added together are equal to 31 per
cent, of the premiums ; the cash bonus, apart from the
deferred bonus on the old policies, was equal to 23 per
cent, of the total premiums. This is a better result than
was shown a few years ago. In fact, for five years run-
ning, each year has given a better bonus than its pre-
decessor. The improvement last year was due to (1)
large profit on the sale of securities ; (2) a lower expense
rate ; (3) satisfactory mortality experience, etc. The re-
duction in the expense rate is the most satisfactory
feature. In 1903, when the mad competition for new
business and big figures was still raging, the expense rate
was £2O 14s. sd. per cent, of total income. For last year
it was got down to £l2 3s. 7d. per cent. This means less
glittering aggregate-business figures ; but it means bigger
bonuses for policy-holders. They get more substance
nowadays, wherefore they are not likely to weep over the
lost shadow.

While Victoria had an eruption of trustee cos., and over-
did a good thing, N. S. Wales was content with two —the
Perpetual, incorporated in 1886, and the Permanent,
which is two years younger. The authorised and
issued capital of each is a round million ; and
the paid capital of each is a modest £25,000.
There was, and still is, virtue in this big heap of
uncalled capital. Twenty-five years ago the trustee co.
was a new institution, whose chief merit was that it
guaranteed that nothing unpleasant would happen to a
trust estate after it had been committed to the co.’s
keeping. In Victoria the pioneer co. got in a large paid
capital by way of guarantee ; in N. S. Wales both the
cos. argued that the practical effect would be the
same if they made the shareholders liable for a large
sum. The trustee co. doesn’t guarantee the trust
estate against losses. It doesn’t guarantee that
the trust investments will bring in any specified
interest. But it is bound to see that the instruc-
tions in a will are carried out, and if it misinterprets
them it is responsible. Also, of course, it is respon-
sible if any of its officers leave for parts unknown with
the cash, or drop it in backing sure things for the Metro-
politan. It isn’t, however, as liable to make mistakes as
the amateur trustee, or even the lawyer who is a trustee
in his spare time ; and in other respects the advantages
of the trustee co. are overwhelming. Whether it has yet
done all that it is capable of doing is another matter.
The Maoriland State office has, in fact, gone a step farther
than the Australian private offices, and guarantees a fixed
rate of interest if it is allowed to choose the trust fund
investments; and, boiled down, a life-insurance office,
which has some capital in the business, does practically
the same thing. It seems very likely that, sooner or
later, Australian trustee offices will have to follow suit.
As they get more and more experienced their risk of
making losses through misinterpreting wills will diminish ;
and the gradual growth of business will produce a further
slight reduction in the percentage costs. It should,
therefore, be possible to do more for the same money, or
the same for less money, without diminishing the aggre-
gate of profits. In common with the best of the offices,
the Permanent’s earnings in proportion to funds
in the business (capital and reserves) are very fine
and large. Last year they amounted to £l2 13s. 3d. per
cent. ; and when a co. can make £l2 13s. 3d. per cent, on
its funds, it is time to ask whether it can’t afford to ex-
tend its benefits a trifle. Admittedly such a co. may be
hit heavily at some time or other; and admittedly, there-
fore, one single year’s profits are not a fair criterion. But
as this co. has been going on for very many years
without being hit, it looks as though the peril can
be regarded as only very moderate. In the past
10 years tne co. has made great progress. The
amount to the credit of the estates in hand
is £3,029,970, as against £1,350,258 ten years back. But
the proportion of trustee work done by the N.S.W. pro-
fessional trustee cos. is still much less than it ought to
be.

This co. found the usual trouble waiting for it out on the
rocks when the boom-tide had ebbed. It had a capital of
£30,000 and very fair reserves ; but, unfortunately, it had
put almost everything into mortgages, and the value
of the securities slumped horribly. But troubles
are sociable birds, and no sooner had one arrived
than two or three more perched alongside. For in-
stance, the co. had the lease of big city office pre-
mises ; and some of its sub-lessees failed and others
cleared out. Also, there was the expensive Swan litiga-
tion. That is the way with trustee cos. ; they rarely
have troubles when values are rising; it is when
the things are falling that the beneficiaries nose
round and scent out misinterpretations of wills,
and come at the trustee co. to recover losses
sustained. Thus it came about that for four or
five years this co. paid no div.; and, as the table shows,
when it did resume it did the thing modestly. A fine
improvement in profits is disclosed ; but it is possible
that in the early years some of the revenue was used
quietly to fill up the gap between the book value and
the market value of the bad old securities. Still
that wouldn’t make the present profits any the
less. Mortgages don’t now bulk overwhelmingly in
the list of assets. The big investment lately
has been office premises in Queen-street ; on
these, which cost £13,950, there is a mortgage of
£5OOO. Apart from that, there are no debts. Other
assets include £lO,OOO of Victorian 3 per cent, stock,
taken into the accounts at par. The co. has had a good
year ; estates to the value of over £400,000 have come
under administration, making the total upwards of
£2,500,000. In view of this, and of the generally satis-
factory outlook, the dividend for the scond half was put
up to 6 per cent.; and even then more was laid by than
was distributed. On shareholders’ funds (capital and
reserves combined) the profits were at the very good rate
of £9 ss. 4d. per cent. If the co. can put by £l5OO a
year, and if it can only earn 4 per cent, on that money,
the extra income is equal to another 4s. per cent, per
annum on the capital; so even if profits from business
should presently ease off somewhat, there is a strong
factor at work to maintain the dividend.

This is the Federal’s second time on earth. Its first time
climaxed in a brief but gorgeous boom-drunk, followed
by a long and painful recovery. When the society came
to its senses after the boom it found that it had
given I O U’s for £389,939, against which it held
assets over the bulk of which it could only shake
its sore head doubtfully. It then put its nose
to the plough, and kept in the dusty furrow for 12 years,
selling a bit when it could, paying off a bit when it could,
and generally feeling very sorry for itself. All this time
it paid no dividend at all, of course. In 1904 a revalua-
tion was made, and the surplus of assets over liabilities
w'as set down at £122,839. A new co. was then formed,
with a capital of £96,351, leaving £26,488 to represent
reserves—a contingency fund in case things should go
wrong with the new valuations. The provision was ex-
ceedingly liberal, because more than half the assets con-
sisted of new advances, and considerably less than half
was represented by old foreclosed properties. The new
society continued to get rid of the latter w'hen buyers
could be found ; and the money was put into new ad-
vances. Also, new deposits were secured in moderation,
and use w'as found for them also. This shows the pro-
gress in the several lines :
1904. 1908. 1909. 1910.
Liabilities—
Deposits, etc... £25,048 £32,651 £43,830
Advances .. £63,160 136,217 143,452 152,156
Properties i n
possession .. 54,071 20,124 18,813 16,729
Cash balances.. 4,779 2,548 8,006
Thus only £16,729 worth of properties remain on hand.
For some years, as the first table shows, a profit was
made on sales. What has happened in that respect
latterly isn’t disclosed. If any profit has been made it
has been stuffed away somewhere. However, it pro-
bably doesn’t amount to much, because the last
two years’ sales have been small. The advances include
balances due by purchasers on time-payment sales.
There may, of course, be some losses over these ; but the
more instalments that are paid, the less the society’s
risk ; and, as few'sales have been made lately, most of the
balances ought to be well protected. There is, how-
ever, some entirely new' business ; the risk with regard
to that depends entirely upon the management’s view of
what is a safe thing to advance. Deposits are increas-
ing ; but the security is still beyond all question. From
the investor’s point of view, the strength of the society
lies largely in the size and genuineness of the reserves
with which the new co. started ; they amounted to about
6s. per £1 share, and they naturally make it a tolerably
easy matter to pay a substantial dividend on 20s.

The Bellambi Coal Co. (N.S.W.) —the last few
years of a remarkably prosperous concern :
To Re- Re-
Profits. Dividends. serves. serves.
1907 .. 17£ p.c. = £30,625 £77,351
£25,000 transferred to Capital .. 52,351
1908 .. £61,936 17£p.c.= 35,000 £26,936 79,287

It seems probable that no other Australian coal co. has
been making quite such profits as this one has done
during the last few years. It only arrived on the
scene 21 years ago, and as it spent a good while
getting things ready, it has still a long way to go
before it will own no more than a damp hole
in the ground. Though its Bellambi property was
pretty extensive, it added the South Bulli a few years
ago, and now its total holding runs to 200,000 acres,
partly leasehold and partly freehold. Its remarkable
money-making success has been due to the discovery of
the virtues of its output for bunkering purposes ; and it
has big Orient and other contracts on that ac-
count. Also its small stuff, regarded one time
as refuse, has found a good market for coke-making
purposes; and it has a seven years’ contract to
feed the Broken Hill Prop.’s ovens. The co. has at
various times issued bonus shares, which explains why
the reserves are no greater than they are ; for, of course,
these shares have had to be paid for out of accumulated
funds. For a fthv years prior to 1907 the capital was
£175,000. Then it was decided to issue another 75,000
shares, bringing up the total to the full £250,000 author-
ised. Of the £75,000 of new capital, £25,000 was trans-
ferred from reserves, and the other £50,000 was got in by
issuing shares at par—a sweet titbit for shareholders,
seeing that the shares W'ere then paying 17J per cent.
Last year the profits were affected to some extent by
strikes, though the trouble on the southern field didn’t
last so long as it did in the north. For appearance sake
more, perhaps, than for anything else, the dividend for
the half-year was put down to 10 per cent, per annum ;
for the second six months 15 per cent, per annum is paid,
making 12J per cent, for the year. There is still £26,095
left over to lay by. It may be that this is not all net profit;
the bulk of it (£26,000) is money added to the reserves
“ for insurance of steamers, steamers’ renewal and de-
preciation, collieries amortisation, and colliery stores de-
preciation.” If no other provision is made for deprecia-
tion, etc., the true net profit for the year is not the
£57,345 mentioned in the table—it is that, less what-
ever ought to be written off to cover depreciation
of mines and plant. But it is highly probable that, in
addition to the formal reserves, something is written off
the assets. The main features of the balance-sheet com-
pare as follows with those in the document of 1905 :
1905. 1910.
Liabilities £39,445 £12,017
Assets —
Collieries, plant, steamers 209,345 265,069
Stores and stock .. .. 4,591 7,334
Liquid assets .. .. 55,183 131,375
In those five years £50,000 of capital has been got in (and
obviously it wasn’t got in because it was wanted). That
much, and a great deal more, has been put into the mine
and plant; while liquid assets have moved this way :
1905. 1910.
Liquid assets (inc. stores).. £59,774 £138,709
Liabilities 39,445 12,017
Surplus £20,329 £126,692
That is £106,363 added to the liquid assets in five years,
after paying big di vs. and putting large but unknown sums
into plant, etc. It is dazzling. In fact, one is almost
tempted to say that, with economy, the co. could have
afforded to pay—well, at least, three ha’pence a ton to
the miners for hewing small coal.

The most modern, simple and effective Vacuum
Slimes Table is the “ Wilfley.” It is continuous, and boh-
sists of a revolving drum which is partly submerged ih a
tank of slimes or material to be filtered. The drum Is
covered with a filter cloth supported on grooves oh the
drum, which are connected by means of holes to the
interior to allow the liquid to be withdrawn. Through
the trunnions at the ends of the drum the vacuum pipe
passes to the interior. This pipe connects with the
interior of the drum, through which the liquid and sh-
are withdrawn by means of a vacuum pump. The filtered
slime is removed by a simple apparatus placed at or
below the level of the pulp in the tank. So that directly
the cake is removed the surface of the filter cloth as it
revolves is-immediately re-immersed in the pulp ready to
receive a fresh cake, thereby preventing the sucking in
of air, and consequent loss of vacuum between the time
the cake is removed and a fresh one is forming. The
residual liquid in the slime cake is removed by distribu-
ting water over the surface of the semi-dried cake. Owing
to the fact that the filter is circular in form, it will be
seen that it is continuous in operation, and has no com-
plication of valves to get out of order ; furthermore,
work is being done continuously during the ivhole revolu-
tion of the filter drum. The Austral Otis Engineering
Company, of South Melbourne, have the sole manufactu-
ring rights of this filter.* [2

■and had £2170 of cash and liquid assets; now it
•owes £1550, and has £2835 in cash, and liquid
assets. So a deficit of £1213 has been turned into
a surplus of £1285. Meantime, however, £1764 of new
■capital has been got in. The co. has, therefore, so far
put by £734 in cash or liquid a*ssets out of trading, and
this has to cover depreciation (renewals, etc.). The co.
can’t afford to be any more liberal. It exports what it
■can’t sell locally ; and in that business it is always liable
to be hit by a sudden slump. It ought to start an export
■reserve fund.

From London: — “The Spelter Convention has
just been renewed, and prices have tightened. Con-
sumers’ stocks are low, dealers hold little, and Conven-
tion stocks of metal are smaller than they were, so the
outlook is good.”
* * * *
The Amalgamated, the second of the big Aus-
tralian zinc cos., has got on to the dividend list. An
interesting yarn will be written some day about how
Australia suddenly became the second-greatest zinc pro-
ducer in the world. At first it was thought that magnets
would get the discarded metal out of the Broken Hill
sulphide ore and tailings. Two or three German
cos. , experimented on those lines for years, but
didn’t achieve much success. Then three pro-
cesses arrived in a heap. One belonged to the Elmores,
two hard-fisted young English brothers who spend their
lives in inventing things and punching their way'through
difficulties. The others came, curiously, from two Mel-
bourne brewery chemists, working independently. Of
these two Potter had brutal luck, and died before he
reaped any' reward ; the other, De Bavay, got the back-
ing of Melbourne brewery people and afterwards of the
Baillieus. The De Bavay'process was some time getting'
to work ; but now there’s a gorgeous profit while the old
tailings last and the spelter market keeps good. The last
10 weeks pan out something like this: —
Revenue, all sources .. .. £76,829
Outgoings—
Working expenses .. ..£34,004
Percentage to South 00. .. 14,183
48,187
Total profit .. .. £28,642
Administration costs are not included with the exes. On
the other hand the zinc is taken at £22, which is nearly
£2 below the current price ; so the surpluses will prob-
ably balance the additional exes. The co. is working the
Broken Hill South’s tailings under a partnership agree-
ment ; and the foregoing calculation shows that ii is pan-
ning out well for the South, which is apparently getting
about 3s. Id. per ton in excess of the flat rate agreed
upon.
* # # *
Tolwong Minerals (N.S.W.) expects to get its
plant in order in a month from now. Only the most indefi-
nite of reports are allowed to escape regarding this mixed-
metal proposition. The valuably mineral stannite (a
double sulphide of copper and tin) occurs entangled in
dense arsenical pyrites, in addition to the usual gangue
minerals. The flat, ore body is exposed on either side of a
precipitous gorge close to the Shoalhaven River. Along the
north exposure the vein is mostly a uniform streak, charac-
terised mainly by its narrowness. This feature is con-
firmed by’ the only adit driven on this end of the property.
The south exposure shows a series of small lenses, down
the centre of which short adits have been driven. None
of these adits have been connected away from the out-
crop, so there is no apparent reason to suspect the
deposit of changing its exposed character. The short
lenses are connected usually by a' narrow vein of ore.
The assumption that the widths exposed by the pre-
sent working faces are averages over the distances
between the adits is liable to cause trouble. Ore
reserves will remain mythical unless the co. conde-
scends to get a move on with mine development.
The complexity of the ore and its novelty' has so far fas-
cinated the management, and the usual business of get-
ting a little ore blocked out has been neglected. Treat-
ment by means of revei'b. furnaces will, it is alleged, pro-
duce marketable products of arsenic, copper and tin.
The means and methods to be adopted have been tried
wholly or in part elsewhere ; and costs have developed
the habit of killing such schemes iti their infancy.
The co.’s pot has been kept bqiling by market-
ing the reserve shares, (>ut this source of “re-
venue ” is fast becoming exhausted. Considering this item
together with (2) lack of developed ore, (3) experimental
nature of treatment plant, and (4) doubtful value of
final products, the co. is in for a parlous time in the near
future.
•* * * *
Great Cobar celebrated the acquisition of the
Cobar Gold Mine (Fort Bourke) by a substantial increase
in the monthly gold return. The output of copper still
remains far below the frequently predicted 900-1009
tons, with little chance of ever getting close to that
quantity. Work now in hand at the Oobar mine is ex-
pected to improve mullocking facilities to the extent of
permitting a slightly increased tonnage from here. Fur-
nace work has been slowly improving, and an increase
could be handled by the treatment plant. For reasons
indicated, the big mine cannot, however, yet supply the
tonnage. Also the Ohesney mine is feeling the strain of
having all the plums carefully removed, and the re-
maining stuff is not likely to be rushed. Unless something
unforeseen happens, things will, therefore, continue dull
at the Chesney. Fort Bourke now provides the silicious
part of the furnace mixture, and the gold output should
be well maintained. Development work here should
also be satisfactory, as the workings are within the zone
where secondary enrichment is supposed to have
occurred. It would be interesting to know the cost of
the increased output, comparing old and new plants.
* * * *
Bourke’s Hill tin (N.S.W.), havinggoton to the
dividend list, looks like staying there awhile. Working
account profit for the half-year to February, 1910, was
£2884 ; for the past half-year, £2233. In the 12 months
£4500 has been divided, and, after paying all debts, there
was more than enough for another 6d. (£1125) in hand.
Altogether, £5625 has been cut up so far, and two-thirds
of the total area still remains to be worked. The,tough-
ness of the ground is the co.’s chief bugbear; it means
constant shooting and heavy costs. The paid capital is
£16,875.
* * * *
Cosmopolitan, Hillgrove (N.S.W,), crushed 227
tons for 4260 z, gold, with 30oz. from concentrates. The
profit on the month’s run is reckoned at £1000; but this
is a bit doubtful if the long course of expensive develop-
ment work is taken into account. How long the 2oz.
stone will last is also worth consideration. The end of
the main south level at 224 ft, is already looking rather
poor, with 2ft, of stone averaging 13dwt. In the second
level, however, the few feet of driving from the shaft has
proved some good-looking stone, though even that is only
worth 24dwt. for an average width of 20in. The reef ap-
pears to have been thrown about by erosscourses; but
these are sometimes associated with rich makes of stone.
That was the case in the neighboring Baker’s Creek mine,
where the tributors are now doing fairly well by picking
out the best of the stone left in the old stopes.
Southern Cross (W. A.) is having the time of its
life, and is swelling visibly over the number of visitors
who are journeying to the old-time centre to see the
Bullfinch finds. Leases have been pegged for miles
around ; but complaint is naively made that it is a pity
so little work is being done and so much time is spent
hunting for buyers. The Bullfinch itself is looking
gorgeous, and is apparently to hold its own. A little fly
settled on the ore last week, when it was announced that
the Kalgoorlie Gold Recovery Co. had had "some difficulty
in treatment; and, as the plant is a pretty complete one,
the note is significant. But if Doolette, Harris and Co.
can gouge out many more tons of ore like that so far
treated, they will be able to afford to let somebody else
do the puzzling later on.
* * * *
Adelaide is speculating wildly in Bullfinch
options. The holders and first robbers are losing n» (
time : already one co. in 130,000 shares is on the market,
or about to get there. A Bullfinch in the hand is worth
two in the Southern Cross bush, so the birdcatchers pro-
ceed to get on velvet and have a bit over. There is no
certainty yet about the course of the lode, wherefore the
whole thing is a gamble.
The half-yearly report of the Sons of Gwalift
South, the VVestralian-owned mine which was recently'
under offer to the Sons of Gwalia, show's a profit of
£11,958, of which £6692 is written off for depreciation.
This leaves £18,394 to be carritd forward. On this the
directors declared a shilling dividend, which will absorb
£3500. The plant is now reckoned complete, so capital
expenditure should not be a large item for some time.
As the mine shows 61,000 tons of ore worth 415., besides
15,000 tons of sands worth 75., and 7780 tons of slimes
valued at 65., shareholders will not trouble whether the
option on 30,000 shares given to Lionel Robinson and Co.,
of London, is exercised or not. The option runs to
October 23, and if the report oT'-Cge geologist now going
through the property is satisfactory, iTTxI is to hand before
that date, the London brokers may take command. Also,
it is whispered the last has not been heard of the pro-
posed sale to the Gwalia.
r * • * *
The Golden Ridge, a local Kalgoorlie concern,
has followed the example of many of the English cos.,
and taken a two months’ sampling option and a six
months’ purchase option on the Augusta at Laverton.
The move is not viewed favorably by some of the share-
holders, as it means swallowing up reserve cash now
standing against share values, while it is also taken as a
reflection on the Golden Ridge and its chances at depth
—chances, by the way, which are not being much rushed.
Besides, that appalling disappointment., the Laneefield,
is near the Augusta, and that doesn’t make people very
anxious to make the lady’s acquaintance.

The new Oroya-Links Co. (W.A.) has knuckled
down to it, and authorised “ a reduction in quantity of
ore treated to 7000 tons monthly, pending vigorous
development in Eclipse and Amalgamated groups of
lenses.” To temper the wind to the shorn lamb it is
added that “the result? are very satisfactory” at the
Eclipse and the Amalgamated ; but it is really too early
to say much of any value on that point.
* * * *
New Temperance is to rise out of the stone-cold
ashes of the old Temperance at Ballarat. A bulky
£270,000 is the record of the mine, and the old story is
told that work was stopped because of a boom elsewhere
and the want of system in the old co.’s operations.
These are poor props to rely upon. That there
was grit among the old-stagers is shown by their
having punched out stone down to 850 ft. At the
spree over the re-opening the other day in Ballarat East,
one James told that he was the last man to leave the
bottom of the old mine, and that as good gold was then
carried as there had been for five years before. No one
paused to ask if the five years’ work had been profitable,
or whether there had cotne at last the tired feeling on
the part of shareholders which naturally follows years of
profitless call-paying. Anyhow, the co. has three strings
•of some sort to its bow—the Temperance shoot and a lode
on either side of the shaft. Its plan ought to be to try
the two latter while it is pumping, for Ballarat records
show that the best gold is nearest the surface.
* * * *
Side-line work at Bendigo still spells profits.
Central Red, White and Blue is turning out fair-grade
stone from the saddle at 311 ft. The worst is that the
shoot of stone is not large ; hut its persistence charms
those who have boldly locked up shares; l So far the
shoot has been opened up 120 ft. north and south, and it
is within the mark to say that the two faces now look as
promising as ever. Other development ,w;ork is, how-
ever, only so-so, and it wants more than a 4ft. shoot of
stone to justify the screeds that are being posted to
Melbourne to make softies there believe that a second
New Moon has appeared on the horizon. What helps the
tarradiddlers is that at Lansell’s Little 180 rich gold is
being got. But it is all in a small way. There is no Bull-
finch records to set the blood rioting, and that is about
what is wanted at Bendigo to get a hustle on the
people who want to forget all they have dropped of late
years on premature booms. . *
The fuss over the Cathcart, Ararat, is material-
ising in a way that gives some people a sick feeling under
the belt. Meanwhile Harry Dodd is back from Mount
Copeland with a smile as bland as that of George Reid
when he took the dog from Manly on his holiday
trip abroad. And George Buchanan and the
Bryants aren’t in mourning either, and the Stock
Exchange has settled down to the opinion that
the Cathcart isn’t the iame dog of yesterday.
Manager Bryant isn’t likely, after Boulter’s pilgrimage to
the legal manager, to let developments lie fallow, and
the Stock Exchange cannot complain if he should fill the
notice board with glad tidings. The change arises from
the high ground having been met down the lead to the
east. The latest wire, flashed in Sydney to sizzle up de-
tractors there, tells of 12dwt. per truck dirt. This
is as good as was got off the rises east of No. 5,
and helps to explain the nice little yield of 700 oz. for the
past fortnight. Should the mine furnish, a second Cup
season surprise, look out for high jinks in Ararat! It’s
a bit soon, though, to bet on that event, seeing the pace
the wash was beaten out from the No. 5 to No. 10 rise ;
and it’s just that fact that keeps outsiders from swallow-
ing all the yarns about the rich ground ahead.
* * * *
Forest Creek Hydraulic Dredging Co. (Vic.),
which has been cheering old Castlemaine for 10 years,
sees its hearse galloping up. It used to have enough
ground to keep two plants busy ; but one of them has
lately been sold, and the second will probably take in its
last mouthful towards the end of 1911. Last half-year’s
work gave a profit of £530; but as there was a tidy bal-
ance in hand when the term started, and as £466 was got
from the sale of the plant, £1687 was divided. They call
this money dividend, hut most of it is merely return of
capital. The Forest Creek Co. is one of the many that
don’t write down mine and plane ; so that 18 months
hence its capital, though nominally intact, will really be
represented by nothing better than a few pounds’ worth
of old machinery and a few shillings’ worth of distracted
ground.
* * * *
The half-year’s copper output from Mt. Lyell
(Tas.) to September 30 was 4024 tons, which is 140 tons
below the yield for the previous term. For the full 12
months the output was 8188 tons. The only thing that
causes any immediate concern is the decline in the
value of ore treated. Reckoning up the returns for 12
weeks to October 5, the smelter handled 84,985 tons ore
and the converters produced 1689 tons copper, 140,8580 z.
silver and 23940 z. gold. Taking copper at £5O, the aver-
age return doesn’t pan out more than 255. per ton of
ore: even if the red metal is taken at £6O, only 4s. can
be added, making’ 295. for the stuff treated (84,985 'tons
for 1689 tons of pure copper). Last month’s return to
October 5 certainly looked up (584 tons 'copper, 49,7570 z.
silver and 7900 z. gold). Still the grade is low ; for the 12
weeks the furnace has 'required 50’03 tons of ore to pro-
duce a ton of copper. And gold and silver values nowa-
days average -less than 6s. per ton of ore. There is less
than £l5 worth of the precious metals in a ton of copper,
and a big'slice df that has to be cut off to pay for re-
ining.
* « * *
Mount Lyell had a Melbourne Stock Exchange
crowd at' it last week. Plainly a body of speculators is
making the North Lyell shoot of ore the winning post ot
the Lyell Blocks. One well-known trainer puts it: “If
it ain’t been spewed over the boundary. Well I’m bust! ”
The assertion crystallises the view of tbfespeckers that the
Lyell Blocks has at last got in side Sticht’s armor. Everyone
realises bv now that the llOOfc. level of the North Lyell is
not only below the measure of the ore in the 1000 ft. level,
but is far short of the 850 ft. level resources. While the world
knows that there was a pitch of the ore-body down to
1000 ft. towards the Lyell Blocks, there is not the same
w4d’e recognition of the shrinkage in the size of the shoot
at 1100 ft. The country is getting as hard as the gates of
Sheol, and the ore is not as rich as at 850 ft. So the
point, after all, is, what will be the grade of the ore and
t he size of the shoot should the Blocks get it ? Even if they
do make a strike, where is their supply of pyritic ore to
come from ? The possession of that asset is what inakes
Mount Lyell so cocksure, in spite of the behavior of
the North Lyell deposit at depth.
• * * *
Evidently on the strength of Shepherd’s report,
McGregor, Cloncurry (Q.), has got its new share issue
underwritten. It is apparently taken for granted that
Shepherd will carry out the construction work, and pro-
bably run the furnaces afterwards; and when Shepherd
says he can make a profit there is a strong party prepared
to back the opinion.
* * * *
Hampden-Cloncurry (Q.) is doing well with its
prospecting and development work at the Duchess mine.
The deepest level at 400 ft. vertical has been driven 467 ft.
north, and has proved two highly payable wedges of ore,
with rather a long blank of calcareous material and
quartz between them. The first make of rich ore started
about 200 ft. north of the shaft, and at a point 220 ft.
north a rise was started which will connect with a winze
from the 260 ft. level. It may be noted that the ore near
the top of the rise at 96ft., and at the bottom of the winze
at 30ft., is considerably below the value of that obtained
for the first 50ft. of rising above the 400 ft. level. The
second wedge of ore started to make good just past the
400 ft. mark, the barren quartz and calcareous material
changing to gossan, with rich veins of copper-glance and
bOrnite.
• * * *
East Mexican, Charters Towers, crushed 298
tons for £1723, or £5 16s. 7d. per ton. This is not so rich
as previous returns, but is payable. The good prospects
so much talked about a week or two since have shaded off
at the end of No. 6 ; and although there is a wide forma-
tion in the rise, the stone is patchy and somewhat lightly
mineralised. All of which excuses a weaker market,
irrespective of the crushing. It is madness, though, to
judge a show like the East Mexican on face prospects at
any time.
* * * *
The fact that New Brilliant Freehold, Charters
Towers, has bought an acre from its neighbor, the
Brilliant Block, for £lOOO shows that the Freehold is any-
thing but faint-hearted. It evidently intends to explore
the Day Dawn formation much deeper. So much atten-
tion ha's been devoted to Day Dawn developments that
the Freehold’s first love, the Brilliant formation, has
been almost forgotten. Perhaps the Freehold directors
■ remember old times and the sickening calls that had to
be made to explore the Brilliant formation, which
was so erratic as it tailed west. The N.B.F. report
mentions that No. 3 east is out 136 ft. with no stone
showing, but it is continuing this drive in the hope of
finding a payable reef. Prospects along No. 3 west for
372 ft. have been in and out, but it is encouraging to find
that the winze below No. 3 is down 66ft. on a reef of
fairly good stone. A second winze has been started
below No. 3, but so far there are only “splashes of
mineral.” The winzing ahead of No. 3 and below the
level show that the Day Dawn is very capricious.
* * * *
The Brilliant, Charters Towers, sends out a
report full of information about the prospecting work
done for it by the New Brilliant Freehold. The latter
co, extended its second level 387 ft. into the Brilliant,
and sank a winze at 220 ft. east of the boundary to a
depth of 100 ft. The last 14ft. showed a strong make of
stone; 12£ tons crushed for an average of £4 10s.
Those few tons are the firstfruit of the Day
Dawn development east of the dyke, which,
by the way, not only displaced the reef, but
has left it even more erratic than it was to the west
in the Freehold and Mills. The Freehold can’t continue
prospecting on behalf of the Brilliant, so the latter is
thinking about repairing its shaft at a cost of £3OOO.
Meantime the Brilliant keeps an anxious eye on the cross-
cut coming towards its boundary from the Brilliant
Block, because the work will test the Day Dawn forma-
-1 tion at a depth of 2100 ft. The Brilliant Block has bought
some mining machinery, and to lighten the expense has
sold a scrap of ground to the Freehold for £lOOO.
*• * * ' * .
Brilliant Block, Charters Towers, has stopped
prospecting while it instals the machinery purchased for
£2500 from the Block and Wyndham. The crosscut at
2100 ft. vertical reached the Brilliant P. C. boundary, but
didn’t pick up the reef. Probably it will be early next
year before sinking can be resumed. The Brilliant P. C.,
next door, is repairing its shaft; but it would be very
much better for these two'cos. to come to an understand-
ing, for the Brilliant Block shaft is so close to the
boundary that both leases could be prospected from it. '

Mungana (N. Queensland) has bought a crush-
ing plant and engine for the Girofla mine, which is provid-
ing shipments of mixed copper-lead carbonates and lead
sulphides for the Chillagoe smelter. The Lady Jane is
also producing some high-grade copper ore, low enough
in lead to be sent direct to the copper furnace. The total
monthly output from the mines is from 2000 to
2500 tons. The extraction from 3036 tons in August
was 176 tons c'-jyper, 142 tons lead and 42,9530 z.
silver, Showing a gross value at average prices of £16,564.
It is good ore from money-value point of view ; but it is
complex, and the cost of separating the metals always
stands in the way of substantial profits. And there is
cause for anxiety about reserves, too ; hence the boring
all round the cqmpass in search of ore. The deepest
level Is in the Girofla, at 610 ft. ; the lead sulphides in the
face there show a better quality;, but that doesn’t tell
whether any profit will be left after treatment. The pur-
chase of a crusher suggests that the co. is going to try
working on a new line.

Waihi (M.L.) shares are not so jumpy as they
were. After all, the scare seems to have given “cold -'
feet” to very few ; the statement of transfers issued by
Rhodes, the co.’s Auckland attorney, seems to prove that
much. In June, when shares were fairly high, 3090 were
transferred to London, and in July 1245. ■ In September,
when shares struck the low bass note, 3105 were back
in Auckland. The sinister rumors got going first
through Thorne George, a local director and big
holder,'having his shares transferred to the London,
register. It was explained that the transfer was only '
to convenience him in drawing his dividends while in
the Cold Country ; but lower assay values in some cross-'
cuts in the No. 9 level now being blocked off didn’t help
to warm the toes of investors. Still there has been a big
chance to get out at a high price and in again at a low,
which makes one think hard.
* * * *
Talisman, Karangahake (M.L.), crushed 4000
tons for £19,112 ; costs for the month, including develop-
ment, are reckoned at 375. lOd. Shares lately got a bit
of a set-back through afire throwing the air-compressors
out of work ; but the manager reckons development will
be in full swing again by Christmas. A new lot of shares
has been issued to finance the opening up of the Wood-
stock section of the co.’s property.
From Kalgoorlie : The recent cave-in of the sur-
face of the Golden Horseshoe is only what will happen
at nearly every bier mine here. In the early days the
upper levels were badly timbered, and filling was done with
sand, which has invariably proved a failure for mullock-
ing-up. For several years past- most of the ore has been
slimed, and damp slime is the best filling' BUbstabpe. It
does not “run "like sand, and it hardens to rock in a
very little while. So, though there will be serious trouble
over the top levels, the bottom workings should be
steady. As a nutter of fact, the top of the Horseshoe has
been shaky for the last four years, but any whisper on
the point has been instantly suppressed.
