Cookin’s not half bad if you
have nice people to cook for—
my idea is a cook ought to be
boss of the kitchen, and nobody
should interfere with her the
sort of place 1 like is where
the missus leaves everythin’ to
me —-l knows what’s wanted
how much to order, and the
best sort to buy—much better
than any missus does.
The thing that most people find
hardest to buy, with satisfaction
to themselves, is tea —it isn’t
everybody as knows when tea
is good, do’ye see? —now, I’ve
tried ’em all, and the only
tea l ever bother about gettin’
is Robur 1 like the No. 1
Grade, and 1 believe it’s as
good tea as can be got.
Tea travellers don’t waste their
time about my kitchen l tell
’em straight out 1 won’t be
bothered with ’em —1 know what
Robur is—it’s good wholesome
tea it’s always the same, and
the flavour’s just as nice as
nice can be, and what more
can anybody get or want.
The Cook.
QUALITY—BUCHANAN WHISKY
“BLACK & WHITE.”
A WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, October 18, 1910.
My Dear Arini, —
The Chelmsfords took a spell from bazaar-
opening and other Imperial labors one recent
evening and went to Her Majesty’s. They
seemed to enjoy the show, but I fancy'their bliss
was mitigated by the attention the audience paid
them. Every time Langford Kirby or the Gibbs
family did or said an amusing thing a hundred
heads were twisted towards the Chelmsford box
to see how vice-regality was taking it. Use recon-
ciles one to all things, saith the sage ; but for me
such an experience would be overmuch like
taking my pleasure in the monkey-cage at
the Zoo. Still, Government House doesn’t need
to go to the play to be a target for the public eye.
A few days ago I saw what, at first, I took to be a
popular street-rising against the Wade Govern-
ment. Not so, however. It turned out that one
of the Dudley household was buying a tie in a
haberdashery shop, and the crowd was there to
see he wasn’t taken down or something. It was
the same at Randwick. Hordes of expensively if
not well-dressed suburbanites massed about the
vice-regal pens and peered into the occupants’
faces with the frankest sort of interest. But
the wooden expression on the vice-regal face
never wavers no matter how fierce the fire of
eyes. On the principle that there are few things
the normal person dislikes more than to be bailed
up in a corner and persistently stared at, it seems
to me that our Governors earn their salaries while
in Sydney. They are better off, in ways, than
the imported polar bear, who has to endure the
same trial, year in year out, for a miserable fee of
a few buns and an occasional lighted cigarette
hurled down his throat; but that is about the
best that can be said.
A feature of Sydney’s recent festivities was the
majestic height of many of the young women who
were on view. One of the handsomest was Mrs.
Cyril Ward, a visiting sister-in-law of the
Dudleys. She is 6ft. if she is an inch, and the
owner of a straight, slim and perfectly-modelled
figure, calculated to make a show-girl envious.
The good-looking Anderson girls tower above
ninety-nine male escorts out of a hundred. So
does Miss Enid Bayley ; so does Miss Ogilvie, a
fair vision from up-country. And there are others.
The Cyril Wards were due to proceed to the
Carlton at Manly after they returned from Med-
low. There they proposed to test whether the in-
digenous sport of surf-bathing is really the thing
it is cracked up to be, as a preliminary to the
strenuousitiesof Cup week in Melbourne.
I was in time to meet the remains of Mrs.
Allen Taylor’s reception to Lady Henderson, wife
of the visiting Admiral, coming away from the Town
Hall on Monday afternoon. It was a beautiful re-
mains. There was Miss Rose Scott, glad with a
spice of sorrow at the turn the elections had taken.
There was Mrs. (Dr.) Collins in a green dream-
frock, with a white gardenia in her corsage,
and lime-and-pink silk roses overhead ; and
Mrs. Earp (in a dawn-in-December pink cloth,
with an eighth of a mile of pink corals round her
neck), and Mrs. Monty Severn were more re-
mains. Mrs. Layton was also “just going,” with
Mrs. Flulfy-Ruffles Justice Cohen. The guest-
in-chief still conversed with her hostess. Lady
Henderson is of medium height, with the figure
of a girl of 17 and clear-cut features, and was
silkily frocked in grey. She is a sister-in-law, I’m
told, of Mrs. (Dr.) Jenkins.
Gaby Deslys, who, according to the cable man,
was a side-issue of the Portuguese trouble, was
well known by sight to a number of Sydney
people who chanced to be in London when the
“New Aladdin,” in which she had a part, was
produced there some four or five years ago. They
describe her as a blue-eyed, orange-haired girl,
angelic-looking in her fairness. She was a great
friend of Fanny Dango. A lately-returned
Sydneysider saw the dancer at a French water-
ing place wearing a white linen skirt, a short
coat of bright cretonne and a lingerie hat,
and looking like a beautiful doll. I don’t sup-
pose that many persons believe that her flirtation
with King Manoel (if any) had anvthing to do
with losing the lad his job. Most likely the
cabled yarns anent her share in the revolution
represent the astuteness of her manager. ‘‘ Here’s
an important crisis,” says he ; “I must drag in
Gaby by the tights.” And he does, for the cablers
have such large ears and the public such a wide,
wide mouth—especially when the potage to be
swallowed is heavily seasoned with scandal about
a man and a girl.
If the aged Francis Joseph of Austria, in whose
capital the Deslys is now dancing, were to be sud-
denly bereft of his throne by revolutionaries, and
told to go and get work in Albion, the dancer’s
manager would certainly rush out to the live-wire
man with the misinformation that “ the woman
did it.” Albion, by the way, notwithstanding
Byron’s line about “ the cold in clime are cold in
blood ; their love can scarce deserve the name,”
seems to have been ever sympathetic to dis-
tinguished lovers. Charles 11. was never in
danger of having his throne bounced away from
him through his intrigues with orange girls ; and
there have been later philanderings than those of
Charles and Nell.
'■A man sends me this: “King Alexander, of
Servia, lost his crown and life the other day because
his Queen hadn’t been all she should ; and now one
reason advanced for the summary dismissal of
Manoel, of Portugal, is his devotion to Miss Gaby
Deslys. These happenings indicate a change in
the popular ideal of a king. Of old the royal
morals weren’t questioned, so long as the man
who owned them was handy with the broadsword
and lavish with his subjects’ money. The
habits of England’s Georges were inexcusable.
The domestic story of the French kings is unfit
for any decently-conducted Sunday-school; and
the same applies to the more prominent members
of the House of Savoy, to the Hapsburgs, to the
Spanish sovereigns, and, above all, to the Napo-
leon family. The populace always accepted their
ruler’s frailties as necessary accompaniments of
their position. Not so to-day. The king, if he
is to hold his job, must give general satisfaction.
His character must at least be as good as his
butler’s. He has laid down the battle-axe
and helmet of his ancestors and taken up
in their place the umbrella and top-hat
of the city man. And he must act accord-
ingly. In the period when it was part of a
king’s job to lead forlorn hopes, quest after
Grails, attack the Saracen in his insanitary lair,
and otherwise take big risks, the nations were
willing to excuse a few moral lapses. But now a
king has little to do save draw his salary and lay
foundation-stones. There are so many who are
capable of these duties that derelictions from the
path of duty are severely chided.”
Poor King Manoe 1 started handicapped, any-
way. He was christened with 13 names. And
now he has but one—Walker.
The Frank Browns—she was Miss Elsie Blax-
land of the ancient exploring family—are off to
older worlds. They intend to be some years
travelling.
Other departers for the Motherland and the
Coronation will be the ex-Mayoral Tom Hughes
pair. A very presentable brace indeed. No
shaky syntax, saggy deportment or audible eat-
ing of consomme in that direction. Sydney ad-
mires the Hughes couple, and they have its
permission to attend the George V. production,
for they will be creditable representatives. The
W. H. Kellys, who have just endured a week at
Federal Government House, are going in Decem-
ber ; and Mrs. Forsyth, of Dudley, near Newcastle
—the wonderfully-frocked Mrs. Forsyth and one
of the best riders anywhere—is preparing for
early flight in the same direction.
An exhibition of George Lambert’s work will
be opened at the Society of Artists’ rooms at the
Q.V. Markets on the 27th of this month. Mr.
Hardy Wilson, a Sydney architect, who has been
six years in foreign capitals studying his craft and
learning to loathe the disfiguring Austra-
lian architecture, such as mars Mosman,
will manage the exhibition. Allowing for
the partisanship of an admirer, Lambert
is a bigger man than Australia realises.
He is recognised, says Mr. Wilson, as a leader of
British art. Dusseldorf has asked him to select
the British work for its exhibition, and he has
already bought for German galleries. New
York has almost stopped work waiting
for him. Lambert’s portrait of the late
King was a masterly achievement. The
monarch stands near his horse, whose head is held
by a groom. Men and steed are life size ; the
picture is lift. high. “The Blue Hat,” which is
amongst the pictures Mr. Wilson has brought
here, he describes as a miracle of artistry. A
lady holds a small boy on her knee, and has
put on his head the big blue hat of a visitor who
is standing near. The group represents Lam-
bert’s wife, two little boys, and Miss Thea
Proctor. The painter is said to be a certainty
for the next R.A. Associateship.
Mr. Wilson’s own contribution to the exhi-
bition will be his collection of 18th century
carved ivory fans. He has the finest collection
of its kind in the world, he asserts. Some are
painted on chicken-skin, others on silk. The
carving is the work of old Chinese artists to the
order of noble French houses, and, as the art is
lost, we ought to be grateful to the collector for
letting us see something that has been, but won’t
be again. The world is too busy inventing aero-
planes to go groping after lost Chinese arts, es-
pecially the kind that are painted on hen-skin.
Contralto Ella Caspers has sprung a little mine
on the subscribers to the fund that sent her for
study to foreign parts. She writes from some
place in Germany to Miss Blanche D’Alpuget
that she is to be married to Mr. Charles Bradley
in January, an-J requests that the balance of the
£3OO collected for her should be returned to the
subscribers or placed to the credit of some other
student. About £7O of the fund remains, and the
committee is puzzled as to the ultimate bestowal
of the money. Legally, the committee cannot
convert it to any use but that for which
it was collected. To return it to the
givers seems ridiculous, as each person’s
share would be infinitesimal. Before Miss
Caspers left here, her engagement to Mr. Bradley
was rumored but denied. However, she has had
two years’ musical education, and will, she
promises, return here for a tour. Next year,
probably.
Concerning singers : Sydneysiders would like a
chance of hearing Mrs. Mary Conly in oratorio.
Her work in this class of music is said to be very
fine. The Philharmonic might arrange the
matter.
Dolores will come back to Sydney about two
months hence for the sole purpose of singing at
Mr. Sykes’s concert. She is raining the silver of
her voice on the Westralians about this date. A
Melbourne girl, an R.C. in training for the cheru-
bim, tells me that Dolores and her faithful com-
panion, Miss Something, never missed matins
whilst in the southern city. Every early-morning
of the week the devoted and devout pair were to
be found in the doubtful daylight of the cathe-
dral. Their efforts to save the soul of the star’s
manager (Southwell) by getting him to arise and
be plucked from the burning were heroic but un-
availing. The wretched man remains Lost. At
Horsham the whole town turned up at the railway
station to meet the singer, and the Mayoress read
her an address. At Stawell she opened the new
bowling-green by bowling the first jack on its
shaven sward.
Tenor Walter Kirby is in Sydney, a living
picture of what to wear and how to wear it. He
looks as if he were paying a chronic morning call
on the Countess of Dudley. As a matter of fact,
he has been singing duets with her nearly every
day of his stay. And he speaks of her musical
knowledge as far above that of the amateur, and of
her voice as “somethingexquisite.” When thelady
married, correspondents wrote of her as having “a
voice like Nordica.” But she does not raise it at
any even of her private parties. None of the Few
Called and Chosen who are summoned by telephone
to an informal evening have been honored to the
extent of hearing that voice breathe in song.
They lose something, for Kirby says Lady
Dudley’s singing (to her own accompaniment) of
French ballads is a memory that will dwell for aye
under the regidn of his ashes-of-roses tie with the
powder-of-carrots’ spots.
The tenor, who, by the way, is a Maorilander,
has been like “ one of themselves ” amongst
English and foreign royalties, and the coroneted
persons of England who pay artists high fees
for warbling at their town and country houses.
The late King commanded Kirby to supper, and,
of course, be had to go whether he liked or not. I
wish some King would invite Randolph Bedford to
supper. Efjs account of the occasion would be worth
printing. Princess Henry of Pleiss, our visitor says,
is the most beautiful woman he has ever seen, and
she sings and dances “as well as a professional.”
She does the Italian and French dances. Princess
Louise of Argyle, a daughter of the late Queen
Victoria, is also the owner of a voice. Voices
seem to be raised in all sorts of unexpected
persons and places. Kirby sang a duet with
her at one of her charity entertainments. Further,
he says “she looks like a girl of twenty.” One
wishes he had found out more about this phase of
the royal lady’s accomplishments. Lady Want-
age, who is chieftainess of Lloyd’s Bank —a multi-
millionaire and a fosterer of art and music—has
been one of the tenor’s greatest friends amongst
the Lights o’ London. She it was who armed him
with introductions to the Dudleys.
Mr. Kirby’s concert will eventuate next Thurs-
day, the 20th inst., at the Y.M.C.A. He will
sing the aria from Andre Chenier, Caruso’s great
aria from Pagliacci, and a group of Italian,
French and English ballads. Mrs. Slapoffski,
Staell and Harold Whittle will help.
The engagement of Miss Norma McMeiken, of
Melbourne, to Mr. Keith Maxwell is announced.
The lady is dark, strikingly handsome and an
heiress. Last year she went to London, was pre-
sented at Court, and otherwise had a pleasing
time. Mr. Maxwell is a member of a local business-
house, and belongs to one of N.S.W.’s most aged
families.
How much more brains go to the making of a
play like “ Paid in Full ” than to the stringing of
beans or the writing of an “Our Miss Gibbs.”
To your “ Akenehi ” “ Paid in Full ” seems a suc-
cess of construction and American portraiture.
Katherine Grey, as Emma Brooks, wife of the
collector of a steamship company, is a revelation
of the essentially feminine. Cyril Mackay plays
the husband, Joseph Brooks (that name of Joseph
again tacked on to the unsatisfactory man !), and
suggests the modern American of Claudio in
“Measure for Measure” for meanness and
sordidness of character. There were cataracts of
applause during the progress of the play ; but the
final curtain fell to no tumult of appreciation. I
fancy the audience was disappointed at finding
that the old captain, whose flat Emma Brooks
visited at close upon midnight, hadn’t strictly
dishonorable intentions towards his visitor.
Everything pointed that way. It was a cun-
ningly baited hook of the dramaturgist’s to lead
his audience up to expect a deadly sin and then
present them with a cardinal virtue.
It is a singularly quiet play save for the young
husband’s froth and bluster. And it is not a
particularly frocky one. Miss Grey, while she is
poor and living in a cheap flat, wears a poor,
cheap, white frock. When she is well-off and in a
flat to match, she has a martingaled gown of
flame-colored satin, with a tunic of amethyst
ninon. Susie Vaughan, who is Miss Grey 's stage
mother, dashes in and out on her way to and from
a theatre in the blue silk get-up of a woman
whose husband’s will, as she herself says, has been
a disappointment. Florence Gleeson, with a
motor disguise, has a dandy little motor-bonnet
of brown, with bunches of little pink flowers.
Mrs. William Desmond, wife of Miss Grey's
new support, is built on the same noble plan as
her sister, Nance O’Neil. She is large and fair.
Probably a later play will give her a chance of
appearing.
This is for Mr. George Willoughby, of
“The Night of the Party,” at the Criterion.
The make-up of the manager of the Had-
stone Castle Hotel would drive clients to the
opposition shop. He wears a pair of let-us-prays
(or side whiskers), and looks like a dissenting
parson paying pastoral calls. Mr. Willoughby
ought to send him to the Australia to study
Father Smith, or run him up to Mealow to collect
an imitation of Beautiful Burgess.
For Coogee and Manly, where surfing started
with great enthusiasm last Sunday, there is a
new bathing wrap of red and white Turkey
towelling. Another of blue and white-bordered
towelling is a useful and harmonious one. Both
are merely for wear between the water’s edge and
the dressing-tent.
The other afternoon I went in the Long Bay
tram to La, Perouse. As we sped past the
Female Penitentiary—the grim pile which houses
the most unpleasant of our female offenders —an
elderly person in my compartment seized the arm
of her companion and said : “ Fine building that,
ain’t it? It’s the Ladies’ Gaol.” It struck me as
a unique effort in the art of genteel expression.
Hotel Australia is wearing its new race dress.
Only it wears it inside, not out. In fact, the
dress may be said to wear the hotel. A mile of
carpet arrived by some recent liner, and now
rests, in pieces, on the various corridors. New
floor-frocks of soft, glowing colors are on the re-
ception and dining rooms, and now will the tail
of the restaurant gown have a fine relief. Some-
thing wonderful in the way of bridge-building is
to be done in the neighborhood of the smoking
gallery.
What may happen to a motorist: A Mosman
doctor, accompanied by his wife, was motoring
down that steep road that leads to the Spit, where
the punt chains lie low to upset your boat and
drown you. A tram and a cyclist were coming
towards him, and, in trying to do the right thing
by both, he struck the cyclist’s machine, The
rider was hoisted high into the air, then threw an
aerial somersault and fell into the lap of the
doctor’s wife. The surprise of the faller and the
fallen-upon was about equal. Fortunately, neither
was hurt.
Isn’t it Jerome K. Jerome who tells the story
of how he hunted for days for a method of curing
fits in cats, and finally found it at the end of a
cookery book ? After pondering over his dis-
covery, he came to the conclusion that it was the
only way the author could find of unburthening
his mind of the unusual bit of information. I
marvel that Savarkar, the Indian person who
printed his bomb-making manual in London and
posted it out to India, didn’t strike the idea of
masking his combustible knowledge with a couple
of hundred pages about flour and baking powder
and eggs. No detective would ever have found it
then. Which only proves once more how
variously-useful an animal woman really is; for
without women, of course, there wouldn’t be any
cookery books.
A small girl who signs herself Margaret Bal-
cxnqual, and who writes from Pier’s Hill, Edin-
burgh, has sent a box of pink heather “ pulled at
Quarri Wood, Elgin,” to one of The Bulletin’s
staff. It has retained its scent of sea and wind
(or, maybe, this is imagination), and is a remem-
brancer of Charlotte Bronte and grouse. I believe
the grouse eat the flowering heather, and I recall
that the Bronte girls, from their lonely house near
the graveyard on the Yorkshire moors, used to
look upon a wondrously beautiful scene when the
pink and white heather bloomed in the autumn
over an otherwise objectionable landscape. Little
Margaret Balcanqual is hereby thanked.
Yours affectionately,
AKENEHI.
When Minister Sammy Moore was re-
jected at Bingara (N.S.W.) he had been 20 years
in Parliament. His colleague, James Alex.
Hogue, who also went down, had just finished 16
years.
“ Pa ” Delohery, Sydney’s old magistrate,
represented by his eldest son, who is instructed
by another son, is suing the N.S.W. Government
for £5OOO, alleged arrears of pension money. The
case came on for the first round last week. The
State seems to have two defences —(1) that the
superannuation fund went bung, and so the Gov-
ernment isn’t liable, at any rate, for more than a
portion of the money; (2) that the Government gave
Pa Delohery a job as Public Service Commissioner
at £lOOO a year up to last January, and hopes he
doesn’t expect to draw his salaryfor his valuableser-
vices simultaneously with a pension granted on the
supposition that he was past work. As to this,
Pa Delohery and his boys have said nothing ; but
as tc the first point, they say that the fund went
bung because G. H. Reid flung a host of pen-
sioners on it who weren’t entitled to pensions at
the time. The Crown has demanded from the
Delohery family the names of these persons, and
they (the family) have been given a month to dig
them up.

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, October 17, 1910.
My Dear Yarrie,—
Caulfield Cup has always a fine day. Curious
how consistent the weather can be if it likes.
Nothing could have been more erratic than the
wild gusts, the hot puff«, the tropical rain and
the hail and sleet that forced itself on the notice
of even the most supercilious scorners of
weather moods up to Friday. Then suddenly
the elements pulled themselves together, and
Saturday was a great day entirely. All the
people—the sporting people—and all the sporting
people’s best clothes got an outing. Men made
dandies of themselves, and put on socks that only
a chord of music could describe. The new gloves
among men are chamois leather, just like the ones
Mary Ann wears when she polishes up the handle
of the big front door. With these paw-coverings
you wear (if you are a marl with any soul) it
buttbii-hble of cornflowers and baggy £>ants, tapef-
ing.iri at the ankle.
The women wore everything—positively every-
thing. Three in a row held a harvest festival on
their three hats. The collection included sheaves
of corn, stacks of flowers, and feathers of all the
birds that ever harried the early worm. Amaz-
ing what some women will put on. One, for
instance, boasts of her motor-cloak as being
made of hundreds of skins of “ unborn birds.”
She has forgotten the name of the bird, as it is
“almost extinct.” The garment may be made of
the cast-off skin of the sea serpent, for all I know
to the contrary ; but I am told that it is one of
only five such cloaks in the world. People who
own thrones and castles and bomb-proof shelters
have the others. It was not worn at the
races, as it is designed for motor-wear
only. I shuddered at a black hat with quite
50 white aigrettes. The sepulchre came down
to the shoulders of the destroyer who wore it,
completely hiding her face. In that one respect
it was a sensible piece of millinery.
Mummer Atwell brought a lovely girl in grey,
but she was almost hidden under her hat. Re-
cognition is as uncertain with this shoulder-
drooping headgear as if the girls were veiled in
true Turkish fashion. Mrs. Eugenie Duggan
AndersOn ivas irtost chastely subdued in black.
Black is such a smart Color. Mrs. Reggie Power
had a key-pattern, the color of skinned rabbit,
tattooed all over her tight dress. She carried a
lavender parasol lined with rose. If there is any-
thing that can beat the joyousness of a pink
parasol, it is One of this lilac color with the light
filtering through it. Mrs. Captain Polar-Scott
walked Out in a narrow mole-colored frock that
gave her a Bond-street air. Mrs. Syme showed a
cute little flat black bow on her hat, and the pret-
tiest neck imaginable. She wore creamy fixings,
and brought her sister along half-hidden under a
grey hat and roses.
The Burston ball was a gorgeous and defiant
mayoral swan-song. The Town Hall and all its
surroundings looked like a red-ant hill that some-
one had wickedly poked with a stick. Inside the
hall, Frank Walker, like Jinney the Djinn, had
made roses to grow from the ceiling, and japonica
to sprout from the balconies, while hollyhocks
flaunted where concert operators usually make
their noise. Hostess Mrs. Burston, slender and
handsome, looked like an American Trust
in gold and silver; gold flowers, crusted
on to silver net, were wound about silver-
grey satin. She danced with Governor Car-
michael in the first set, while Mayor Burston,
hot in his robes, led a cool Carmichael lady. The
Carmichael lady wore a coat of wonderful lace,
she had her white satin underdress tied round the
knees with black tulle, and some considerable
diamonds blazed for further effect. Captain
Scott paused long enough on his way to the Pole
to frivol through an evening’s dancing. He was
in civilian dress, with several small, but tremen-
dous medals pinned on his chest. His most
decorative possession was his wife—a sparkling
lady, whom General Hoad guided through the
official set. She wore a dull blue gown, glued
on to her; over this floated quite four
ghosts of gowns—filmy ninons in deeper and
deeper shades of sea-blue, with a turquoise film
fluttering somewhere. It was a poem of a dress.
Mrs. Justice Isaacs got herself into lurid reds and
wine color. Miss Burston, with Titian hair, wore
a white little dress—ankle short and fixed with
lilies.
The Mayoress-elect, Mrs. Davey, was at home,
mourning her mother’s death. Mrs. Madden,
\who has been a sad, black-clad figure lately, was
.also away. Daughter Beatrice, with laurels in
her hair, came with papa. John Madden is
growing Pickwickian with the two little tufts of
grey hair springing from either side of his shin-
ing, benevolent, bald head. Guy rolled round in
ibis kilt, like a big, gambolling, good-natured St.
Bernard puppy. Singer Maggie Stirling stood up
■out of the ruck like an Amazon, and Mrs. Campbell
■of much sheep-wealth waved metallic hair-fixings
dike a message to Mars. Mrs. Willie Sharp—black
.and blue in gowning—led the diamond brigade
.after the Carmichael lady. Mrs. Railway Tain
■broke out in huge black spots on a fresh-colored
silk. The Best lady made a re-appearance, fined
.down and thin after her illness. And there was
.also a great plenitude of pretty and decorative
Effirl.
St. Vincent’s ball shared in the gorgeous
ffroCking of the week. The depressing black and
i purple Court mourning has been cast off and every-
thing is sparkling. Silver and gold cover clothes
as if King Midas had been playing “touch.”
St. Vincent’s had so many sets that the ball
looked as if it were of the fancy species. The sets
were schemed for a big effect. Most of them took
a flower for a basis, with the blossom itself for a
cap. No matter how small it was, the girl who
“setted” it had to hide her hair under only one
bloom. The violetshad hard work tomagnify them-
selves sufficiently ; but it was managed somehow.
The nurses had a jolly set on their own all
in their smartest bib and tucker. The Frepch
Maids took the eye in the N whirl of the Lancers.
The Directoire plumped solidly for black and
white, and scored a striking effect. It was a
cheerful affair, and the balconies were crowded
with soberly-clad watchers, helping the hos-
pital with their looking-on fees. Secretary
Nat Fitzgerald, behind his monocle, skilfully
steered the enjoyable frivol, like the best chauffeur
that ever sat behind a glass screen.
The city newsboys, who from time unremem-
bered have had an annual ball to help their
finances, this year had a matinee instead. The
affair happened at the King’s on Friday afternoon.
The Carmichaels and a noble company occupied
a large segment of the front half-moon of the
dress-circle, and were jovial and interested. It
was very much their party. The newsboys are
a pet charity of Lady Carmichael, and she
had done her vice-regal best to get a good pay-
ing houseful. Aide Vaux worked like a tiger,
and helped secretary Miss Onions until a sprained
ankle laid him low. On Friday he was up and at
it, limp and all. Miss Greta Garnett had a party
which filled the front row of the stalls —mostly
j'Oung people of her Own number Of summers,
With sister Mrs. Svme as chaperone. The Frank
Madden’s fortified part of the house, and Mrs.
Staughton packed friends in another corner.
There was not breathing space for the unhappy
unbooked.
Every theatre “permitted” mummers to do a
charity stunt. All the present Melbourne plays
got in an act. Rickards sent Lorna and Toots
Pounds—the clever children who have been
mimicking current mummers and their manner-
isms at At Homes both here and in Sydney.
They wore simple white frocks that adver-
tised the good taste of the grown-ups behind
them. The two “Whip” leads broke into a play-
let of one act called “ The Fourth Kiss.” The
title sounds weakly sentimental, but the thing
was really powerful. The Kiss is a deathly
thing frorrij the ghost of an Asiatic god
which is pressed upon a too contemptuous
European; The little Dickenson maid from
the Mejmell and ClatkO Co.; growing into a
charming girl, danced a burlesque With Crom-
well, with her fascinating lint-white locks a-flow-
ing round her. Altogether, the programme had
more meat in it than one generally gets at a
matinee.
Henley-on-the-renovated-Yarra seems to be the
only reason why this week exists at all. House-
boats are being hammered into shipshape ready
for disguising flower screens. Girls lay them-
selves out to manufacture square miles of paper-
flowers, and so beat Creation at its own game.
Shops fill their windows with studied simplicity,
variously labelled Henley hats, Henley cushions,
Henley umbrellas (mostly paper) and fluttering
Henley scarves. People invited on to house-
boats, and the energetic crowds who “syn-
dicate ” for rowing boats and launches will
see the sights and the rowing—when they are
not being tipped into the water. The banks will
be swarming with people, thick as ants on a dis-
turbed hill-nest, who will pay a half-crown to
walk on the grass and look at houseboats from
the outside. As far as the marine workings are
concerned, the rule of the river is growing into a
bogey as big as the sea serpent, and seafarers will
have to mind their oars and propellers with
exquisite care if they want to avoid breaking the
rule in several places and being ruined with tines.
Calvd departed amidst a whirl of enthusiasm,
sending wireless messages in a private code from
her marvellous eyes. Galileo Gasparri also
seemed to be snapping off a few of his own as the
mailboat moved away.
Major Wilson, who promenaded so magnifi-
cently at Caulfield, departed by the India to the
Coral Strand, with several lily-fair hands waving
an affectionate farewell. Sister Mrs. Kathleen
Watson-Dearden piloted him tremulously round,
and let him peep only at the nicest girls—for the
Major is a desirable bachelor of 40 summers
(Indian ones mostly). The Colonel of his regi-
ment (54th Sikhs) is married to an Australian
girl; so the regiment turns its eyes this way
whenever matrimony is mentioned.
Some more than usually-noticeable good-byes
have been said to Miss Burleigh, the late matron
of the Melbourne Hospital. The whole hospital
staff, down to the pantry girls, found something
to press upon her as a memento. There is great
dignity attached to the high office of matron of a
big house of healing, for the one holding it has as
much responsibility as a captain of a ship. Un-
like most women in her position, Miss Burleigh
was not content merely to direct others. At the
time of the awful Sunshine smash she did a man’s
work out in the yards, lifting sorely-wounded men
and women from the ambulances. All the mem-
bers of the committee were aboard the boat that
carried her off, and had a last handshake. Presi-
dent Mrs. Godfrey, the committee member who
invited Miss Burleigh here 10 or more years ago,
was the last to say good-bye. The new matron, a
Miss Bell, is now on her way from Scotland.
Miss Darling, a well-known St. Kilda girl, who
recently went tripping to Java with her father,
basely deserted her adoring poppa in the Land
of the Nail, and entered into the bonds with a
fellow-passenger, Mr. Wills. Strangely enough,
the lucky man had spent 13 years in Melbourne
and had never met his future wife—which proves
once more that 13 is an unlucky number. Fate,
who arranges these matters, chose to wait until a
steamer was starting off to an odd corner of the
earth to bring them together. The two are
descending on Australia for the Flemington spree
next month.
On Thursday, the day this letter impacts your
eye, a wedding of interest to us will occur in your
Harbor city. Dr. Horace P. Godfrey, of St.
Kilda, marries Miss Pilcher, of Orange. Bride is
a sister of Mrs. (Dr.) Howse, of that fruitily-
named.city, and a niece of Pilcher, K.C. Bride-
groom is a son of Mr. George Godfrey, well known
to politics and the law.
Since his triumph of last week, William Moore
'is hatching bigger dramatic schemes, with the
Turn Verein for scenic background. The triolet
of plays —his own and those of Louis Esson and
Miss Pritchard—were given in the cosy German
hall. The next will be offered to a cafe chantant
audience out in the quaint gardens roundabout.
There is a long, wide verandah, high like a ter-
race, curving round from the main part of the
Turn Verein Club to the ballroom ; and this will
make a capital stage, with excellent exits for thb
mummers into the rooms behind. At the' last
show an orchestra played in the seductive little
gallery above the back of the hall. It was a
startlingly good orchestra, drawn probably from
several city bands at the call of the Turn Verein.
Antarctic Scott and wife—she is a nice-looking,
quite-young party—have just departed for your
city, and will put up at the Australia. He is of
the Shackleton build, clean shaven, and with
well-bred bulldog points. Dr. Simpson, who
registers “from Simla,” is with them.
The Victorian Artists’ Spring show will have
opened at the touch of Alfred Deakin by the time
this climbs into print. Wednesday, the 19th, is
the day fixed for the start of the exhibition, which
is to be held in the Upper Room of the Athenaeum
—nowadays the chief shrine of those who paint
and sculp.
, Maud-Grantham is obliged to .postpone her
'“KipTing'Night” at the Independent Hall from
October 21 to November 5. The date originally
fixed on had been dedicated to a children’s con-
cert, and Miss Grantham thought it wiser to
suffer a postponement than risk a clash.
Professor Lingham is to take the platform at
the Austral Salon once again —on Tuesday,
October 25. Faulkner Smith, who is managing
the scholarly entertainment, blows a trumpet for
the modest Lingham
To’see and hear this great artist in his magnificent im-
personation of Marcus Antonins and other characters is
a liberal education. He has a superb stage presence, is
endowed with the supreme gift of expression, is a subtle
tone-colorist, and has a voice that is an arrow for the
heart.
Which is a pretty warm personal reference for
poet Uingham. He will fire the arrow in three
monodramas, including “Saved by an Earth-
quake.”
A recent letter from Mrs. Doubleday, in Vienna,
to Mr. Oehr, in Melbourne, states that her
daughter Leila will study further before she gives
the concert which, according to her teacher s pre-
diction, will set Austria talking about the young
violinist. In anticipation of the success she is to
make in Vienna, there is talk of arranging a
London concert for Leila Doubleday next June.
Meanwhile, she is adding to her repertoire.
“During the summer holidays she has done the
Brahms concerto and the Lala symphony, and is
now doing the Beethoven concerto, writes mamma.
The distinction between holiday pastimes and
work-a-dav tasks is not very wide in Leila s case.
Blamire Young has finished up with a nicely
spotted collection of pictures. The show was
very much alive on its last day ; but in tnese ex-
traordinarily artistic times it is impossible to wring
the last guinea from the laggard purchaser, as the
showing place is booked for other hangings, and
must be vacated promptly. This is the city
where people cried aloud that there was no
market for Australian work in Australia ! iSow
We ha've two picture halls going solidly—the
upper room of the Athenaeum and the Guild Hall.
And both of them are booked well ahead. Blamire
Young this time painted as he sees, instead of
posterising his pictures into charming but con-
ventional schemes. Stolid city people say lie has
idealised. Evidently they do not know their
own country. Probably some of them have never
had a real good look at it; for one may easily cut
across vast stretches of country by train and see
nothing.
Jennie Brennan has her small fry in hand for
another opera. The Foundling Home is quite a
boomed charity now ; but Miss Brennan and her
children-dancers are old-time supporters. It is
two years now since they gave their first perform-
ance in aid of the unclaimed and homeless babies.
Sometime in December the next child-opera will
happen ; the youngsters are busy on rehearsal
already, and the Brennan clan have great enter-
tainment, as well as hard work, watching the
small mimics. A feather in Jennie Brennan’s cap
is the success of Addie Hine in London. She was
engaged for solo-dance work in principal panto-
mime only a fortnight after her arrival in Eng-
land. Royce, the stage manager, refused to
allow her to take any more lessons other than
some he gave her himself. “ The tuition she had
had in Australia,” he said, “possessed an indi-
viduality that must not be spoilt.” So Miss
Jennie is modestly elated.
The designing art of Desbrowe Annear was
lately employed in the transformation of the
old lounge and winter garden at Menzies’
into a large hall, which will be subdivided by
accordeon doors when occasion requires. The
ball is a beautiful improvement to Melbourne’s
toniest hostelry. Its decorations are an adapta-
tion of the Renaissance style, with a color scheme
of . old gold and sage green. Phil Goatcher has
painted the ceiling panels, and another artistic
worker claims the authorship of two domes of
leaded glass in browns and light greens. For the
accordeon doors aforesaid, Blamire Young designed
the grilles in the upper panels. The new hall
and all that is strike one with a sense of
harmony, and suggest American up-to-dateness.
And the" up-to-dateness runs also to the ventilation.
Under the Plemim system the breathing material
is first scrubbed and polished before being driven
in, cold or hot, as the seasons suggest.
From “Rosna,” of Adelaide:—■
His Ex. has opened yet another country show. Also,
on Monday night he exchanged his cocked hat for his
stars and garters, and went to a ball at Unley. It was an
army ball, and hence, in addition to floriculture and
banners, there was a brave array of military frocking and
gold lace. The Lady Excellencies helped the warriors
through the intricacies of the grand chain.
Some 50 white-robed angels warbled beneath the
baton of our new singing maestro, Mr. Winsloe Hall, at
the Conservatorium on Monday. Part-songs of assorted
sizes were soulfully given ; also a cantata, with the un-
Christian name of “ Johanistag.” A new young voice
appeared in one of the solos. It is the property of that
slim and graceful person, May Chamberlain, a sister of
tenor and newspaper-man Jimmy Chamberlain. The
voice is a pure, sweet, lyric soprano. A Van Senden
damsel managed another solo rdle, and Mr. Lionel Clark,
the one valorous male on the cantata’s landscape, set
the world a shining example by his fine enunciation. In
fact, this feature was conspicuous with all the performers.
To hear what a chorus happens to be talking about is an
unexpected luxury, and one to be encouraged.
While Eight Hours’ Day was raging in this City of Cul-
ture last Wednesday, we had the tail-end of the Spring
meeting at Morphettville. All the frocks and frills had
seen the light of day before, so there was nothing very
exhilarating.about the fixture except the effort of Gunna,
a kindly steed who paid his devotees a little dividend of
£24.
Adelaide Town Hall has now reached the advanced
age of 44, and the authorities begin to think the
shghtly-increased population justifies a few small ad-
ditions and a general hair-curling and face-polishing.
For instance, there ought to be a clock in the tower to
run races with the Post Office clock opposite ; but the eye-
socket designed for the clock hasbeen boarded upsince the
edifice was built, and we really don’t think a blind Town
Hall, with a common wooden shade over its eye, is worthy
of us. The Prince Alfred Hotel next door has a face that
matches the Town Hall’s ; and it has been proposed,
when the lease runs out, not to let it come in again,
but to absorb the Prince Alfred for municipal purposes.
However, this millennium doesn’t happen until 1914,
alack ! Meanwhile, the Town Hall is in the grip of pic-
ture show after picture show, and the musical societies
that can’t get in to wail to us for love or murder,
have been bombarding the Village Grandfathers with
protests. They threaten to go out into the highways
and hedges and build an opposition hall if the said V.G.’s
don’t push out the picture-shows and let the musicians
wail on occasion. But the city parents sadly wave the
bank account and point out that picture-shows pay
thousands in' rental, and thousands are useful things.
They will make roads and bridges, which are of more solid
comfort to the'citizens than the best of classic noise.
There is plenty of room in Adelaide for a new concert-
hall, anyway, most of the existing samples being cracked
in the acoustic properties.
Mrs. Harry Dutton, daughter-in-law of the Anlaby
Nabob, is being photographed by London papers as the
Australian Beauty. She was the elder daughter of the
Gawler Martin family, and went to England to see sister
Jean do her preliminaries on the British stage.
Pianist Arthur Williamson, just back from Europe,
brings news of that jolly and clever soprano girl, Clytie
Hine. She is still pursuing her scholarship at the British
Royal College of Music, and is adding all sorts of lustre to
her pretty voice. . J

PALE, WEAK WOMEN
Will Benefit by the Experience
of this Woman who
Regained Health*
Mrs. Blanche Kingsley, Ferguson Street, Glen
Innes, found her health in a state of as
the result of an anaemic, bloodless condition. She
remained bad until she tried Dr. Williams’ Pink
Pills, which renewed her blood supply. Mrs.
Kingsley gives the following account of her
cure :
“I became anaemic as a girl, and I could not
pick up strength,” she said. “My blood turned
to water ; not a scrap of color showed in my lips
or cheeks; dark rings came under my eyes, and
when I pulled down the lids I could see no rosi-
ness. By 10 o’clock each morning I would feel
so utterly done up and languid I would have to
lie down if I had a chance, I dreaded doing any-
thing, as I had not the heart. I never ate a good
meal, yet I fancied the strangest things, such as
a scrap of burnt meat, or lemon or pickles or any-
thing very sweet. My feet and hands were
always stone cold. I could not walk a short
distance without my heart thumping furiously.
My head would ache in the temples day and
night, so that I would feel quite dizzy and con-
fused and heavy, and for six months my eyes
were so dim that everything near or far was
smudgy and hazy, and I could not do one scrap
of sewing or reading. Mynerves were all unstrung.
I would start at any noise, and I did not want
anyone to come near or worry me. My hands
were waxy and the nails were blue underneath.
I tried all sorts of tonics, but got no relief. On a
hot day I would be quite played out, and it was
not till I tried Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills that my
health picked up at all. I would faint right off
at times I was so weak, but since I finished the
last box I haven’t had a sign of ansemia. All the
distressing symptoms have gone, and I am now
in excellent health.”
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills are 2s. 9d. a box, six
boxes 15s. 3d., of all dealers, or from The Dr.
Williams’ Medicine Co. of Australasia, Ltd.,
Sydney.

POLITICAL
POINTS
If it keeps going as it is going at present,
Bananaland will be entitled to a tenth member in
the House of Reps, next general election.
Thomas, the Commonwealth’s P.M.G.,
knocked a few inches more off the top of the State
boundary-walls last week. The uniform Austra-
lian postage-stamp hasn’t arrived yet; but from
this out any one State’s penny stamp is good for
a penn’orth of postal work in any other State.
And the Minister has his postal Bill ready, to
establish penny postage right through the Com-
monwealth.
Both the S. M. Herald and Telegraph
leaped to their feet after the N.S.W. election to
point out the great advantage enjoyed by the
Laborites through their members being all mere
professional politicians, who'can stay in the House
day and night if necessary, while the Liberal
members have their businesses to look after.
That was just a few days after these same papers
had denounced Labor for putting up as candi-
dates barristers, farmers and other people with
private businesses to attend to. The Tory paper
is mighty difficult to please.
It was characteristic of Premier Wade
that he should take his wife to one of his political
meetings, and when a few people boo-hooed
should assume that they were groaning at her,
and discharge great toothfuls of language to the
press about their alleged lack of chivalry. Some
people always boo-lioo when they see Wade,
while others weep bitterly. The former probably
had no idea that the lady was brought as a pro-
tection, and that any common ordinary disarrange-
ment of sounds made that night would be charged
against her. How were they to know ? Notone
in 100 was aware of her name.
The Westralian Tory Ministry pulled
through its vote of censure by a majority of one.
Just before the vote was taken a rash Labor
member sassed the Speaker, and as he refused to
apologise he was ordered to leave the precincts of
the House. His attitude was that if the precincts
felt that they and he couldn’t get on together
they might go, but he wouldn’t. Then the mem-
ber and the Sergeant-at-arms fought three badly-
selected rounds, in the course of which the official
was severely grassed, but at last the member
went out. And that was why Labor was a vote
short.
For several reasons Westralia’s new Pre-
mier has been christened Ratcatcher Wilson.
Two of the reasons are in the Cabinet.
The mere shadow of the Federal Land
Tax served to burst up four estates in the Vic.
Western district the other day. Three of them
belonged to the Manifolds, and one to a wealthy
Warrnambool grazier, J. W. Anderson. The
operation was simple and instantaneous. All the
land in question was in the occupation of tenants.
The owners met the tenants ; an agreement as to
the price was come to. and the business was done.
And to-day there are about 40 more freehold
farmers in the district than there were.
The discussion on the Navigation Bill in
the Senate has been a bad advertisement for the
Tory character. Every proposed provision for
the care or protection of life at sea has roused a
lament on the sacrifice that the shipowner will have
to make to meet the comfort of his crews or the
convenience of his passengers. The Bill’s effort to
provide reasonable berthage for the mariners, or
to ensure that steamers shall have watertight
compartments, is regarded as a plot to reduce
Fat’s cargo space and bite off some of his sacred
profits.

Under all (he assumption of blustering cocksureness by

Premier Murray’s Factories Amending
Bill provides a penalty of £5O or three months’
gaol for employers convicted of dismissing em-
ployees on account of their connection with a
Wages Board. This has roused the anger of the
Vic. Employers’ Federation, which is spluttering
hvsterically about the “ deliberate insult to re-
putable persons. Nobody has so far remarked
that the 21 years provided for burglary, or the
hanging provided for murder is a reflection on
reputable persons. Which suggest that the people
whom the Vic. Federation is really concerned
about are not the reputable but the disreputable
employers.
It is now up to Toryism in N.S.W. to
cast round for a new alias —its invariable habit
when it encounters disaster under the old one.
From a descriptive standpoint, the most honest
title the party ever owned was Freetrade —though
N.S.W. never knew a genuine Freetrader outside
an asylum. The anti-Socialist cry was palpably
bogus, which accounted for its early demise and
the determination of Toryism not to call itself
“ anti-Socialist ” any more. The word “ Liberal,”
as applied to folks like 0. G. Wade, is grotesque,
and last week the State of N.S.W. said so as
explicitly as it could. Heaven knows what
Reaction’s next titular disguise is going to be, but
there is a cheering certainty about the fact that it
can’t be as unconvincing as the last two anyhow.
“Boswell,” his parable :
During the week I saw the opening of Lismore (N.S.W.)
Circuit Court. An accused person was presented, and
presently convicted. Then did one seize a ponderous
tome and read therefrom a damaging list of delinquencies
on the part of him who stood in the dock, the same run-
ning back several years. I looked for him to rise and
loudly proclaim that these statements were “ diabolical
lies,” “ calumnv,” “misrepresentation”; that, any-
how, they would soon be forgotten, wherefore all this
fuss and muck-raking were most unseemly, and, surely,
greater matters demanded attention ; besides which one
of the robberies happened, nor, on August 29, as stated,
but on August 28, wherefore it hadn’t happened at all,
and those who still insisted on its occurrence were
venomous slanderers ; in consequence of all of which he
claimed discharge with a clean bill of health, and would
requisition the Daily Tklkgrapii cartoonist to draw his
persecutors in a pursuing attitude with buckets of mud
in their hands. But nothing of the sort happened. And
lo ! in a little while he was led away, and I saw him no
more.
A FAIR EXCHANGE.
‘ People rush into my vestry every now and then to
tell me they are going to Korea,” said Rev. John Fer-
guson to the Presbyterian Assembly. “They find me as
cold as ice. Isay to them, ‘Well, God bless you; but
when you do arrive see if you can send someone from
Korea to do the work of the same character that is wait-
ing to be done in Australia.’ ’’—News Item.
Some State railway employees in South
Australia recently got a small increase in their
screws, and forthwith those of them who
lived in Private Enterprise houses at Petersburg
received the following from their landlords
Take notice that, from October Ist, all rents will be
advanced Is. Id. per week. This is on account of the
advance of Is. per day being made in your wages.
Within about four minutes—less or more—of the
above document being brought to the notice of
Labor Premier Verran, that tyrannous person
announced in the Assembly that he had arranged
with the Railwav Commissioners to spend £BOOO
in building State cottages for the Petersburg em-
ployees. It was a beautiful example of the lengths
to which a Labor Government will go in inter-
fering with benev’olent EriVcite Enterprise,
vet, strange to say, not a solitary Liberal can-
didate even mentioned the matter during the
N.S.W. elections.

DEFINITE.
The questions to be asked in connection with the
census are now under consideration. It has been
decided ttiat the religion of each person shall be one of
them. There will be a proviso that any person who does
not care to disclose his religion may decline to do so by
writing in the word “ object.”
Census Collector : “ And what is your re-
ligion 1 ”
The Other: “Well, I dunno; yer heller put
me down as a hobject.”
London Referee, in predicting an early
conquest of Australia by the Yellow Races,
remarks that:—
When a population scarcely exceeding the half of
Greater London owns an empty continent, the glamor of
the landowner surrounds manual laborers like a halo.
Pastoralists are patricians.
Also the same writer alleges that—
The Sydney Bulletin, one of the ablest Socialistic
oversea newspapers, teaches its subscribers that the
brain and pluck of Australia are sufficient to keep the
world at bay.
London Referee says many wise things; but its
information on this'matter has worms in it. In
the view of the Australian manual laborer the
big landowner isn’t an angel with a halo; he is
much more like “a cow ’’—either with or without
a spiked tail. Also, The Bulletin only teaches
its subscribers (and borrowers) that the brain and
pluck of Australia are sufficient to keep the world
at bay when there is a vastly greater number of
Australians to own the brain and the pluck.
The manifesto issued by the N.S.W. Pre-
mier on the eve of the election was a marvel of
dubious grammar and hazy platitude. He didn’t
tell the people whether he was going to build the
Sydney city railway and the connection with
North Shore; or whether he would allow the
people of the metropolis to decide the Greater
Sydney question by referendum; or whether he
would open the Closed Ports and build railways
to them ; or how he was going to pay the famous
railway and tramway pensions; or what his
views were on making an attempt
to pay off the public debt. But he
stated in nine paragraphs that he
favored (1) industrial progress; (2)
financial stability ; (3) land for
settlement; (4) freehold tenure ;
(5) a progressive public works
policy; (6) maintenance of law
and order ; (7) clean politics; (8)
maintaining the Federal Constitu-
tion ; and (9) “ Liberalism.” And
all these things were only footless
ghosts walking upside-down in the
sky. The nearest he came to any-
thing definite was when he said that
he was in favor of (4) freehold
tenure. And even then he didn’t
state clearly whether he believed in
giving State leaseholders the free-
hold of their lands at £1 an acre
when the land is really worth £5
to £l5. Also, he quite forgot to
mention that he favored (10) Heaven ;
(11) avoidance of premature burial ; (12)
general happiness; (13) a "sufficient rainfall ; (14)
the conversion of the Devil.
Two chief factors in the Wade debacle on
(Friday—Wood and Coal.
A statement that grows rather wearisome
is that of Defence Minister Pearce re the cost of
the Australian fleet. It is to consist, he says, of
one armored cruiser, three cruisers of the Bristol
type, six destroyers and three submarines, and
the bill is to be £3,695,000. This has been men-
tioned so often that many people begin to have
an idea that when £3,695,000 has been paid for 13
vessels the whole business is completed and done
with. It would be better to explain frankly that
the Australian fleet will consist of a num-
ber of vessels of which no man can see the
limit, that the bill is wholly in the hands
of Providence, and that only the long, bound-
less wash of events upon the shore of
history can decide whether we may not have to
maintain 70 big ironclads some day, and be darned
glad to do it. The blessed “ unit ”is a beginning,
like the early British warship which the crew
took home on his shoulder at night and used as a
pillow, and the end is only limited by the size of
our necessity. The gods may know it, but even
that is doubtful, unless the gods have an extra-
special telescope. Certainly £3,695,000 has
nothing to do w T ith the case.
A determining factor of great tonnage in
last week’s polling in N.S.W. was a single word—
wowser. It is hard to ever-estimate the influ-
ence of a descriptive phrase which really describes.
“Wowser” instantaneously presents a fully-
developed life-sized portrait of a dreary
monkey-shaven fraud, with a collection-trough
in one hand, an anti-everything brick
in the other, and a half-empty bottle
of gin projecting from his coat-tails. Ever since
the word was coined the electors have been busy
hurling out of Parliament members whom it
seemed to fit; and in the Local Option poll
thousands voted “Continuance” because the
wowsers of their acquaintance yelped for thrip-
pences and “No License” with almost equal
enthusiasm.
Someone seems to be pulling a very power-
ful string in Melbourne. Though the Powlett
State coal mine is now able to turn out about
2000 tons daily, and though the railways can carry
at least that much, the output is being limited to
1300 tons, equal to 1000 tons of screened coal per
day. Yet the railways are stacking up huge re-
serves at Spencer-street, and to do this are taking
Newcastle coal in almost as large quantities as
before Powlett opened. Seeing that “ Powlett ”
is some 6s. a ton cheaper than Newcastle, this
action of the Railway Department calls for more
explanation than has been given so far. Mean-
while, the general public, which can’t get a,ny
Powlett coal at all, except a trifle of slack, owing
to the obstreperousness of the Fat Landlords, con-
tinues to pay an unnecessary 10s. a ton to Messrs.
Barabbas and Co., coal and shipowners.
“ Steelsmifch ” sniffs a new danger
It is cabled that the Pacific Slope Chambers of Com-
merce are sending a large delegation to China to make
an inquiry. The decline in China is at the back
of the jaunt. Take the most important example to
realise what it means. Four years ago America started
to get pig-iron from China (Hankow district) in notable
quantities. Pig-iron in China has ever since kept steady
at about 16 silver taels per ton. This meant 15 gold dol-
lars in 1906 ; but now it only means 10 gold dollars.
Consequently, Chinese pig-iron is rushing into America.
The Western Steel Co. (Seattle, U.S.) orders 3,000,000 tons
of pig from Hankow. The U.S. Steel Corporation, the
biggest on earth, is about to establish works in China.
Note the cable message that Schwab, the bitter rival of
the Corporation as its sacked boss, has secured the
making of the Chinese battleships and cruisers. This will
be with Chinese steel, and involves his establishing works
in China. So there will be a Chow Pittsburg springing up.
All of which screeches at the top of its voice for
various things, but chiefly for High Protection in
white men’s countries and prompt steps to better
defend Australia against people who will become
a greater danger with every ton of steel they
make.
B. R. Wise would be invaluable to the
N.S.W. Labor party in the Upper House, which
is bound to prove obstructive. Curiously enough,
he appears to be acceptable to the fossil-growth
there, despite the deplorable Radicalism of his
views; it is questionable, in fact, whether any
other politician could have accomplished his feat
of engineering the Arbitration Act through the
reactionary Chamber.
THE SLANDEROUS LABORITE AND THE FAIR-
MINDED LIBERAL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
No man or woman having any sense of honor or decency
left, and knowing how the game is being played by the
Laborites would give a vote in their favor. Their utter de-
pravity is appalling, and if the country is going to be
governed by people so lost to all sense of honor, decency or
morality, Heaven help it! It is time that every person in
the country with any sense of decency rose up in righteous
indignation against the unscrupulous band of vile con-
spirators who are threatening their country with destruc-
tion. It is a crisis in the history of Australia. It is simply
a question as to whether all honor, decency and morality
are to be sunk in favor of vice, immorality and unscrupu-
lousness. It is a question as to whether this country is
going forward to its great destiny, or is to be handed
over to the vilest band of conspirators known in history
since the days of Catiline. —“ Common Decency,” in S. M.
Herald.
See where the loathly Laborite
Stands, in his garb of shame :
A monument of infamy,
A type whose evil fame
Shocks all right-thinking Liberals,
So that they curse his name.
Think of his callous viciousness !
To him.the Marriage Tie
Means absolutely nothing. He
Delights to drink and lie
And kick his wretched wife ; then bolt
And leave her there to die.
The Laborite’s spare time is spent
Conspiring to destroy
The welfare of his fellow-men.
It is his wont to toy
With bombs and such-like lethal goods:
They fill his soul with joy.
His utter lack of decency
And honor have been made
As clear as inud, repeatedly.
He shamelessly betrayed
The State of New South Wales, and hurled
Dead cats at Mister Wade.
Two thousand years, and odd, have passed
Since Lucius Catiline
Made Rome a moral Hell on Earth.
We do not carp or whine ;
All we observe is : “ Would that it
Were b.c. sixty-nine 1”
The old-time Roman criminal
Was mild and just and good
Compared witli modern Labor men,
Who’ve raised the price of food
And swamped the State in Vice, Lies, Crime,
Oaths, Calumny and Blood.
The Liberal is fair and straight;
He seeks not to enthuse
The waning feeling for his cause
By virulent abuse.
For libels, mud and such-lilce things
He lias no sort of use.
Accordingly you find, that when
He feels impelled to state
His views, he does not utter words
Of calumny and hate.
No, no. His language always is
Quite calm and moderate.
N.S.W.
H. SINGEMDINE.
