SPORTING
NOTIONS
The Coongy Handicap, on the second day of
the Cup meeting at Caulfield, has often been a reliable
test of “ form.” Last year, when Dhobi and Woorooma
ran first and second, the field was of poor quality ; but
this year a lot of noteworthy Cup “fancies” saddled up
for the little event, and the”result should be worth con-
sideration. The closeness of the finish between Eye
Glass, Flavian, Artillerie and Langloh—heads and heads
—suggested that very little superiority in stamina would
give any one of them a decided advantage in the big han-
dicaps. On the other hand, the chances of Balgowan,
Matouree and Aborigine were heavily discounted by their
poor performances, although the black horse, Aborigine,
did make a bit of a show early in the race. Hoax, who
had been much talked of for the Caulfield Cup before
running like a non-stayer on the previoils Saturday,
shaped badly again in the Coongy. Nervous, fretful and
‘■flash,” Hoax seems more likely to score a surprise when
she runs unbacked than to carry the confidence of the
stable first past the post from a crowded field of triers.
* * * »
The Caulfield Cup race was not only a record as
regards the time for that particular event; it was also
the best contested Cup that ever was from the public’s
point of view. Out of 28 starters, fully 16 were declared
to be “good things,” and the race was practically con-
fined to these. They didn’t all run prominently at one
or other point of the struggle (two or three of them,
notably Artillerie, got badly away and could never
emerge from the ruck), but they supplied the
dangerous division all the time. The other dozen
were never in it, though a couple of them may
have finished in front of some that were easing off.
Of the four that fell only Call Boy had been seriously
backed, and he was beaten ere he came down in con-
junction with the penalised Eric and a couple of rank
outsiders. Considering the number of hopeless chances
in the crowded field, calculated to cause interference and
add to the inglorious uncertainty of the proceedings,
it was remarkable that the two popular favorites
at the start—Olivaster and Flavinius—both finished
in the first four, and that the Sydney horse
won so comfortably from Apple Pie (the sixth
favorite), who had been his immediate follower
in the Epsom Handicap at Randwick a fortnight
earlier. The Caulfield Cup, as it happened, vvas in strict
accordance with recent Randwick “form.” Punters
who believed only in what J. J. Miller’s latest
edition of the “Sporting Pamphlet” told them, and took
it for granted that the Sydney horses were more forward
in condition than the Victorian lot, rightly assumed that
Flavinius, Apple Pie and Britain were three of the best
handicapped horses in the race. The argument that
wherever one was the other two couldn’t be far away,
proved to be sound reasoning. One of them won the
Cup and the other two got the places. The luck of the
race tended to a vindication of recent form.
Two or three predictions by experts were ful-
filled. A Melbourne trainer said that the pace would be
too hot for the heavyweights from the jump. As a
matter of fact, all the running was made oy lightweights.
Sequarious, Danaus, Captain White and Eye Glass were
in front for the first nine furlongs or more. Eye Glass
lasted longest of the quartette, and was actually in front
at the home turn, where Olivaster, his stable companion,
joined him. Then Apple Pie made a run, and just as he
was tackling Olivaster there came Flavinius between
them.
If there was any bumping to speak of in the
straight, little Apple Pie and Eye Glass got the worst of
it, probably. Anyhow, Flavinius went ahead (his run
had been r well timed), Olivaster appeared to tire, and
Britain, coming with a very late run, pipped him for
third place. Olivaster was placed fourth, and Eye Glass
finished next to him. None of the others can be said to
have had a chance at any time, not as the race was run.
The early pacemakers, Sequarious and Danaus, had spun
themselves out. Which also was according to prediction.
There was absolutely nothing to be said against
Flavinius (Bst. lib.) as a “good thing,” except that in
previous tries for big handicaps the consistent animal had
always seemed to be not quite good enough. In last
Newmarket Handicap he carried Bst. 91b. into third place.
At Randwick a fortnight ago he was second for the Epsom
with Bst. 61b. up, and third in the Metrop. with 41b. less
on his back. His owner was said to be complaining that,
what with bad luck and other bad things appertaining to
horse-racing, he was getting tired of the game. Apple
Pie was another that had been running into places with
monotonous persistency, but on his performance in the
Waverley Handicap a week previously, where with 7st. 81b.
up he ran Britain (7st. 91b.) a close race, he seemed bound
to turn the tables on the Grafton horse at Caulfield.
Worth noting that tlie three first horses in the
Caulfield Cup had only lately arrived from Sydney. The
long journey is generally supposed to be an upsetting in-'
cident; but, seemingly, the change of air is likely to.
prove an ample equivalent. Given a fairly comfortable
trip, the gee gains more than it loses by being transferred
from Sydney to Melbourne close on the time for a Cup
race. If it causes a slight pause in its training, the holi-
day is beneficial.
Considered as a guide to the Melbourne Cup
tlie big race at Caulfield leaves Britain and Olivaster withi
better chances than Apple Pie and Flavinius, both of
which w'ere more favorably handicapped for the Urn.
race in the first instance. The latter, with a 101 b. penalty,
will haveBst. 91b. to hump, and, although he is a big horse,
Bst. 91b. is a fair lot for him on his best form. Flavin-
ius, moreover, has never shaped like a 2-miler. Britain,,
who has already won over lfm., will meet him on 101 b.
better terms, and so will Olivaster. Also, they will
both meet Apple Pie—a very doubtful stayer—
on 61b. better terms than their Caulfield Cup handicap.
Between Olivaster and Britain the question is open to
dispute. The Sydney horse has the more stamina on
paper; but he was probably quite fit last Saturday, whilst.
Scobie’s overrated favorite was apparently short of work.
Want of condition may have stopped Olivaster in the
Caulfield straight. If so, he will have to come on rapidly
before November 1- Seeing that the second-rate Britain
looks the most promising of the Caulfield Cup horses for
the two-mile go at Flemington, and that Comedy King is
not yet a proved stayer, the chance of Trafalgar is very
blooming just now. His credentials are undeniable.
The two-year-old racing at'Caulfieid indicated:
that most of the youngsters might as well have stopped
in their stables for all the chance they had, or were even
supposed to possess. In the Debutant Stakes, the three
favorites filled the places in the order of the betting at the
start. In the Gwyn Nursery, last Saturday, the betting
was 3 to 1 against the favorite and 7 to 2 against two
others. The winner and runner-up were among these
three fancies.
* * * *
The centenary of the first organised races in
Sydney occurred last week. The meeting took place on
what is now Hy'de Park, but which, until the last quarter
century, was known as the Racecourse. The course was
formed by the officers of the 73rd Regiment; most of the
regiment was camped at the time on the Liverpool-
College-street end of the park. The sports were con-
tinued for three days, and were followed by a ball. Three
Plates were run for, valued at 50 guineas each. The
grandstand was on a spot near the Market-street of to-
day. The dinner was a great success ; there were nine
set toasts and a few extras.
* * * *
The S. Aus. Derby (250 sovereigns added to a
sweepstakes of five sovs.) was put off from September 1
to mid-October, to catch the new Eight-Hour holiday. It
resulted in the most astonishing turn-up on record. S.
D. Brunton’s Isothermal went over from Melbourne to
win it, and was backed down to five to four in a field of
12. In the fastest-run race on local record, the favorite
looked all right till well up the straight, when a little
locally-bred colt, Sergius (Koran—Lulu) came from no-
where in particular and won by more than a length.
What would Moses have laid against the outsiders in a
field of 12? Twenty-fives? Forties, perhaps. Well,
Sergius paid £59 3s. There was another starter
that would have returned over £5O, and one that would
have paid £330 ! Further, the backers of Isothermal got
their money back, or thereabouts. (Are you listening,
Isaac?) His dividend for running second was 15s. for
each £1 invested. In the two days £25,000 went
through the totalisator, and the fact that this is nearly
three times the sum taken in 1906 proves that racing is
booming in S.A., or else that the bookmaker has been care-
fully squashed—probably both.
* * * *
Stanley Ketcliell, once looked upon as a world-
beater, recently went to pieces physically, and had prac-;
tically retired, perhaps for good, from the ring when he
was butchered. He called off the fight with Lang be-
cause he was quite unfit to even train, let alone fight;]
so retired for a long rest. He was only 23, but si nee he lost
his little manager, Willis Britt, he has been living at rail-
road speed, burning the candle at both ends and letting
rats eat it in the middle. His battle with Johnson
seemed to break up both Britt and Ketchell,
driving one to the grave and the other to drink.
Ketchell had fought about 60 battles, and accumulated
quite a tidy little fortune among them all, which fortune
manager Britt had invested rather well for him. But
popular report, which is generally a liar, credited him with
practically nothing nowadays, and accused him of having
“ blewed ” his whole substance. If what is said is anywhere
near the truth, Ketchell would have had to get work
immediately his “ long holiday ” was over. They said the
same about little Joe Gans, and when his affairs were
cleaned up it was found that he was worth nearly £14,000,
of which £2OOO was in hard cash at the bankers, £2OOO in
the form of diamonds, and the rest household property,
mostly saloon. The idea of boom, glitter and bust seems
to appeal to the Yankee writers.
The “long holiday” had commenced on the
ranch of some millionaire admirer, and Ketchell was en-
gaged in the simple life when he had a row with the
hired male help. The hired help seems to have been of a
broody disposition, and, having slept on his grievance,
he arose with butchery in his heart. He invaded the
breakfast-room with a rifle and shot Ketchell as that
young man was trying to escape. Ketchell lived long
enough to tell how it happened, and then passed out in
an odor of respectability. The millionaire host imme-
diately offered a huge reward for the murderer’s dead
body, but stipulated that he wouldn’t give a cent for the
live body. That killer is in for a bad time.
* * * *
Seems that Lang has been taken in hand by
Gentleman Jim Corbett, who has “been drumming
aggressiveness into him.” That was always the trouble
with Lang. When he had his man beat, he usually stood
off and waited to see him fall; and the brute promptly
revived and clinched. But Lang’s real trouble is that left
hand, which he has broken altogether this time. That is
going to be a lifelong drawback. He can get out of his
Peace Society methods of fighting, but once a bruiser’s
mauley has been splintered it is never again quite the
same.
* * * *
The latest yarn with regard to the downfall of
Jeffries is that two of the five doctors who examined him,
according to law, declared him suffering from intense
nervous strain and physically unfit to fight. Then it was
pointed out that, uniess they gave a unanimous certifi-
cate that Jeffries was fit, there would be frightful
losses and probably a riot. So, in the end, the two
medical men went back on their opinions and gave the
certificate. The result was that Jeffries went into the
ring unfit, and got Sheol. This yarn is somewhat
elaborated by other authorities, who state that Johnson
had secretly agreed to lie down in return for the lion’s
share of the boodle, and that the Governor of California,
findingthis out, blocked the fight. The Governor of Nevada
then offered to allow the fight to take place at Reno on
condition it was square. All parties agreed, and 4S hours
before the scrap Johnson asked Jeffries to agree to a
different division of the gate, as the fight had to be on
the square. This was the first intimation that Jeffries
had that Johnson was likely to give him the double-
cross, and he was flabbergasted, started worrying and by
the time of the scrap was a nervous wreck. What hits
the average dense-headed citizen in the eye is why men,
working on crosses and double-crosses, trust each other.
One would think that a gentleman of the rank and per-
fume of Mr. Jeffries would have trained hard and been in
perfect nick, so that, in case of Johnson trying to cross
or double-cross, he would be in a condition to hammer
the black caitiff into mush.
* * * *
Johnson is under the impression that boxing is
doomed in Yankeeland and will be shut down on alto-
gether even as horse-racing was in New York. He opines
that England and Australia are the coniing centres of the
world’s great bruising areas. Quite evident that Johnson
didn’t test the lung power of the wowsers during his stay
in Australia, or he would never have made that remark.
* * * *
“Pompadour Jim ” Corbett is wandering round
the S ates talking of “the Horror of Reno,” and inci-
dentally letting out that the Yank is no sport. This
from his own lecture: “As we sat in the corner,
Jeff and Tagreed upon another thing. I said : ‘Jeff, this
fellow Johnson likes to kid when he’s fighting, and he’ll
talk his head off if there’s anyone to talk to. Now, I’U
get him going right away, and say things to him that
will demand an answer. When he starts talking to me
you hook him with the left, and we’ll all be home in 10
minutes.’ Well, I was there with the gab, but Jeff
wasn’t there with the wallop.”
* * * *
A hundred yards’ race at the Adelaide Eight
Hours’ sports this year brought out 11 members of the
State Parliament, including some of the Ministry. Blun-
dell, Jackson and O’Loghlin were the placed men. That
field ouo-ht to be about a record for a Parliamentary race
* * *
S. Aus. football has lingered late this year.
Sturt was ahead on the season’s play, and, going down
before Port Adelaide in the “final,” had the right to
challenge again. This extra-final resulted in the same
way, and Port deservedly finished top, with 14 games won
and two lost. Sturt’s figures were 12 to four; but this
team was never beaten by any except Ports, while the
latter were not quite so deadly against the weaker clubs.
It is the first time Sturt has risen as high as the final;
also West Torrens had never before been as high as third.
The District system—pretty rigidly enforced in Adelaide
—is levelling up all the clubs. Norwood (which once,
long ago, held the premiership six years running) was
fourth, and West Adelaide, the champion of the past two
seasons, could only 7 get fifth among the seven competing.
In Sydney cricket on Saturday the trundlers
had a more or Iless joyous day of it for the first tune this
season In the competition Waverley, Redfern ana
Gordon are in the van with four points each ; Paddinffton
is on the second line with two, and Burwood and Sidney
walk together with one each. After that four clubs fly
the sign of the water-fowl, and three more are something
less tkan nothing. Saturday was graced withi only one
centurv-that of W. Farnsworth, who carried his bat for
103 for'Petersham against Waverley. But »t.was* aseconid
innino-s score and did not count in the final result, lhe
o he top scores were Fisher’s 92 not out for University
against Gordon ; and Small’s S 5 for Paddington against
Balmain. The successful bowlers were Asher, of Sydney,
who took eight Central Cumberland wickets for 3b ,
R B Minnett, of University, who smashed up seven
Gordon wickets for 44 ; Macartney, of Gordon, six Uni-
versity sticks for 85; and P. Docker, of Burwood, ha
of Middle Harbor’s for 34.
The feature of the games was the remarkable
upheaval of Gordon. It was at the dead-end of the list
last season, yet it walloped University, the premier
club. The victory was a first-ipnings one by the margin
of 63. On the home pitch, Redfern, where Jimmy
McGowen comes from, maintained the order of things
bv smashing up Glebe, where Jimmy Hogue was
defeated. Glebe is a powerful combination, and
had never previously been defeated by Redfern,
but it came to grief on Saturday by a margin
of 26 runs in the first innings. Having totted up
the, for it, amazing score of 306 in the first inn-
ings, Sydney District club gave Central Cumberland
two bites at "the cherry. The first yielded 134, and the
second only 93 ; so Sydney stood a proud victor with an
innings and 79 runs to spare. When will wonders cease .
The Waverley-Petersham argument was a close thing,
from which Waverley emerged with a credit balance of
21 on the first innings. Burwood gave Middle Harbor a
fearful doing. In response to Burwood’s 223 Middle
Harbor could only feebly ejaculate 99, and, following on,
made another 72 for the loss of one wicket. Then time
ran out, leaving Burwood victorious by nine wickets.
Balmain, having smacked up 331 for seven wickets,
closed, and started to get Paddington out. But only
nine wickets had fallen for 215 when time ran out and
Paddington had saved its face with a draw. North
Sydney had the bye.
* * * *
The first round of Melbourne Pennant cricket
finished on Saturday with a finely-compiled 133 by the
champion, W. W. Armstrong. This total helped Mel-
bourne to a first-innings victory by 213 against the flan-
nelled furies from South Melbourne. St. Hilda, which
massacred University for 53, easily declared a first-
innings surplus of 156 runs ; whilst East Melbourne sur-
passed die Essendon warriors with merely 23 trots to
spare. Richmond, defeating Collingwood by 97 runs,
humbled the pride of the Magpies in the mud ; and Carl-
ton, walloping Fitzroy by 95, left ’Roy in an extremely
despairful condition. Horrible excitement prevailed at
P’ran, where the local wonders just secured a draw by
making 175 for nine wickets against Northcote’s total of
195.
* * * *.
Victoria is on the look-out for promising young
players, and Jack Worrall has been installed as coach,,
with special instructions to get busy when he sees any
hopeful young vines. Cricket has been languid in.
Victoria of late years, and the presence of a keen-eyed
coach with latitude in the selection of colts may do.
something to lift it out from among the lotus leaves.
* ■* -* *
Though everyone else has put the windbag out
of sight for the year, the Victorian Junior Ass. played its
final at Croxton Park, Northcote, last Saturday—but
the end is not yet. The matter was supposed to have
been settled the previous week by. the victory of Port
Juniors over Yarraville, but it appears that the ball col-
lapsed a few minutes before the firysh, and when the
spare sphere belonging to Yarraville was requisitioned,
it was found to be punctured in its stomach, and before
a substitute could be commandeered the bell rang. There-
upon arguments raged and both sides claimed the match ;
so the Ass. ordered the collision to occur again, which
it did on Saturday, in the presence of 2000 spectators
and the Law. Notwithstanding this latter hint, Bill and
Lizzie were very fit, and the Yarraville 18 were as willing
as they make ’em; but Port Juniors declined to take
more than a casual interest in the proceedings, presum-
ably relying on the “ grand final,” to which their un-
beaten record entitles them, to gain the pennant and
whatever glory attaches thereto. Anyhow, the scores
Yarraville 50 points, Port Juniors four points—shout for
inquiry by the Ass. This body passed out four players
during the week, and it mightn’t be a bad idea to pass the
two teams out altogether and hang up the premiership
for a year. The footbrawler doesn’t seem to know when,
the public has bad enough of him. #
The Vic. senior football clubs have been handed
something to ponder over during the warm weather..
The trustees of the metropolitan parks and gardens,,
having casually noted the gaudy “gates,” want a trifle
themselves to improve the grounds and the parks sur-
rounding them. Some clubs, like Collingwood, North
and Carlton, pay a fair annual rent; others, like South,.
St. Hilda and ’Roy, pay £5O a year; others again merely
“ come to an understanding” with the local cricket club,
and the two bodies between them “ part ” a peppercorn
rent of £1 per annum. The trustees have an idea that at
least 10 per cent, of both cricket and football revenues
should be handed to them to be spent in improving the
people’s properties, both outside and inside the playing
areas ; and the public considers that the trustees haven t.
developed this mercenary spirit too vigorously or too.
soon.
* * * *
A southern city tells the story of a recent fist-
fight (under Marquis of Spoofb.erry rules) between two
reputable middle-aged citizens, in the presence of a select
company. The younger and smaller of the two gladia-
tors is not more than 40, but his advantage in youth and
agility was discounted by a tendency to palpitation of
the heart and pains across the kidneys. It was he who.
invited the trouble by twitting the other fellow with his
supposed lack of valor, whereupon the insulted party,,
being an offieah as well as a gentleman, challenged him
to a duel with nature’s weapons in a place set apart for
that purpose. The challenger carried weight for
age, i.e., he is a few years older and two stone
heavier. As neither party could fight for sour
apples, a fairly well-balanced battle was expected.
A well-known barrister and connoisseur of stoush under-
took to engage a private boxing saloon and manage the
entertainment; so at 8 o’clock p.m. the combatants
came along with their friends. They were both in the
pink of condition, especially the heavy-weight, whose
skin shone with the effects of whisky when he was
stripped. The ringmaster ruled that the bodies must be
bare if the “dooks” were unclothed; and although the
challenger had forgotten to bring a champion belt, he
was soon fixed up. He drew his pants together with
a safety-pin and prepared for battle. The fight
lasted five rounds, during which only' one frontal
blow was struck. This was a slow over-arm de-
liverv by the light-weight, which broke in from
w and "lanced off the military man’s nose, causing
it" to bleed. All the other attacks surprised the
combatants in the rear, whilst they were involved in
complications, aggravated by the discovery that Spoof-
berrv Rules forbade them to hit one another when down.
Each man in turn appealed to the referee to relax the
law on this point; but he gravely ordered them to break
awav and "et up. Finally the fat man was wrestled to a
finish. He lay helpless whilst the learned referee counted
19 Then his seconds sued for peace with honor, and the
gathering slowly dispersed after shedding three tears for
the victor. # *
In the second Victorian Marathon, the heavy
run from Frankston to the Wesley College cricket ground
at St Kilda attracted a field of 62 ; and Australia can slap
itself with the wallop of delight because despite the
heavy going, and with nothing more to look for than
certificates, 25 pluggers finished the course under the
allotted four hours.
* * * *
Australian boy-billiardist, George Gray re-
cently put up an amazing exhibition against Englishman
Hughes, whom he conceded 1000 points in 4000. After
making his record break of 985 he put up some other re-
markable runs, chief of which were 418, 471 and -53 (un-
finished), and ran out 2155 points to the good. In other
words, he made 4500 while the Englishman made 845.
And they call it sport 1
* * ■* *
Billiard champion Stevenson has sounded the
first English note of warning re young George Gray s
losing-hazard stroke, the monotony whereof is liable to
prove its undoing,” says the old stager. It didn t need
Stevenson to predict a Billiard Act for the prevention of
breaks of 1000 or 10,000 off the red. Monotonous potting
of the red off the spot was prohibited long ago, and
monotonous scoring off the red from “baulk will be
likewise dealt with in due course, either by limiting the
number of permissible successive hazards, or (more
likely) by altering the baulk conditions. The gate-money
interests of the game will demand it. Nobody who has
seen much of the play at which boy Gray is the world s
wondercan find any more entertainment in it. But, mean-
time, the novelty of Gray’s excellence is an attraction, and
the fact that the boy hasn’t yet played a championship
match and been beaten on his monotony, so to speak, must
deter the billiard bosses from any legislation against the
stroke. It is “ all in the game ” of which Stevenson is
the nominal champion, and until the lad has proved him-
self invincible by means of it, there can be no excuse for
blocking* his specialty. Stevenson, apparently, antici-
pates a challenge sooner or later, for he says he is not
likely to play against Gray until the latter is prepared to
enter for the championship.
* * * *
Tasmania’s champion amateur sculler, Cecil
McVilly, who also holds the blue ribband of the continent,
is the latest recruit on the Parramatta, where George
Towns is putting the hall-mark of proper finish on him.
Towns is said to be enthusiastic, and to hint darkly of
another champion looming horribly on the horizon.
“Glebe”: The rider from Yassberra who
figured in the Goulburn-to-Sydney cycle race (B. 29/9/’10),
was reckoned a “dead cert.” in that district. But he fell
into Paddy’s River on the way, and locally that is re-
garded as completely explaining his failure. People from
Yassberra are only used to water in moderation.
Letter for Frank Skurry Hewitt, ex-champion
runner of the world, lying at Bulletin office. Anyone
knowing his address, please communicate.

N.S.W. Cricket Association is out to do busi-
ness on business lines this season. The State selection
committee has been notified that at least 20 playersmust
be chosen to practise at least three weeks before the
inter-State matches commence. The final team will be
chosen from the regulars at the practice nets. Whicli
means that this season’s N.S.W. XI. will represent form,
and not the intuitive genius of the selectors. It is’a sane
and business-like proposition, which does the Association
proud. Here’s to it 1 ,
In spite of the ruin that fell on Australia in
general and N.S.W. in particular on April 13, the number
of cars on the N.S.W. Traffic Department’s motor register
continues to creep up, and has mounted from 1500 odd to
2000. And still they come. But perhaps these new cars
represent tha loot of the capitalist by the Labor brigands.

IMMEDIATE POSSESSION.
Railway facilities at hand. Reasonable freights to Sydney markets. Adequate
rainfall. Virgin soil, and, generally speaking, an all-round productive area.
Here a farmer may found a home, and at the same time be assured of
satisfactory grain returns as well as drafts of fat stock. The owners are genuine
sellers and give possession at such a period that the best of the year’s grass
is available; therefore spare a few days and thoroughly inspect for yourselves.
ROUTE FOR INSPECTION.—MeIbourne to Harden (on main Sydney line),
thence via Blayney and Orange to Parkes, or Melbourne to Sydney—
Sydney to Parkes.
RAINFALL.—AnnuaI average rainfall about 22 inches, and it falls at such
periods to exactly suit agricultural pursuits.
WATER SUPPLY.—Numerous tanks ranging from 6000 to 12,000 cubic
yards—dams and creek.

to the S.A. Government; and Technical College, Sydney, reports to Messrs. Fisher and
Co., as follows :
Oear Sirs,
I have made an analytical examination of a sample
of “FISHER’S PHOSPHERINE,” taken from stock,
and beg to report as follows :
It is composed of pure chemicals, carefully com-
bined, which are calculated to produce the effects
claimed by you.
“ FISHER’S PHOSPHERINE ” is a powerful
tonic, and will be specially valuable in curing cases
(chronic or otherwise) of neuralgia, rheumatism, and
kindred nervous disorders. It will have a beneficial
action on the kidneys, and will be a good anti-dyspeptic
—aid to digestion.
It contains no injurious compounds, nor anything
contraventing the “Pure Food Act,” or the regulations
under that Act.
It is generally recognised that “ FISHER'S
PHOSPHERINE ” is the best nerve and brain tonic
that can be employed to repair the ravages made upon
them in the strenuous battle of life. Five drops in
water after meals is the greatest aid to digestion known.

FIRST LETTER.
Kogarah, February 21, 1910.
Gentlemen, Taking into consideration the wonderful good your Phospherine has
done me, I feel it my duty to write you expressing my heartfelt thanks, and wishing it a
wider range of popularity. Suffering humanity are exposed to so many puffed quackeries
that a genuine article should have its merits acknowledged. lam an engineer, and was
for many years in the employment of Messrs. Farleigh, Nettheim and Co. ° While in their
service I had the typhoid fever, and had four months in the hospital. After leaving the
institution I was in a very low state, suffering from backache, kidney complaint, bad sight
and other troubles. I tried a number of so-called remedies, and received no benefit. I then
took some bottles of your Phospherine, and was surprised at the wonderful beneficial re-
sults ; my sight improved, my kidneys got right, and my general good health and energy
returned. I now feel as strong and fit as I did 20 years ago. I will not trouble you with
any further details of my case. Again thanking you. I have waited some time before
writing, to be quite convinced that my CURE WAS PERMANENT.
I am, sirs, yours truly,
PRIVATE AND CONFIDENTIAL. ALBERT A. HAWKINS.
SECOND LETTER.
Kogarah, May 13, 1910.
Dear Sirs, —In reply to your letter of March 1, requesting my consent to your pub-
lishing a testimonial sent to you by me, setting forth the wonderful merits of your ELIXIR
OF LIFE, known as “Fisher’s Phospherine,” I now, after some further evidence of its
virtue, willingly concede to your request.
ALBERT A. HAWKINS,
Engineer and Machinist.

Consult any competent judge of billiard tables, and he’ll

these tables—the recent match between George Gray and Fred
Lindrum, jun., when Gray scored 836,, 831 .coming off the red,
thus establishing two records, and for which Gray
received a gold medal, is an example. This score is another
proof of the marvellous accuracy and perfect construction of
our billiard tables.
Each table is fitted with Imperial Low Cushions, and
bears our trade mark.

STATUTORY DECLARATION.
FRED FULLWOOD, of 35 Paling’s Buildings, Ash Street,
Sydney, in the State of New South Wales, Accountant, do
hereby solemnly .and sincerely declare
1. I am an Incorporated Accountant having been
in practice in Sydney for the past twenty years,
2. --That I am a Fellow of the Society of Incorporated
Accountants amd Auditors, London, and also a Fellow of
the Australasian Corporation of Public Accountants,
5. --That I have audited the Accounts of 6 * T H E
LONE HAND* * MAGAZINE for the
period of five months from May to September, 1910,
inclusive, since the price of the magazine was
reduced to sixpence, and I hereby solemnly declare
that the total issue was 177,450 or an average
circulation per month of Thirty-five thousand
four hundred and ninety (55,490) Copies, and that
the circulation has materially increased since
the issue of the first number at the reduced price.
And I make this solemn declaration conscientiously believing the
same to be true and by virtue of the provisions of the Oaths Act 1900.
Subscribed and declared at Sydney
this thirtieth day of September,
one thousand nine hundred and ten,
before me,

When the wowser says No License, what

“ San Remo ” puts it thus :
After months of ructions and insinuations, Minister
Baines has granted another Wages Board for the Bris-
bane Gas Workers’industry. Which seems quite reason-
able until you go back over the trouble. When the
stokers’ unions of Brisbane and South Brisbane obtained
a Board to consider their claims, it was thought that the
Board would be given power to deal with every gas
employee. But that wasn’t the Phidston view.
The Board met, heard the evidence and settled
an award affecting only 38 men. Whereupon the
laborer and the lamplighter, at 15s. per week, hustled
round to Minister Barnes’s office with a petition asking for
an extension of the Board’s powers, so that it might hear
their case too. The North Brisbane Gas Go. (of which a
leading M.L.A. is an incandescent light) thought the
matter out, and the next thing Barnes knew was a
second petition from the same employees declaring, this
time, that they didn’t want any Wages Board. Many
people at once began to smell a dead whale some-
where. But Barnes didn’t; he merely professed to
be puzzled. The next move came from a deputa-
tion headed by Labor-leader Bowman, when some
things were explained—notably, that the second
petition, although it did not originate with the men,
had been signed by them on the gas co.’s premises.
That only Worried Barnes more ; and finally he made up
his mind, or had it made up for him, to grant a separate
Board for the laborers and lamplighters. Observe that
he did not extend the stokers’ Board to fit the whole
industry. Don’t ask me why, because I can’t tell you.
Hid I been in his place, I might have figured it out
this way: If these laborers and lamplighters have been
bluffed or cajoled into signing that second petition, it is
not likely they will produce strong representatives to sit
upon their Wages Board, whereas the stokers’ re-
presentatives are strong and emphatic, and would
certainly hold out against, and perhaps be seriously' in-
convenient to, this poor co. Therefore, let us have a
new Wages Board. I say, that is how I might have
figured it had I been in Barnes’s position; but I’m not,
and probably Barnes never thought of looking at it like
that. But it all goes to show what brilliant administra-
tors the Phidston gang are. If the Wages Board Act
were administered with common-sense, it would do much
good ; but the way the Phidstons are administering it
calls for the scorn of the reflective, and the dead cat of
the impetuous.
“ Caveat,” from the Coort: —
A case, recorded as Ware v. Cavanagh and another,
which occupied the attention of Justice Street forsome
days in N.S.W. Probate Court recently, seems to demand
some investigation now that the police have finished
making up the rolls and inquiring into the jury panel for
the Wade Government. The story as told in the evidence
was that one Campbell, a farmer at Morpeth, having no
family, had adopted his nephew and niece named Ware.
Subsequently, he drew up a will bequeathing them all his
property. He made no secret of it, and on March 13
last, before leaving for Sydney to seek medical treat-
ment, he sent for his lawyer to make sure that
his wishes had been made clear. His lawyer laughed
at his fears, but Campbell hinted that someone would
trv and get the money that he intended for
Lily Ware (or Campbell), and he wanted to make
dead sure that Lily was safe. On reaching Syd-
ney he was operated on for cancer of the
liver. On April 13 he died. On May 12 a will,
with one Cavanagh as executor, was granted pro-
bate. This document disinherited the Wares before they
knew of its existence ; but immediately afterwards they
sued for the probate to be cancelled and their will
admitted. The plaintiffs openly averred knavery, and
bluntly hinted at forgery. The witness of the will was a
man who seems to be entirely and definitely lost some-
where ; while Cavanagh bushed himself to such an
extent that no one in court could obtain a co-
herent account of how the dying man had
executed the will or why he had changed his
mind. The Judge unhesitatingly cancelled the will, de-
scribed Cavanagh as “ untrustworthy and unscrupulous,’’
and flatly declined to believe a word he said. Lily Ware
got her property, less her taxed costs (which come out of
the estate); Cavanagh is to pay his own. The Judge de-
clined to decide the issue of forgery ; but remarked that
Cavanagh had nothing to complain about in being mulct
in his own costs. After calling a man as many things as
Justice Street called Cavanagh, it seemed up to his Honor
to make him pay the costs of the aggrieved party as well
as his own. But apparently the habit of saying “ costs
out of the estate ” is too strong to be lightly broken.

IN BANANA LAND
Queensland Labor-leader Bowman’s health
has been bad for some time, and although it is
now better than it was Dr. Hirschfeld has
strongly advised a long sea trip, preferably the
run round the Cape to Europe and back. Six or
eight months’ rest, with such a trip thrown in,
should, says the medicine man, restore Bowman
to full strength and working capacity. So a sub-
scription list has been opened, with the view' of
presenting him with a bag of sovereigns. And,
as Dave Bowman has been a very unselfish man,
it is to be hoped that the bag will be heavy.
“ Ravenshoe ” ; “C.S.A.” (B. 15/9/’10)
handles the truth carelessly when he says that
“John Moffat, mining magnate, purchased a
township, with saw-milling plant complete, for
£177.” The upset price of the block was cer-
tainly £1 an acre for the 177 acres; but the im-
provements, consisting of saw-mill, stables, barn,
four cottages and three humpies, were valued at
£1067, and the buyer had to pay up that lot
before he took over the township. As a matter,
of fact, they were John’s improvements already,
which explains why, when he bought the land, he
didn’t have to pay himself for the goods that
were on it.
“Bromide”: Since my arrival in the
State, eight weeks ago, I have been in nearly
every coastal town from Brisbane to Cairns, and
have stayed overnight in every place I have
visited. Being a thorough believer in a White
Australia, I barred colored-labor hotels. To my
first question : “Do you employ white cooks and
servants?” in many cases the answer is, “ Oh,
no ! I employ a Jap cook. But, then, you
see” And a long rigmarole follows in apology.
The lies are bad enough, but the thing that gets
on my nerves is the way the blighters insult the
white race. “ You can’t depend on ’em.” “ They
get drunk and leave you in the lurch.” “ They’re
not as clean as the Jap or the Chow.” “They’re
too cheeky”—and many other like reasons,
always concluding with “We tried hard, but we
can’t get’em.” The pub I’m writing from is a
“ white ” house, and a kitehenmaid was wanted.
One advt. in the local paper brought six white
girls before 7 a.m., and by 9 a.m. another half
dozen had applied. I’ll bet a quid if a visit was
paid to some of the colored-labor hotels here to-
day you’d be told: “Oh, yes, I’d like to have
whites, but I can’t cret 'em.” The liars !
President Manchester, of the Brisbane
Water and Sewerage Board, cheers the city up
with the news that, when the full drainage and
sewerage scheme of the Board has been carried
out, the northern capital will be the admiration
of the sanitary experts of the globe, and its death
rate will be something to marvel at. Even now,
with a water supply that would disgrace a third-
rate country town, and a sanitary system that is
an abomination, its death rate is lower than that
of any other capital city in the Commonwealth,
bar one—and it doesn’t always have to bar even
one. Last year Sydney only beat it by about half
a death per’ 1000 of the population.
Brisbane Courier recently published a
portrait of Atticus Tooth, who assisted at the
birth of two Bananaland towns—Bowen and
Burketown—and who, for nearly half a century,
has been defying the climate of North Queens-
land to do its worst. Judging by his appearance,
its worst doesn’t amount to much. As far back
as 1846 Atticus took cattle from the Murray
River to Widgee Widgee, in the Wide Bay (Q.)
district. For some years he squatted on the Dar-
ling Downs, but gradually got farther and farther
north until alligators, the Gulf and other obstacles
hinted that it was about time he tethered himself
for good.
From the report of the boiler inspector
for the Roma (Q.) district:— _
At Murweh station, where there is an 8-h.p. oil engine
driving pump, Miss Cameron, the manager’s daughter
(aged 22 years) manages the whole plant and does it well.
It is very encouraging for the young lady; but
the attendance on engines and pumps will never
become a habit among ladies if the inspector has
to coldly set out the age of the attendant.

At the last Maryborough (Q.) show the
Queensland Forestry Department a
maul head turned out of a mangrove log. The
Department said that mangrove was better than
any other timber for the mauls used by boiler-
makers in beating red-hot plates into shape on
cast-iron templates. B. T. McKay, works man-
ager at the good Australian shops of Walkers
Limited, Maryborough, promptly put the matter
to the proof, and reports that the wood is quite
up to the Department s specifications. Hitherto,
Walkers have used rnauls made of mahogany,
clamped with iron ; but the mangrove maul can
do its job without the iron.
“B. Sticker” : It might pay Queensland
to do a bit of medical prospecting on the big
banana plantations along the Johnstone River. I
have lived and moved there, and can’t get rid of
the horrible suspicion that the expedition would
pan out a few cases of leprosy. John Chow is
aware that too frequent revelations of the disease
are not qalculated to make him any better loved
as a colonist and vegetable merchant; hence, as
the North well knows, he hides, if possible, those
of his countrymen who are falling to ruins. The
extensive Johnstone River banana-gardens are
ideal places of concealment. White men seldom,
if ever, meander that way, and those who do
don’t poke into the humpies. From personal
contact there would, then, be little to fear from
these people; but these unwashed Mongolians
handle the banana at harvest time ! The Saw-
bones brotherhood may differ as to whether the
curse is contagious or not; but the signifi-
cant fact remains that the father of the recently-
discovered Brisbane schoolboy-leper is an in-
mate of Peel Island lazarette, while the sad
case of the two Charters Towers white sister-
lepers makes it impossible to doubt that mere
than one person can be contaminated by the same
agent. It might prove useful to post up in sugar
and banana districts a plain, printed description
of the syihptoms of the scourge, with an invitation
to the public to at once report suspicious-looking
yellow subjects to the Health authorities.
“ San Remo ” : One of the worst misfor-
tunes of a Tory newspaper is a bad memory.
Quite recently, the Brisbane Courier assured the
ironmoulders who are on strike that their ill-
advised action was merely recoiling on their own
heads; that their places had been filled ; and that
the master founders weren’t a bit inconvenienced.
Last week the forgetful old dame chattered thus:
The moulders who have been on strike in Queensland
are not doing their industry any good, as employers are
rightly chary about tendering for work which they may
not be able to carry out, and it looks as if a good deal of
trade will be lost.
Now, is the Courier right, or wrong, or both, or
neither or what ?
That Aero Club of Ours.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Most interesting addresses were given by members of
the Aero Club on the science of flying, at our recent Ex-
hibition. The aeroplane in evidence was much admired,
but the public seemed very disappointed. They evidently
expected to see the thing fly.— Brisbane daily.
OUR Aero Club-men lectured on the science of the
Thing—
How all it wanted was a push, and then you’d see it
spring ;
How it was built on latest lines to lift a ready plane,
And then to cleave the snowy clouds or skim above the
main ;
How ten or twenty thousand feet ’twould mount without
a quiver,
And cross the Alps, or fly the sea, or glide above the
river;
And so they talked in learnfed style and drew the people
round
To gaze upon that aeroplane that rested on the ground ?
We gloated on these valiant men and inly wondered why
They kept theirfeet upon the earth and never did a fly ;
But Jones, who was the scientist that built the aero-
plane,
Explained the basic principles of motor, wing and vane ;
He told us how ’twas sudden death (we heard him with a
shudder)
To tangle up the flier’s tail inside her little rudder,
And how he always took the air with such a joyous
bound —
And all the time the aeroplane kept resting on the
ground.
And Brown, he told with bated breath and wildly
flourished limb,
Of how a deadly accident had nearly happened him ;
How once, in his dirigible, the motor wouldn’t gee,
And so he fell for half a mile and landed in a tree ;
And how he made a rope at last by ripping up his
clothes,
And dropped another 20 feet and landed on his nose ;
And how he soon got home again all whole and safe and
sound —
And as he spake the aeroplane kept resting on the
ground !
And Johnson, he dilated on the terrors of the ’plane—
How, in the wars of coming days, the flying man would
reign ;
How he would scoot above our heads with curious motor
hum,
And drop a bomb, and put us all to sleep in Kingdom
Come :
How ne’er a Dreadnought could escape this deadly air
machine,
Unless it learned to duck and dive just like a submarine ;
And, as he talked, his eye would glare on all the folk
around—
And all the time that aeroplane kept resting on the
ground.
And Brown and Jones and Johnson, then they spat upon
their hands
And said how it was their intent to visit other lands ;
How they would show the Eskimo some fun on his
horizon,
And give the muddy Mississipp. a sight to feed her
eyes on;
How they would see the Brig of Ayr, and skim the Banks
o’ Doon,
And camp a week contented on the Mountains of the
Moon :
And as they gassed we followed them, in admiration
drowned—
But all that time the aeroplane was safe upon the ground.
They are a noble lot of coves, this aero club, you know,
And Lord ! How they will hustle things if once they let
her go.
But yet, although they’ve got a craft that’s trim and taut
and steady,
They will not soar our city o’er until they’re sure they’re
ready
Their Duty (so they tell us when impatience makes us
burst)
Is plainly this, whate’er they miss, to Educate Us First;
Some day, no doubt, we’ll see them all with flying glory
crowned;
But, up to date, that aeroplane is resting on the ground !
QUEENSLAND.
F. BORN.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane. October 15, 1910.
Mr Dear Thargomindah,—
For some reason unexplained, Wee Macgregor
did not hie to the North yesterday, as expected,
but the Morgan couple were hurried off instead.
Ah, well, what they loose in oratory they will
gain in avoirdupois.
There have been such goings-on in the House-
nasty tempers and papa Kidston spanking his
family into shape, and all over that £70,000
which has been put, rightly or wrongly, into a
trust fund for the new University. Such bitter
outbreaks and dirty-linen washings, unparlia-
mentary language and threats, and, finally,
nothing but smoke. To my simple mind, Thargo-
mindah, my dear, it doesn’t seem to matter
whether the little nest-egg is packed away in a
trust fund or in a savings bank, or in a hatbox,
as long as the infant Uni. gets it in the long run ;
but the differences of opinion produced what the
Premier said was “a deliberate attempt to oust
the Government,” and that, you know, is some-
thing pa Kidston will not put up with.
A fleeting message from Tittell Brune tells me
that she is putting in very hard work in the great
city. As the critics, it seems, are inclined to
closely watch her for slight slips, they evidently
think her worth attention, which is something.
Her latest picture shows a very rounded and well-
groomed and generally improved Tittell.
Next week there will be a flood of bishops for
the opening of St. John’s Cathedral. The altar
—bluish Devon stone, with wonderful pillars and
front panel, the gift of the Hopetoun family—has
arrived, and is, with the big rose window of
stained glass, the centre of attraction.
Police-Inspector Galbraith, who retires on pen-
sion, was originally connected with the Maoriland
“force.” He joined here in January, ’B2, and
has served with distinction for 28 years. Pro-
bably no other officer has had a wider experience ;
he has seen service in all parts of the State, in-
cluding the Gulf country, and latterly has been
in charge at Townsville.
In the new constitution of the local Creche and
Kindergarten Association, provision is made for a
modest council of 40, noless. Duringthe recent row
with the Institute of Social Service (which latter
was finally routed), a modest six made their
meows heard from Hamilton to Woolloongabba
Forty—think of it, ye tabby cats ! If anything of
a controversial nature happens in the future,
the squall will disturb the fish in the Gulf of
Carpentaria.
The engagement of Douglas Malcolm Corrie
and Miss Vera Baynes, eldest daughter of that
fine sportsman, Ernest Baynes, is now formally
announced ; and much congratulatory literature
is coming in to both of them.
After many months’ illness George Valentine
Helliear passed over the river on Thursday.
“Old George,” as he was affectionately called,
was almost a native. Born at Bristol in 1848, he
arrived in the Commonwealth in ’54. Having
got his education at the Church of England
Grammar School in Melbourne, he came to this
State in ’64, cultivated the ox awhile, and then
was articled to Mr. J. M. Thompson, at Ipswich,
being admitted by the Full Court as a solicitor in
’7l. Eight years later he was acting Master of
Titles, and became Crown Solicitor in 1903.
George Helliear, a confirmed Johnsonian Club
man, was a popular and companionable chap, of
whom few had anything but good to say. Judges
thought highly of his talents, and never had man
fewer enemies in the profession.
The Ipswich Champion Choir, still blushing
with Sydney Eisteddfod honors, raised a melodious
voice at the Brisbane Exhibition Hall last week ;
and, in spite of torrential rains, the big room
was full. Quite apart from the chorus work, the
fiddling of one Jacob Elkind and the mezzo
voice of Miss Hilda Reid were worth a ducking
to hear; while the smileful Francis and Miss
Josey Bytheway were heard in a Giovanni
number, and afterwards in the Butt specialty,
“ The Keys of Heaven.” *
A she-pal happened back from the Old World
last week, and her observations on continental
life are entertaining. She has come home with
the conviction that Australians are the cleanest
people on earth, and she chuckles at the kindly,
tolerant way in which the Viennese and others
received the Antipodean demand for a daily bath
as though it were an amiable fad. On the subject
of London women she waxes eloquent. There is,
she says, no one so provincial as the London —or,
for the matter of that, the English—house-dame
at large. She is so tied down by tradition that, if
one of the strings slips, she feels lost, and
has an immediate idea that the country is
going to the dogs or the Labor party. The
youthful Australian matron who dares to be
at home to her callers is regarded as
something strange and unheard of, and if she
follows up this enormity by an offer of 4 o’clock
tea she is labelled dangerous. In her travels my
pal discovered Mr. and Mrs. Carter Pickford in a
snug little home in Surrey, whence Mrs. C. P.
runs up to town nightly. Mr. C. P. has forsaken
the buskin for the motor business.
Brisbaneites combine in sympathy with Mrs.
Hall, a local croquet expert, who, while on the
warpath in Sydney armed with a mallet, managed
to smash her ankle, and so spoiled her chances of
trophies and fun.
The latest brand of surprise-visit lunacy in
southern Queensland is the “fowl” visitation.
A worthy citizen is observed putting the finishing
touches to a hen-house and his sympathetic
neighbors call round next convenient evening
each with a nervously clucking hen concealed
under the pinafore. The “surprised” citizen
welcomes the guests, collects the “ chooks,” serves
out refreshments, and retires to rest in the glad
knowledge that two dozen antique barn-door
warriors are roosting on his hospitable perches.
No sensible henwife ever keeps old fowls to run
up the corn-bill.
Yours affectionately,
MARDO.
The sort of tangle that the Queensland
land law has got into can be guessed from the fact
that the recently-introduced Land Bill repeals
provisions of no less than 32 existing Acts, some
of them dating back to 1837.
“ Ossian ” : That proposal to manufacture
alcohol from Bananaland’s overgrowth of prickly
pear has not the merit of novelty, if Gertrude
Atherton does not let loose her wild imagination in
“The Conqueror,” published in 1902, and profess-
ing to be the true and romantic story of Alexander
Hamilton, the mentor of Washington. The
authoress depicts Hugh Knox—the Presbyterian
parson, who did so much to fashion Hamilton’s
youthful mind—hurling advice at his 15-year-old
protege what time the pair sit in Knox’s library
“over two long glasses of ‘Miss Blyden,’ a
fashionable drink made of sugar, rum and the
juice of the prickly pear, which had been buried
in the divine’s garden (in St. Croix) for the
requisite number of months.” This is stated to
have happened in 1772. Presumably the object
of the burial was to permit the plant to ferment
or, at least, to acquire an alcoholic “bite.” Has
any Bulletin reader sipped alcohol decorated
with juice squeezed from the Australian variety
of the, pest? And, if so, what color were the
snakes ?
“Itan” : Nature was a generous lady the
day she fashioned the rich hinterlands of Cairns
(N.Q.). The Herberton plateau, besides possess-
ing a climate which will challenge any part of the
world as a health-producer, is a gallery of the
most beautiful natural scenes. The gulch known
as “ The Crater,” although no relation to any
ancient volcano, is a basin 500 ft. long, 300 ft.
broad and 300 ft. deep. The granite walls are
mostly sheer precipices, while upon the
floor is a cool lake 200 ft. deep. From
the summit, on a clear day, 40 miles of
mountain scenery may be swept with the
eye. The highest land-point lifts through clouds
at 4000 ft., and falls and cataracts jewel the ra-
vines and precipices. The great belt of surround-
ing country will carry an immense population, but
to-day it mostly lies lonely, with here and theie
a settler clearing scrub and jungle, or a mini r
scratching the surface for a handful of tin. Whnt
a country to be lying undefended under the bared
teeth of an Asiatic horde!

THE BELLUBERA is easily one of the finest ferry steamers south of the line, and the Port
Jackson Company has reaspn to feel satisfied with the manner in which the
travelling public and residents of Manly appreciate their enterprise. The Bellubera is
altogether an example of local production, having been designed and built by the Mort’s Dock and
Engineering Company. A beautifully-modelled, double-ended steel craft, 210 feet long (15 feet
longer than the last new steamer Burra Bra), she provides plenty of deck space, for she has a beam of
32 feet, and a depth moulded of 15 feet. There is thus ample accommodation, and although the
certificate authorises her to carry 1500, she has a floating capacity of at least 5 00 more. When the
Bellubera ran her official trial she averaged a speed of 15 knots over the measured mile, which gives
her the proud distinction of being the largest and fastest ferry steamer on the harbor. The vessel
attracted much attention during her trials.
The machinery of the Bellubera consists of a set of triple-expansion engines, having cylinders of
2Bj£in. and 48j£in. respectively, and a stroke of 27m., fitted with steam reversing gear.
Steam is provided by two navy-type boilers, with six furnaces, and give a working pressure of iBolb.
The auxiliaries include an independent circulating pump, automatic feed pumps, feed heater, filter,
etc. The indicated horse-power is 1350,’and the guaranteed speed is 14 knots in service. The
electric light installation is of the highest class, and from the firm of Messrs. Ernest Scott and
Mountain. A wheelhouse is provided each end, on specially-built bridges, each having an inde-
pendent set of Chadburn’s telegraph gear, and an independent set of Alley McLellan’s patent steam
steering gear ; also a complete installation of telephones to engine room—thus assuring the greatest
possible security against mishap.
The Bellubera makes the fleet of the Port Jackson Company up to seven passenger steamers,
which are capable of carrying 8500 passengers' to Manly on one trip, and the Company should have
no difficulty in coping with the largest crowd it may have the pleasure to handle during the present
season. The Port Jackson Company prides itself on the fact—and rightly so, too—that the fine
steamers comprising their fleet are constructed in Sydney. The vessels are striking evidence of
skilful shipbuilding ; and the Port Jackson Company, in thus encouraging local industry, keeps the
money in the country, and helps to show to the public at large what local artisans can do.

The Inky Way.
Died on September 24, at Kenmore, near Goulburn
(N.S. W.), George Frederick Mason, youngest son of the
late Walter George Mason, engraver on wood, of the
original staff of the Illustrated London News, and after-
wards of London Punch, aged 59 years.
* * * *
“ Pressman” : The North Coast Daily News (N.S.W.),
on Monday, October 3, reproached the Lismore Rowing
Club for having got off some of its regatta heats on the
previous day. Said the Pious Editor :
“If certain sections of the Rowing Club are so en-
grossed in sport that they will not obey the behest:
‘ Remember thou keep holy the Sabbath Day,’ they
might at least pay a little deference to the religious
scruples of others and get away out of sight to transgress
one of the most uplifting tenets of Christianity—the ob-
servance of which is generally a proud trait in the char-
acter of even the most rugged Britisher.”
As a footnote to the article the following appeared
“ In order that the religious scruples of our subscribers
may not be further offended, the proprietors of the North
Coast Daily News have decided that there will be no
future Monday morning issue of the paper, ifhis will
prevent our staff continuing in their present desecration
of the Sabbath evening, and will enable our reporters to
attend that Divine Worship which is the inherent right
of the poorest Britisher, instead of working at this office
in order to swell our dividends.”
P.S.—On reading the News over again, I find the footnote
did not appear. Which is very inconsistent.
* * * *
“M. L. Pressman ” : It has been claimed that Tas-
mania is the last country in the world for the free-lance
writer to make a living in, but if its Inky Way is macada-
mised with harderflints than that of Maoriland it ought to
be put in a non-competitive class by itself. And Cohen’s
land has far less excuse than Tassy, for its leading papers
are bigger, and the bulk of them are wealthier. Nearly
all of them, particularly in the large towns, are paying
steady dividends; yet there is hardly a proprietor
who lias charit ible thoughts towards the starved litera-
ture of the Dominion. Adverse criticism to the contrary,
some good literary work has been done in M.L.; but the
men with the money-bags have hardly given a passing
thought to it. Half the proprietors do not know litera-
ture when they see it; a quarter are content to take it
from those standard writers whose copyrights have ex-
pired ; and a quarter would offer to publish it at
advertising rates. Most of them stand aghast at any
proposal to pay anything outside the wages (and not too
much of that, either) of the regular staffs. The scissors
are their standby and the paste-pot their foundation. As
a luxury, the syndicated English or American or (very
occasionally) Australian serial, at 10s., 155., and
£1 is ordered. C. W. Martin’s Australian story,
“ Übique, the Scientific Bushranger,” and J. H.
M. Abbott’s “The Sign of the Serpent” were re-
cently put on the syndicate market at £1 Is. each for
the complete copy. Another luxury is the syndicated
news-letter or series of special articles. A circular was
recently issued by a London publishing co. offering
Maoriland newspapers a senes of signed articles at 4s.
per article of about 1400 or 1500 words. The co. offered
six special and original articles for one pound sterling.
Against this sort of competition the local writer has little
chance, and against the paste-pot and the shears he has
none at all. It may be said that he lias the market of the
wide world, and that if the Maoriland writer can produce
the goods the money is safe. Which is true. But, as a
rule, the circumstances and present necessities of the
majority lead them to stay at home, reporting the re-
marks of the borough council, clerking in offices, whaling
on the gumtields, and praying that one day a patriotic
proprietor will arise and plaster on his front door :
“ Wanted, original manuscript. All copy accepted will
be paid for at fair rates.”
“Bookware”: The Cambridge University Press is to
issue the eleventh edition of the Encyclopedia Britan-
nica early in 1911. It is 140 years since the first edition.
Tne eleventh will come out all in one heap, after eight
years of preparation. The ninth edition was 14 years
toiling out, volume by volume, from 1875 to 1889. Then
the Times brought out a supplement up to li)02. The
whole was called the tenth edition. The Times then set
about preparing the eleventh ; but handed the job over
to the Cambridge University Press. The volumes of the
ninth edition took up 6ft. of book-shelf ; the eleventh
edition may be crowded into 2ft. India paper has done
the trick. “Upwards of 52,000 sets of the ninth were sold
in Great Britain, and probably over 400,000, if piracies in
America are included.
* * * A
“The Snake”: I never read the report of a big
“ mission ” or religious “revival” without a glad recol-
lection of a night when a hard-chivved reporter rose in a
big hall in Sydney and rammed anathema down the
throat of a boss missioner. The Shifter (which name
fits him as well as another) had to report a
“gathering.” But the Shifter went to get vaccinated
or something first, and only reached the hall as the
meeting was breaking up. Adopting the usual course,
he asked the head man of the show, who leaned
down from the stage to hear the request, for a para-
graph. “And what paper might you represent?” said
the bos3 with a look that a horse wears when it is going
to kick like the end-girl in the Tivoli first part. “ The
Morning Telegraph,” was the reply. Straightening him-
self suddenly, and raising his hands to stay the remainder
of the audience, the gaunt missioner cried: “Here
is a young man representing the Morning Tele-
graph, who comes here at this time of night,
after the great gathering we have had, after all that has
been vouchsafed us, and asks for a paragraph. A para-
graph ! Incidentally, lie » asking me to do his work.
But, friends, I can tell%inr that I will not. That, sir,”
he concluded, turning down towards the Shifter, “ is all
I have to say to you.” Then the Shifter pulled himself
up from the press-table with battle-blood in his eye, and
remarked in a loud, decisive voice: “And all I have to
say to you, sir, is that vou can go to II ! ”
* * * *
“ Not At All ” : Have interested myself for some time
past in carefully examining the pen-names which have
appeared in The Bulletin, and have come to the decision
that a great number of them are badly chosen. How
about some explanations from well-known owners as to
the why and the whaffor of their names? For the past
t hree months I have been engaged in keeping a list of
these pen-names in order to donate a prize for the most
humorous, and I unhesitatingly hand a hogshead of the
best (Ist prize) to “Drunk As a Cow” (B. 25/8/T0).
There is nothing more uproarious. I know, because I’ve
I been it.
THE W.P.B.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Perdu
Under the desk, by the Editor’s toe,
There yawoeth a foe ;
Waiting- for 3'ou
And for me,
When the fruits of our pen
Enter that horrible haven—the Editor’s Den.
It yawns,
But slumbers never, and never it sleeps,
But vigilant keeps ;
And fawns
At the knee
Of him serene in that den,
Who feeds it all day with the brains of second-rate
men.
He too,
The editor yawns at his horrible toil
Of flinging the spoil
To that mouth —
The sapless fruit of the pen,
Bastard and impotent sowing of impotent men.
I too
(Yawning is catching) yawn as I scribble,
Knowing no quibble,
No wit and no pathos
No eloquent bathos,
Will rescue these lines from that foe
Perdu by the editor’s toe ;
And so
I yawn for the deep of that den,
Which to reach is the goal of the most of us men
Who tilt at implacable Fate with the tip of a pen.
VICTORIA.

MEDLOW BATH, Blue Mountains, N.S.ff.
MAJESTIC HOTEL.
COMPLETE arrangements have been made by the new proprietor (Mr. W. H. Burgess), late of the Grand
Hotel, Melbourne, for the comfort of visitors during the forthcoming season.
Medlow Bath is an ideal resort. The crisp mountain air is a delight, and the mountains, in their
Spring garb, appeal to lovers of the picturesque. Arrangements can be made at the Hotel for motors,
carriages and riding tours.
Motor parties can be arranged any time to start from the City Office —or your own residence—direct
to the Majestic Hotel.
To pedestrians Medlow offers ever-varying attractions. The views in the mountains are superb, and
hours can be spent in walks through the Hotel grounds.
Hot-air pipes are laid in many rooms. Daily Motor trips are made from the Hotel to Jenolan Caves.
Further particulars can be obtained from the Hotel, or from the Sydney Offices, 42 Castlereagh Street.
W. H. BURGESS, Proprietor.

DERS STREET and PRINCES BRIDGE RAILWAY
STATIONS, and has never changed hands since 1848.

The Richmond railway slaughter has
apparently made Victoria’s weary Premier sit up,
or it has made the remaining Railway Com-
missioners sit up (Tait, Chief Commissioner
and principal cigar-burner, has handed in
his resignation). The announcement goes
forth that the lock and block system of
signalling is to be extended throughout the
crowded suburban railway area, and when this is
done there is reason to hope that another accident-
like that at Richmond will be practically im-
possible. Meanwhile, legal proceedings have
been started against one or two men who were
mixed up in the recent smash. So this is the
situation : (1) The Chief Commissioner has
dropped out, and Premier Murray has,
without investigation, given him a glorious
testimonial for first-class management and pub-
licly acquitted him in advance of all possible
blame. (2) The remaining Commissioners inti-
mate that, at long last, a safe signalling system is
Some to be extended throughout the dangerous
and congested suburban lines. And instead of
them (and Tait) being blamed for not doing it
before the smash, they seem to get special
credit for doing it after the smash. (3) The
men who, apparently, had to struggle along with
m ' efficient appliances bump up against the thick
end of the trouble, as usual.

Young Australia by the Sea.
(Lady Robinson's Beach , Botany Bay.)
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
BEHIND me spreads a continent, before me
shines the sea,
With the sun that warms a nation looking
kindly down on me,
For the morning gleams with promise, and a
gladness fills the land
As I mark where young Australia goes a-romp-
ing on the sand.
For the little waves are running in with laughter,
And a long, long whisper of delight —
Oh, run, little feet, they’re coming after
With their quick foam-fingers creeping white.
Afar, by yonder greener cape, the dingy sails are
furled,
Where the ships of high adventure came a-sailing
round the world,
And the white man gazed in wonder at his last-
found ocean prize,
Looking landward through the sunshine into
bright Australia’s eyes.
For the little waves were running in with
laughter,
And a long, long whisper on the sand,
Where the white man’s feet would follow after,
And the white man’s child love the land.
The sand-built castle tumbles down before the
creeping tide,
With the white shells flung in triumph along the
ruins wide,
And though every little footprint will be cleanly
washed away,
With a happy heart the ocean joins Australia at
her play;
For the little waves are running in with laughter,
And a long, long whisper on the sand—
Oh, run, little feet, they’re coming after,
For the blue, blue sea loves the land.
The sea is hoar with wisdom. Oh, it marks the
coming days
When the hands of young Australia shall be
master of its ways,
When the castle shall not tumble, when the white
feet, firm to stand,
Shall not yield to any foeman one bright yard of
this wide land;
When the little waves are running red with slaugh-
ter,
And a long, long sigh upon the shore —
Oh, you love our bright Australia, shining water,
But the white man’s child loves her more.
N.S.W.
DAVID MCKEE WRIGHT.
The Scoot.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I STARTED business in the cold, wet,
windy city of Ahuriri, as a financial and
commission agent, on a capital of £l3 10s.,
and when the capital was eaten up in expenses I
managed to extract another £9 17s. 6d. out of the
Railway Commissioners for a bag of mine that I
said they had lost. Before that was spent
Williams happened along.
Williams was a solicitor, and, as the Scriptures
have it, it is easier to go through the eye of a
camel than for a solicitor to remain an honest
man. Williams’s intentions were sublime, but
his performances were not to be mentioned in
polite society. He brought no money into the
concern, but he had a “connection,” and on that
we boomed for a time. We became a recognised
firm for winding up bankrupt and intestate estates
—financial wreckers, in fact. Then we secured a
bicycle agency, and gradually worked our way
into the swim.
All of which, Williams frequently reminded
me, was due to his having brought along a con-
nection. Occasionally I retorted that, till he
brought it along to me, it had been of small value
to himself.
Then Buck came on the scene. Buck was a man
who was in a chronic state of being stone, mother-
less broke. He had thrown away more chances
than most men ever dream of, and just as a Loan
and Mortgage Co. was about to sell him up, he
rushed into our office and frantically begged for
an immediate loan of £550. I got him to write out
an application, giving full particulars of the
security, and was about to start out to interview
several money-lenders on the matter when
Williams returned unexpectedly. I lingered a
few minutes to give him details of the amazing
mess into which Buck had got his affairs, when
Williams was suddenly smitten with an in-
spiration.
“ By the Lord, the new Property and Chattels
Act came into force yesterday, and it strikes me
very forcibly that these blokes haven’t got the
hang of the thing somehow.”
“You mean that they are acting illegally under
the new Act?”
“ That’s it, sonny ; and if he points that out to
them they will have to give him nearly another
week, and during that time we can get the splosh
somewhere—l can see a tenner commission and
procuration fee sticking out.”
“A tenner be hanged—who’s fishing for ten-
ners? If these fellows are acting illegally we
must just string them into doing it, and then
come at ’em for damages. I can see hundreds
sticking out 1 ”
As I said before, Williams was a dishonest man,
and the glare of greed that came into his small,
rat-like eyes when I spoke of hundreds was not
pleasant to see. The idea took him where he
lived, and he clutched the copy of the new Act
that lay on the table and plunged right into a
hard consideration of its intricacies.
“We are right—right as the mail, old man.
We’ve got that blessed company by the wool and
the slack of its waistband. But it seems to me
that in order to establish a good case for damages
we must be in a position to tender the cash the
day of the sale—otherwise they will plead that
no harm or injury was done to Buck, as under no
circumstances could he have paid. They will
plead that in mitigation of damages. See? I
think that’s as clear as mud.”
“ And where are we to get the £5OO when the
blessed security’s sold ? ”
“That’s where we fall in —or, rather, it’s where
those hundreds you were fluting about disap-
pear.”
“Well, we must just tender the money at the
sale—that’s all.”
“ Balderdash ! They’d take it. No ; this is a
case in which honesty is, unfortunately, the
best policy. Just call round and point out the
error, and they will give the extension of tune
required by the new Act, hnd we’ll be £2O richer
by the end of the week, and the profits of the
firm will have been at the rate of £lOOO per
annum, for one week only.”
“ This is my affair,” I said, firmly. “ You just
sit tight, keep your mouth closed—that is till I
ask you for legal advice—and you’ll find the firm’s
profits will be at the rate of £21,000 per annum,
for one week only.”
“You’ll lose the blessed lot,” he growled as 1
went out of the door.
I strolled round and saw John Dorey, whom 1
found, fortunately, in a state of affable drunken-
ness. When John was affably drunk he would
go into any gamble, and when I mentioned
the name of the concern that was about to sell
Buck up, he burst out.
“Give you £500! Certainly, my boy. Id
give you £5OOO (if I had it) to get level with those
people. They took me down for £l5 some 25
years ago, and I’ve never got level with them yet;
but if ye can dish them over this ’twill be prin-
cipal and interest. I know Buck’s property. It
has doubled in value three times in the last 15
years, and this concern is anxious to get it.
They’ll not take the money when you tender it! ”
“ 1 don’t want them to ! ” I said.
“Exactly,” said John Dorey. “We’ll just
drop into the Central, and while you order
whisky I’ll write out a wee bit cheque.”
* * * *
Buck’s property stood on a little plateau,
deeply bowered in trees. A man stood at the
gate ringing a bell, a red flag hung over the car-
nation bed in the front garden, and as the sale
had been well advertised the ground was soon full
of a curious prying crowd. Buck’s own place vvas
a charming little villa, and there was a home-like
look about the pillared portico, the red-tiled roof
and the elegant chimneys from which the blue
smoke curled lazily.
The auctioneer had mounted the rostrum which
he had placed on the front verandah. Williams
and I walked up to him carrying between us a
leather Gladstone bag containing 500 sovereigns.
He was yelling something about a desirable pro-
perty, values going up by leaps and bounds, abso-
lutely no reserve, forced sale, and so forth, when
I thumped loudly on the front of his rostrum with
a walking stick. .
“ Heave to and give us a show, old This
sale’s off—l’ve got the money here for you.”
“ Er—l am afraid I don’t exactly understand,
sir,” said the auctioneer.
“This is a forced sale, and the mortgagor has
asked me to offer the money on his behalf. The
sum is, I understand, £470; and I have that
amount here in gold.”
And so saying, I opened the bag and showed
the gold.
“ Excuse me one moment till I consult my prin-
cipals. This seems somewhat complicated.”
He went into the house and rang up his princi-
pals. It seemed to take considerable time, as it
was nearly a quarter of an‘hour later when he
returned with a stiff-looking upper lip, and
announced that the sale would proceed. “The
company had foreclosed, and was now offering
the property for sale in accordance with the deed
of mortgage.”
“ Which means that the sharks are going to try
and buy it in ! ” I snorted in mock indignation to
hide my delight.
The sale went on ruthlessly. They sold the
land in one lot, and then sold the furniture in the
home. The sacrifices were appalling.
* * * *
When the company got the writ it squirmed.
Its legal advisers had been caught napping, and
the hole that yawned in front of the co. looked
very large and dirty. It offered to refund the
value of the furniture and buy back the property
from the purchaser, which was its own dummy.
The offer was refused.
Then came the trial, and a caustic Judge dealt
out powerful language to the Loan and Mortgage
Co. He told the jurymen to dismiss from their
minds all memory of the unjustifiable abuse
that had been directed at these two ener-
getic young men who had taken up the case.
He thought their action was commendable
in every way. He left the matter in the
hands of the jury with perfect confidence that
they would do their duty to their country, and
also to humanity. The jury performed both
duties nobly, and awarded £lOOO damages to
Buck and £3OO to his wife on some side issue.
Over and above this amount were other sums,
which represented the value of things that had
been destroyed, damaged and lost in the re-
moval. The sale was annulled, so Buck got his
property back. The whole business, including
costs, must have run the co. to every cent of
£3OOO.
When we came to settle up, having collected
under the extensive powers which Buck had given
us in his day of trouble, we found that fighting
cases for damages with borrowed money, even
when you win £l3OO, is more or less a fool’s game.
John Dorey reached out for his loan, his interest
and his profit. Then the Mortgage Co. had care-
fully deducted the amount of the mortgage from
the cheque. After that there were costs between
client and solicitor to be paid up, and what be-
tween costs, John Dorey and the original mort-
gage that money just melted away. At last, when
they had all gone, Williams and I stood looking at
a little heap of gold which panned out at £152.
This we carefully divided between us, and then
cordially invited one another to dinner and supper
respectively.
At dinner Williams looked up and inquired :
“ Good Lord—what about Buck ? ”
“Buck be hanged. He’s got his share—a fine,
valuable oroperty, clear of all encumbrances, and
REVENGE—R EVEN G E, my boy ; which
is sweeter than this Tokay.”
“ Well, I hope you can satisfy him, or convince
him that he is satisfied. I smell trouble, and
before it is all over I believe it will be just the
same as if we’d taken the com. and procuration
fee.”
“ Rubbish ! Anyhow', we’ve got it, and there’ll
be some awful fighting before they get it away
from us.” .
But Williams only dipped his face into the glass
and drank silently.
• » * *
Williams didn’t come down to the office next
morning, but a small boy arrived with a brief note
to the effect that he was ill—had the ’flu’. I sold
a couple of bicycles, and was busy getting parti-
culars of some property on which a man with
curly whiskers wanted to borrow £lOOO when
Buck arrived. I requested him to wait outside,
as I was engaged. But instead of going out he
stuck his hands into his pockets, leered and
remarked, coarsely, that as he couldn’t wait on
both stairs at once he’d stay where he was. He
then begged the client not to mind him. The
client’s curly whiskers didn’t shine quite so
brightly, and he seemed to suddenly freeze.
“When a man’s got thirteen hundred quid
(Continued on next page.)

Three years later, Mrs. Davis says:—“Yes, I
