Sydney.
belonging to you, you’ve got to keep your eye on
him, haven’t you, boss?” he said to the man who
wanted the £1000.”
“ I guess so,” said the client, coldly.
“ Buck, you’ve been drinking again. Here I
save your home, get you out of your difficulties,
win you hundreds of pounds of damages, and you
requite me bv coming here and disturbing my
clients. Oh Buck, how could you?”
“Never mind that guyver. I want a settle-
ment —where’s the money ? ”
“Well, I’m engaged with this gentleman now,
and you can just wait for a .statement of accounts.
There’s a chair on the landing, and, if you don’t
go out quietly, I’ll throw you.”
“I’m afraid I’m in the way,” said the curly-
whiskered stranger, rising hastily. “ After all,
perhaps I’d better not borrow this money. ‘ Out
of debt, out of danger,’ sez the missus to me when
I gets into the dog-cart to drive to the station ; so
I'll just go back and plod away, buildin’ them
tanks as I can out of income, so to speak. Good-
day to you both.”
I heard him plod down the stairs, and a drunken
grin spread over the face of Buck. It roused me
to fury. Here was money snatched out of my
very fingers. I wanted to hit this ungrateful
wretch. But I didn’t. I just sat down and showed
him where the money had gone. I thought that
would hurt him more.
“And where do I come in? Where’s my
whack?” he bellowed, springing suddenly to his
feet and glaring at me across the table with his
top hat jammed firmly down on- the back of his
head.
“Strikes me you come in better than anybody.
Your property is clear, and you can make a fresh
start. It has increased in value to such an extent
that you can now subdivide, build cottages
through a building society, and live on the rents
thereof for the rest of your days.”
But he foamed at the mouth.
“Thirteen hundred quid—and not a shilling
for me. Oh, oh, oh, you robbers ! O God, tell
me if- these swine aren’t worse than the blanky
Mortgage Co. ?” And he lifted his arms drama-
tically to the ceiling.
Shortly afterwards Buck retired to a surgery to
get his wrist reset and his eye repaired, while
I ran for the hotel where I lived and bathed my
nose in the hottest water I could stand.
While I was bathing my nose the Official
Assignee came round—must see me at once.
While I went on bathing my nose he explained
that several people had called on him for full
receipts for money they had paid us in the intri-
cate process of Buck’s affairs—we were only
allowed to issue interim acknowledgments—and
he produced Williams’s signature for sums that
had not been accounted for in our statement of
collections. This was serious. I dried my nose,
and took the statement he handed me. Then we
went downstairs to a cab and drove to the office
where we turned up the books. There was no
denying Williams’s signature, and there was no
getting away from the fact that the money had not
been accounted for. I offered to send for
Williams at once, but the Assignee thought it had
better be settled up, right away, and then he
would not be under the necessity of reporting the
firm. He could go back and say that the money
was available when he asked for it, and would, no
doubt, have been accounted for in the next state-
ment.”
So I drew out what was left of my share of the
loot and paid over £3B. While the Assignee made
out a receipt he heard some blood-curdling threats
uttered against Williams.
I went down stairs with him, carrying the cash
book under my arm. At the street I hailed a
cab and drove to Williams’s address. The maid
told me he had left the day after the trial, ex-
plaining that he had made so much money that
lie would have to go and live in Tinakori-road.
Then I realised that Williams had bolted. I
drove back to the office, where I found, sitting in
the little ante-room, three unusually villainous-
looking young men. I knew they were law-
clerks instinctively. Outside on the landing a
little man in a shabby overcoat, out of the high
collar of which protruded a tuft of grey whisker,
was stumping up and down. I recognised him
also ; he was a bailiff.
“Well, what do you want ?” I inquired -in my
hardest tone.
“ Mr. Williams ! ”
“ I’ll give you £5 if you can let me know his
address within the next 48 hours,” I remarked,
fiercely.
“Then he’s mizzled, has he?” said the bailiff.
“He has,” I remarked, and the bailiff headed
for the staircase.
“ How many more of you are after Mr. Wil-
liams?” I inquired, gloomily.
They shook their heads.
“ We want you,” they said.
“Ah, bicycles, I suppose. Come this way;
one at a time, please. First come, first served.”
A tall, blue-eyed young man followed me in.
He closed the door impressively.
“ Awfully sorry to have to treat you with such
scant courtesy,” he explained, “ but instructions
are emphatic. This is a writ. The Mortgage Co.
wants damages for conspiracy.”
“But I thought conspiracy was a criminal
matter ? ”
“ The Crown may think so later on. All the
Mortgage Co. wants is its money back, and it
alleges that you, Williams, Buck, John Dorey and
a solicitor named Brown conspired together to
take advantage of certain actions to strike it for
damages. Williams has got out early; Brown is
offering to give evidence for the company if the
case against him is withdrawn ; and Buck, who
swears you have his £l3OO, is also offering to do
anything he can. Needless to say, he is very
drunk.”
I took the writ and bowed him out.
The next was a fair-haired youth with a bud-
ding moustache. His eyes lit up when they fell
on the bicycles ranged along the wall.
“Well, what’s your trouble ? ” I inquired, for I
felt, somehow, that he had not come to buy a
bicycle.
“ Writ for £lOOO, issued by us on behalf of Mr.
Buck. Mr. Buck was insistent that you should
be served here to-day.”
“Very well. Do you want to buy a bicycle? ”
“Well, I suppose you’ll be clearing out the
stockcheap? I’ll have a yarn with you about
that big roadster Humber any time you like to
cut the price a bit.”
“ I’m not cutting prices.”
As he went out, the third young man came in.
“ Well, after all these other people worrying
me, perhaps you want to buy a bike?” 1 re-
marked, cheerfully.
“No; Mrs. Buck and daughters want £3OO,
and I’ve come round to demand a settlement.”
“ Well, I haven’t got £3OO. They must know
they can’t fight cases for nothing. There were
costs to pay.”
“ They never authorised you to pay costs —and
we just regard you as a person who stepped in,
got hold of the money somehow, and disbursed it
without any legal authority to do so. Of course
these are my instructions, sir. I’m not making
any charges personally.”
And he smiled pleasantly.
“ And do you really expect a settlement ?
“ Of course not—so much so that the writ is in
my pocket. Who is your solicitor? ’
“I hardly know—Williams has bolted, and
Brown has thrown me over.”
“Looks blue, doesn’t it? Well, name anyone,
and I’ll drop round and serve this thing.”
“Well, I’ll take it myself.”
He passed it over pleasantly, and then fell to
looking at the bikes.
“ How much for the big Humber?” he inquired,
suddenly.
“ £26, cash down.”
He mounted the machine, and rode round the
room.
“ It’s a beauty !” he remarked, ecstatically.
“ Well, what do you think about it ?”
“It’s mine—l’ll send you round a cheque.”
“I’ll be here for the next two hours,” I said, as
he went out; “ after that I’ll be out for the rest
of the day.”
* * * *
I walked across the landing to where the Emi-
nent Lawyer sat among pyramids of papers.
He was a most untidy man, also a very busy one.
I had done a little business with him. He re-
ceived me kindly.
“ I hear you’ve struck trouble,” he said.
“Yes, I want your advice.”
“Very well.” He leaned back in his chair,
placed his hands behind his head, listened to my
story, and glanced through the writs, which were
very innocent looking slips of pale blue paper.
Then he stared at the ceiling.
“ How much money have you got ? ”
“ About £40.”
“Well, you’re a young man, and apparently an
energetic one. I never knew a man of your age
in so many holes simultaneously. You might win
all along the line, but it isn’t worth it. Under-
stand ?”
“ No. I don’t,” I said, in a dazed manner.
The Eminent Lawyer arose and walked to the
window.
“ Look there,” he said, pointing over a wilder-
ness of roofs towards the harbor. “Do you see
that funnel?”
“ Which—the Mararoa’s?”
“Yes. Well, she goes to Sydney. You’re a
young man, you have about £4O, and if you win
these cases you’ll be hundreds of pounds in debt.
Some of the most brilliant exploits in history have
been masterly retreats effected at the right time
in the face of great obstacles. If I were you I’d
retire in good order on Sydney.”
“ Scoot! ”
“ I believe that is the vulgar expression. I
admit the advice is somewhat unconventional, but
there is excellent precedent-nnost excellent.”
I thanked him and withdrew.
* * * *
Next day when mountainous seas hurled the
Mararoa sky-high, and then fell suddenly from
under her, causing her to dive down nose or stern
first with amazing irregularity, a miserable,
dejected specimen of humanity crept out of the
coal bunkers and asked the firemen to kindly drop
him overboard and end his misery.
But they didn’t. They washed the coal dust
off him, and after he was made presentable,
hauled him forward to interview the captain, to
whom he explained that he was doing it for a
bet, and, producing money, offered the price of
his passage. All he asked was to be allowed to
keep out of sight, and also out of mention, at
Auckland, so as not to lose his bet.
The captain smiled. Then he rubbed his
hands and chuckled.
“I don’t know what they want you for, but I
expect it is something to do with money,” he
said. “ Anyhow, a man who can spin one like
that deserves to get away. I’ll send you down to
the purser.”
It was Williams. He, too, was retiring in good
order on Sydney : and, like a genius, he was earn-
ing a salary during his retreat instead of paying
his passage.
HERMES.
Evanescence.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
latter children of the race
Who pore upon decaying shells,
Which speak of Egypt, Home and Thrace?
Or con the Sphinx, with stony face,,
Whose eyes are like unfathomed wells ;
The latter people say, “How deep
Humanity has gripped the past !
Our forebears sowed that we might reap
Above the soil wherein they sleep ;
How deep we reach, how long we last! ”
And yet those lives were but an hour;
The dynasties were but a day
With varying zones of art and power
That carved the stone, or built the tower ;
Then left the shells, and hied away.
These shells the antiquarians prize,
And sage, and archaeologist,
Whereof historians grow wise,
Remain when that which made them flies
Where all must go, and none may list.
The palace and the straw-roofed shed,
The picture scratched upon a bone,
The paintings of the gifted dead.
And Seti’s tomb and Nero’s bed,
The potter’s urn, the reaper’s hone—
All these, the relics which we scan,
Pertaining to that mighty past—
The dim receding wake of man—
Prove but a little space —a span
Betwixt the first thing and the last.
Sum the organic world thereby
From eozoons up to kings,
Through things that crawl to things that fly,
From worms to ships that sail the sky—
A span takes in the sum of things !
But ever the attendant moon,
The earth itself, the fervent sun,
The stars across the heavens strewn,
Swing to their’twilight or their noon,
And slow, unending courses run.
These have their long, wide way to tread
Cut in the void that has no shore ;
Before and after us whose bed
Shall be of earth whence we were bred,
And man shall know his home no more.
VICTORIA.
O. G. A. COLLES.
